HEADLINE: Guidepost;
Adjustable Is Attractive

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO

 BODY:

When adjustable-rate mortgages were introduced in the early 1980's, they were promoted as a lower-cost alternative to fixed-rate mortgages. Now, even with fixed-rate mortgages at their lowest rates in years -- an average of 9.41 percent -- adjustable mortgages, with initial rates averaging about 7.43 percent, are still cheaper. The rate on a variable-interest mortgage rises and falls with a particular financial index. Each lender selects its index based on an analysis of what best protects its own loan portfolio. Almost half of all adjustable-rate mortgages are tied to Treasury securities that are traded daily and mature in one year. In shopping for a mortgage, be aware that other choices are available, like two-step mortgages, which let the lender reset the rate once. Here are the more commonly used indexes, which borrowers may use as a guide in estimating how their present adjustable mortgages may change, or in seeking the best deal:

HEADLINE: Manboxinzhai Journal;
The 'Barefoot Doctors': Shod, but Still Footsore

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MANBOXINZHAI, China

 BODY:
Dr. Su Ke faces little risk of malpractice suits, but there are other hazards of working as a doctor in a remote Chinese village like this one.
"When we go out and try to inoculate babies, some of the peasants are very frightened and hide their kids," Dr. Su, a 46-year-old man with a graying crew cut, explained in a tone of sorrow. "Or they turn their dogs on us to bite us and drive us away." 
 Even if Dr. Su manages to get his needle into the derriere of a local child -- a task made easier since none of the village children wear clothes -- then he is as likely to win censure as gratitude.
"We give them injections against measles, and then the kid gets a cold and they come and complain," he said. "They say, 'You promised that my child wouldn't get sick!' "
Dr. Su and hundreds of thousands of people like him are the inheritors of the legacy of the "barefoot doctors" who charmed the West in Maoist days. These days they wear sandals, and they are far better trained than in the past, but fundamentally the task is the same: to transport medicine from the textbooks and laboratories to the villages, where three-quarters of Chinese live.
 
Chicken Pox, Chest Pains
 Here in Manboxinzhai, a village of 400 in the Xishuangbanna district of southwest China, Dr. Su worked out of a small medicine box one day recently to minister to a variety of patients. There was a little boy with chicken pox, a young man with a swollen leg and an older man with chest pain. On the way to this village, he has stopped off to pull a young woman's tooth.
Dr. Su runs a small clinic 20 miles away, but he and the other doctors periodically hike off into the distance to treat peasants in the villages. Sometimes these "house calls" can last weeks and take a doctor from village to village.
China has made enormous strides in medical care in the four decades since the Communist Revolution, and in some ways the health profile of the population resembles that of a developed country more than that of a country with a per-capita income of $300 a year. The World Bank lists life expectancy among Chinese at 70 years, compared with 76 in the United States and 58 in India. But there are some indications that the gains in health care have slowed in the last dozen years, at the same time that China has opened to the world and doubled its per-capita income.
 
'Barefoot Doctors'
 By some counts, for example, infant mortality has shown little improvement in recent years. At last count, 31 Chinese infants out of 1,000 die before the age of 1, compared with 10 in the United States and 97 in India, according to World Bank statistics.
The main reason for the slowdown in health-care improvements in recent years appears to be the collapse of the commune system, which used to provide rudimentary health care, usually free of charge, throughout rural China. After the communes were dismantled in the early 1980's, some "barefoot doctors" returned to their farm work or started businesses, and many villages were left without adequate health care, or else had to pay more for it. It also became more difficult to mobilize people for large preventive medicine projects.
"Everybody participated in inoculation programs or hygiene projects in those days, because if you didn't then they'd be docked work points," Dr. Su said. "But these days it's hard to do public projects, like mosquito extermination to reduce malaria."
The problems of rural health care can be seen in the life of Mi De, a 29-year-old woman who lives in this village. She sat barefoot on a bench weaving cloth for clothes as she described in a matter-of-fact tone how her first child died soon after birth seven years ago. Her 5-year-old son has not had a medical check-up since he was 13 days old, but as the boy looked on with a solemn face she explained that this was not a problem.
"He's been very healthy, so he doesn't need to see a doctor," Ms. Mi said, adding that he had been inoculated against several diseases. In another concession to modernity, a midwife was present when Ms. Mi gave birth to her third child, a son, in December. In the past, only her mother was present when she gave birth. But throughout her pregnancy last year, Ms. Mi never saw a doctor.
As he makes his rounds, Dr. Su tries to combat traditional attitudes as much as diseases themselves. He scolded two 18-year-old women who are cradling their newborn babies, complaining that they are too young to be raising families. And he reproached the village's health officer, Sha Piao, for failing to implement a new policy for prenatal checkups.
"You're too busy getting ready to build your new house to look after the pregnant women," Dr. Su said sternly. Mr. Sha smiled wanly and explained that he would get to it in the next couple of days. "What with building the new house, I'll be too busy to do the health work this year," Mr. Sha said.
 
Family Planning
 The new program that Mr. Sha promised to begin calls for registering all pregnant women and charging them an obligatory fee of $2. They then are to receive three prenatal checkups. Gynecologists will go from village to village to perform some checkups, and in other areas many women may have to go to the clinic for their checkups.
When it is time for the birth, the woman is encouraged to use a midwife with Government training. If she does, the Government will pay the midwife's 40-cent fee and return $1.60 to the mother as a bonus. But if she does not use the approved midwife, then her $2 fee is forfeited. In some areas, this will also help the Government track pregnancies for its one-child family planning policy, but here in the ethnic minority areas of southwest China the family planning programs are implemented very loosely.
One difficulty that doctors have in this part of China is that urinary tract and gynecological problems are common, but are commonly attributed in the local folklore to promiscuity. So women are sometimes humiliated and furious when they are told they have such problems.
"So when we make our diagnosis," said the long-suffering Dr. Su, "they are outraged and do nothing but yell at us."

HEADLINE: Farmers Get Price Rise

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
Prices that farmers receive for raw products rose 2.1 percent in March from February, the first monthly increase since May 1990, the Agriculture Department said today.
Despite the gain, the department's "prices received" index for farm commodities was 1.3 percent lower than a year earlier. 
 Higher prices for eggs, oranges, cattle and upland cotton were mostly responsible for the March increase, the department said. But lower prices for milk, sweet corn, celery and strawberries partly offset the gains. Prices for cattle and calves were at record levels for the second consecutive month.
"Higher citrus prices reflected shorter supplies caused by the December freeze in California and also higher-quality Valencia oranges moving to market," the report said. "Egg prices reflected a strong seasonal demand."
The cotton price was the highest since January 1981, while corn, oats and sorghum prices increased slightly during the month. Crop prices collectively rose 3.3 percent from February but were 2.3 percent below March of last year.
Most prices are mid-March averages that will be revised next month.
Department economists forecast that 1991 consumer food prices will climb an average of 2 percent to 5 percent, compared with a 5.8 percent gain in 1990.

HEADLINE: Can 'Image' Cars Help Detroit?

BYLINE: By PAUL C. JUDGE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, March 29

 BODY:
Can one sexy car model make a whole company look good?
Detroit has gambled for decades that the answer is yes and sometimes it has been right. The GTO revved up Pontiac's stodgy image in the 60's, much the way the Shelby Mustang put some pizazz behind Ford. There have not been many successes since then. In the mid-80's, the Pontiac Fiero, a two-seat sports car with a glass fiber body caught the eye of showroom shoppers until its engine developed a tendency to catch fire. 
 The most recent flop was the Buick Reatta, the two-seater luxury roadster that General Motors canceled this month after four years of dismal sales. The Reatta's track record was not all that different from a string of high-concept, high-priced cars by Cadillac and Chrysler that have not fared well in recent years either.
While car makers aim to sell at least a few thousand of these "image cars," as the industry calls them, their real hope is that the cars will attract other buyers to more prosaic models in the company's lineup. The theory is that customers will be attracted to showrooms by a Cadillac Allante but drive away in a less expensive model, like the Seville.
That approach may have worked with the GTO and souped-up Mustangs a generation ago, but marketing experts say today's car buyers are more sophisticated. These shoppers are more prone to choose a model based on its attributes and are not as easily lured to showrooms merely to ooh and ahh over the fancy sheet metal on a roadster, they say.
The idea that image cars pull more people into showrooms and lift overall sales "has largely proven to be nonsense," said John Schnapp, who heads the motor vehicle industry group at Temple, Barker & Sloane Inc., a consulting firm in Lexington, Mass. "There's no evidence going back that any image car really transformed a nameplate."
With record losses and the worst auto sales slump since the last recession, car companies can ill afford to keep slow-selling models like the Reatta in their stables.
"In the middle of a recession, in the middle of declining market share due to lack of quality and reliability, the spill-down image from a hot car won't solve those problems," said Thomas C. Kinnear, a professor of marketing at the University of Michigan business school.
 
 Quick Start for the NSX
 Foreign auto makers have had some success in adding cachet to their entire product lines with extravagant, high-priced, low-volume cars. The Honda Motor Company has its attention-grabbing Acura NSX sports car and Nissan has its 300 series sports cars. BMW and Mercedes-Benz both offer powerful top-of-the-line models that are engineering showcases but which few people can afford.
But image problems beget image cars, and managers and engineers often justify building an expensive image car by offering it as an advertising campaign on wheels. Among the Big Three domestic auto makers, bringing out a hot car remains a time-honored tactic for adding panache to a warmed-over product line or for trying to change the way buyers think about a brand.
At Buick, "We introduced the Reatta as a 'halo' car to attract customers who may not have considered Buick before," said Buick's general manager, Edward H. Mertz, when he announced last week that G.M. would stop building the cars in May.
But analysts say that the Reatta halo never lighted up the rest of Buick's line. "Reatta reflects an era when Buick did not know what it was," said Maryann N. Keller, an automotive analyst with Furman Selz Inc. in New York. "If Buick is going back to its heritage of selling larger, more conventional cars, there's no place for Reatta."
General Motors continues to produce the Cadillac Allante, a $57,200 two-seater with an Italian-made body that has floundered in the luxury marketplace. G.M. has sold only about 3,000 Allantes a year since the car was introduced in 1987.
The Chrysler Corporation re-entered the image car game in 1989 with its TC, a $37,000 convertible designed and built by Maserati in Italy. The idea behind TC was to spread a richer, more luxurious patina over a basically pedestrian fleet, though the TC bears a striking resemblance to Chrysler's less expensive Le Baron convertible. But the No. 3 domestic auto maker declared victory and then retreated when it canceled the program in December after its dealers sold fewer than 7,200 TC's.
Despite its experience with the TC, Chrysler is betting heavily that image cars remain a useful way to draw buyers. The company has invested hundreds of millions of its scarce dollars to develop two new image cars: the Dodge Viper and Spirit R/T models. Chrysler is also spending heavily to market a third image car, the Dodge Stealth, which is built by the Mitsubishi Motors Corporation.
Unlike its domestic competitors, the Ford Motor Company has steered clear of image cars in recent years. Paradoxically, the company garnered one of the industry's biggest image coups in the 1980's with the Taurus, a mid-sized family sedan with styling that was ahead of its time.
 
Another Japanese Success

By and large, foreign car companies have learned how to make their image cars pay for themselves. Japanese manufacturers, in particular, developed ways to make several models on the same assembly line, keeping their investment in new tooling to a minimum.
Consider Honda's NSX. The low-slung two-seater has garnered a great deal of praise from auto enthusiast magazines. Its list price is $61,000, but buyers eager for prestige often pay thousands more. The Japanese plant that makes the car's engine also makes four other Honda engines.
"It won't take long for Honda to make money off the NSX," predicted Ron Harbour, an automotive manufacturing consultant in Troy, Mich.
But for American car makers, financial constraints "made it tougher for product guys to sell image car programs" to top management, said Ralph Sarotte, marketing manager for Dodge. The value to Chrysler of a car like the Viper, which is expected to go on sale next year at about $50,000, can be viewed as equal to the cost of a couple of major advertising campaigns, Mr. Sarotte said.
"If the Viper sells in low volumes but becomes the center of a new Dodge image, it takes us a long way as far as how much we have to spend on advertising to establish Dodge's performance credentials," Mr. Sarotte said.
G.M. executives argue that the Reatta did add luster to the Buick line and drew buyers to other models. They cite Buick's increased sales and market share during the last four years. General Motors executives say they canceled the Reatta only reluctantly, not because the car failed to meet expectations, but because the market for two-seat luxury cars collapsed.
Automotive analysts estimate that General Motors spent about $500 million on the engineering, design and tooling for the Reatta, and earned back a fraction of its investment through the sale of about 20,000 cars.
Some industry analysts argue that the Reatta was poorly conceived from the start. Priced at $29,300, the roadster was supposed to draw affluent, middle-aged customers out of imported sports sedans and into Buick dealerships. But Buick planners wanted enough of the familiar comforts of an expensive American sedan to make traditional Buick customers feel at home.
The result was a tight, sensuous exterior paired with a boxy interior that was lifted from the Buick Riviera to save money. The car was loaded with features, with the only options being a 16-way adjustable driver's seat and a power sunroof. But enthusiasts carped that G.M. engineers had left out a five-speed manual transmission that seemed a natural.
"I still think image cars are a wonderful idea," said David E. Davis, editor of Automobile magazine. "But it's important that the car have an image. The Reatta had no personality whatsoever."

HEADLINE: Treasury Securities a Big Hit With Americans Last Year

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN

 BODY:
Individual American investors bought a record $120.5 billion worth of Treasury securities last year, according to data from the Federal Reserve Board. The purchases by individuals came to nearly 45 percent of the more than $272 billion in securities issued in 1990 to finance the budget deficit.
"That is an eye-opening number," said William M. Brachfeld, executive vice president at Daiwa Securities America. "But in the aggregate, individuals are more intelligent making investment decisions than the professionals who manage money. And there were lots of good reasons to be in high-quality securities last year." 
 The Fed's numbers showed that household purchases of Treasury securities were biggest in the first and fourth quarters of the year as depositors appeared to shift money out of savings accounts and other bank instruments at times of marked concern about the stability of the financial system.
Not coincidentally, fears about the savings and loan industry heightened during the first quarter. And in the fourth quarter, concern about the health of some big banks added to the nervousness of investors after Saddam Hussein invaded Kuwait.
"The first and fourth quarters were periods when the backdrop was unsettled, and concerns about the financial system were at their height," said Neal M. Soss, chief economist at the First Boston Corporation.
For a year at least, the Fed's numbers put to rest the notion that strong demand from foreigners was required to sell Treasury securities.
Attracted by higher interest rates elsewhere, foreign purchases of Treasury issues were essentially flat last year, compared with the levels for 1989, at around $30 billion. That level is down sharply from 1988, when foreigners bought $62 billion.
The Fed's numbers for household purchases are estimates, and include purchases made by personal trust accounts and nonprofit organizations. They do not include purchases of Treasury securities made by mutual funds or money market funds, often owned by individuals.
Because the increase in household purchases rose so much -- to $120 billion, from $48.5 billion in 1989 -- some private analysts assumed that the Fed had piled securities into that category because it had no idea where else to put them. Fed officials, who asked not to be identified, conceded that was the case.
But the officials said that since they collected hard data on purchases by other major groups -- banks, savings and loan associations, mutual funds and money market funds -- and knew how many securities were issued, it became a simple matter of subtraction.
"If market interest rates appear high compared to bank rates," Mr. Soss of First Boston said, "you will take money out of the bank and put it into bills or notes."

HEADLINE: U.S. Shifts Complaints On Trade

BYLINE: By CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
The Bush Administration has found fewer trade barriers in Japan than it did last year, but more in the European Community, Canada, Mexico, China, South Korea, India, Australia and Thailand, according to a special trade report published today.
But the Administration still listed more trade complaints against Japan than against any other nation. 
 Carla A. Hills, the United States trade representative, said the sixth annual inventory of trade barriers "underscores the tremendous amount of work that still needs to be done" in trying to open markets.
As coordinator of Administration trade policy and head of the trade office that negotiates with foreign governments, she was in charge of preparing this year's catalogue, called the National Trade Estimate Report on Foreign Trade Barriers.
 
 Soaring Deficit With China
 Particularly sharp attention was drawn to China, where the United States trade deficit almost doubled last year, to $10.4 billion. Trade officials said they believed the deficit with China would continue rising this year, to become second highest after that with Japan.
Last year, the deficits of $41 billion with Japan and $11 billion with Taiwan were the main components of the overall $101 billion trade gap between the United States and the rest of the world.
The principal complaints against China involved its system of import bans, covering 80 types of products, mostly consumer goods, as well as its licensing regulations and patent, copyright and trademark piracy. The Government says these measures cost American business billions of dollars a year.
 
 Effect of Accords With Japan

The reason for fewer citations against Japan was the conclusion in the last year of market-opening agreements covering supercomputers, satellites, wood products, sound recordings and metal alloys for electrical distribution transformers.
The annual report is required by Congress as a way for the executive branch to shed light on trade policy directions over the coming 12 months.
For the first time since the series began in 1986, there was a discussion of trading and economic conditions in the Soviet Union and Eastern Europe -- a sign of the recent varied and halting efforts of these countries to join the world trading system.
Of the Soviet Union, the report summed up the bleak conviction of many in the Administration that "comprehensive reform had come to an end" and that the Government was still "attempting to achieve market performance through administrative solutions."
 
 'Not an Exercise in Name Calling'
 Although the report lists specific barriers and attempts to assess their impact on American exporters, Joshua Bolton, general counsel of the trade office, said it was "not an exercise in name calling" but rather a "resource for us to use in assessing where we are and where we ought to be going with each of our trading partners."
The report was longer this year than last year -- 257 pages against 216. The first report in 1986 was 300 pages.
Asked whether the size of the report this year should be read as a sign of failure of Government programs to open markets, Mr. Bolton said there had been "notable successes," citing resolution of some disputes with Japan and with South Korea. Washington concluded market access agreements in beef, wine and telecommunications with Seoul last year.
But Mr. Bolton warned that the trade reports would never get much slimmer until the 108 member nations of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade reached an accord in their talks on new rules.
Those talks, which began five years ago, broke off in December amid a dispute over farm subsidies that pitted the European Community and Japan on one side against the United States, Canada, Argentina and other food exporters on the other.
The talks have resumed, but for them to be concluded Congress has to approve an Administration request for an extension of negotiating authority. Congress is expected to vote sometime before the present authority expires on May 31.
 
 Mexico's Restrictions
 Mexico, with which the United States is seeking a free-trade arrangement, was cited for a variety of restrictions, including high tariffs and import licensing rules. Mr. Bolton said many of these problems could be resolved under a free-trade pact.
One country was added to the list this year, Singapore, where the United States trade deficit in 1990 was $1.8 billion, or $223 million higher than in 1989.
Although Singapore imposes few tariffs, it has major restrictions on imports of cigarettes, and its state-owned telecommunications authority severely limits access by American companies.
The trade office is expected to follow up this largely informative report with another on April 26 naming major violators of intellectual property rights. That report could lead to some specific trade action -- the citing of countries for "priority" negotiations under a 1988 trade law that could lead to trade retaliation.
The International Intellectual Property Alliance, which represents the film, recording, publishing, music and computer software industries, said it wanted four nations cited: China, India, Thailand and Indonesia.

HEADLINE: New-Home Sales Show Biggest Gain in 5 Years

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
Sales of new homes surged 16.2 percent in February, the biggest advance in nearly five years, the Commerce Department reported today. Analysts attributed the upswing to lower mortgage rates and the end of the war in the Persian Gulf.
The department said the February gain, the first since November, was paced by a huge advance in sales in the Midwest, although all regions enjoyed increases from an extremely depressed January level. 
 The February increase still left sales at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 467,000 units last month, 22.9 percent below the level of a year earlier.
The report was the third piece of evidence that the housing industry is beginning to rebound. Sales of existing homes were up 7.9 percent last month, also the biggest rise in nearly five years. Also, construction of new homes and apartments rose, and applications for new building permits posted the first gain in eight months.
 
 Only One Month's Data

"One month's statistics should not be overemphasized, but the trend toward an upturned market is definitely very real," said John Tuccillo, chief economist of the National Association of Realtors. "People are back in the market due to low interest rates and postwar euphoria."
Fixed-rate mortgages fell to a four-year low of 9.25 percent in February but have since climbed back up a bit as financial markets have begun to express concerns about inflation.
But economists forecast that rates should begin to back off in coming months as inflationary worries subside and the Federal Reserve loosens credit further to end the recession.
David Seiders, chief economist of the National Association of Home Builders, said he looked for fixed-rate mortgages to return to around 9.25 percent, and possibly as low as 9 percent.
The Federal Home Loan Mortgage Corporation reported today that rates were falling in the past week with fixed-rate mortgages edging down to 9.52 percent, from 9.59 percent the previous week.
The rate for one-year adjustable mortgages, which was already at a low, fell even further, to 7.41 percent from 7.44 percent.
 
 Prices Rise, Too
 The Commerce Department report showed that the upswing in new home sales in February was accompanied by a rise in prices to near-record levels.
The median selling price climbed to $127,500. That was up from $120,000 in January and was the highest since a record of $130,000 in April 1990. The median is the point at which as many homes sell for higher prices as for lower. The average price was up as well, rising to $156,800, the highest since a record $158,600 set in September 1989.
Thomas Holloway, senior economist at the Mortgage Bankers Association, said large backlogs of unsold houses would exert downward pressure on prices for some months to come.
The 16.2 percent rise in new single-family home sales followed a 12.6 percent decline in January and was the biggest one-month gain since a 21 percent jump in March 1986.
The Midwest enjoyed the biggest gain of any region, a record 70.3 percent increase, but analysts said it was unlikely to last.
Sales in the South were up 9.1 percent, to an annual rate of 192,000 units, and sales in the Northeast rose 5.9 percent, to a rate of 54,000 units. Sales in the West posted the smallest gain during the month, a 1 percent rise, to a rate of 113,000 units.

GRAPHIC: Graphs: "New-Home Sales," tracks new private homes sold during month, seasonally adjusted annual rate in thousands, Sept. 1989-Feb. 1991 (Commerce Dept.); "Prices of New Homes," tracks median price for new homes in thousands of dollars seasonally adjusted, Sept. 1989-Feb. 1991 (Source: Commerce Dept.)

HEADLINE: ECONOMY INDEX UP, SPURRING OPTIMISM ON RECESSION'S END

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
In a sign that the recession may be approaching an end, the Government said today that its main economic forecasting index climbed 1.1 percent in February, the first advance since June and the biggest in more than two and a half years.
Even though the bulk of the rise in the index of leading indicators was caused by financial and psychological factors, many analysts regarded the Commerce Department report as evidence of an impending upturn in the economy after a half-year or so of contraction. 
 "Things are coming around," said Kenneth T. Mayland, chief economist at the Society National Bank in Cleveland. "The recession's not going to last too much longer."
 
Caveats About a Prediction
 Some experts said, however, that it was still too early to predict a recovery with confidence, since the index has often proved unreliable, even when for three or more months it appeared to be calling a turn in the economy. Moreover, the lag between a correct signal and an economic revival can be quite long, as it was in 1982 when the index rose an average of 1.2 percent in February and April and the recession continued until almost the end of the year.
"The sharp rise in the leading indicators may represent a false positive reading," cautioned Gordon Richards of the National Association of Manufacturers. "It would be premature to forecast an early end to the recession."
The Commerce Department also reported today that sales of new single-family houses rebounded 16 percent in February, further evidence of some quickening in housing activity. [Page 29.] Groundbreaking for new homes and sales of existing homes have been previously reported as moving up last month from depressed levels.
Although soaring stock prices and a surge in consumer expectations dominated the increase in the index of leading indicators, analysts noted that five other components also rose. Only 3 of the 11 elements declined, with vendor performance -- the speed with which companies obtain goods from their suppliers -- unchanged last month.
The index of coincident indicators, which is intended to gauge current economic conditions, fell six-tenths of 1 percent last month, the department also reported. That was the eighth straight decline for the index, but the smallest drop since August.
The advance in the leading index, which was somewhat larger than most analysts had expected, followed a revised decline for January of five-tenths of 1 percent. New factory orders for consumer goods were lower than originally estimated when the department initially reported a January decline of four-tenths of 1 percent.
Rising stock prices, as measured by the Standard & Poor's 500 index, accounted for more than half of the increase in the leading index. Other sizable contributions came from the jump in the University of Michigan's survey of consumer expectations, an advance in building permits and a higher M-2 money supply.
Small positive impetus came from higher factory orders for consumer goods, higher factory backlogs of unfilled durable-goods orders and higher prices for materials that are especially sensitive to business cycles, like copper.
 
 Other Economic Measures
 Holding the index back were a surge in initial claims for state unemployment insurance, a reduction in the length of the workweek and a decline in contracts and orders for plants and equipment.
The 1.1 percent overall increase was the biggest since a 1.6 percent gain in June 1988.
The department's index of lagging indicators, which includes such data as the prime lending rate and the average duration of joblessness, declined two-tenths of 1 percent in February after two months of increases averaging six-tenths of 1 percent.
This helped push down the ratio of the coincident indicators to lagging ones -- an alternate forecasting tool -- for the sixth straight month.
Two privately constructed predictors showed increases similar to that of the Government's leading index. The Macroeconomic index compiled by Jesup, Josephthal & Company was calculated at minus 92 for February, up from minus 112 in January and from minus 118 at the trough in November.
Safian Investment Research, another New York firm, said its index had risen 1.6 percent, with 13 components up, 9 down and one unchanged. With stocks and monetary components removed, the Safian index fell one-tenth of 1 percent.
"You're seeing a definite bottoming in economic activity," said Kenneth Safian, the head of the firm.

GRAPHIC: Graph: "Leading Indicators" shows index of 11 indicators designated to predict overall economic activity. (Source: Commerce Department)(pg. 29)

HEADLINE: Four Bid for Units of Bank of New England

BYLINE: By MICHAEL QUINT

 BODY:
The Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation announced yesterday that three banking companies had offered to buy the three failed banks once owned by the Bank of New England Corporation, while a fourth group had offered to buy just one of the failed banks.
A larger field of potential bidders shrank in recent weeks amid uncertainty over the immediate prospects for the New England economy and the enormous task involved in taking on such a large and troubled institution. 
 The Bank of New England, once the second-largest banking company in New England, today has $20 billion of assets and 320 branches in Massachusetts, Connecticut and Maine. Its collapse is expected to cost the Government $2.3 billion, making it the third-most-costly failure of a banking company, after the failures of the First Republic Bankcorporation and MCorp, both of Dallas. The winner of the bidding will be announced on April 16.
The largest bidders are the BankAmerica Corporation, based in San Francisco, the nation's second-largest banking company, and the two largest banking companies in New England: the Bank of Boston, the largest, and the Fleet/Norstar Financial Group of Providence, R.I. These three companies are expected to bid for all three of the Bank of New England subsidiaries, but the three companies and the F.D.I.C. would not provide details of their offers.
An investor group led by Marco F. DeSalle, a businessman in Portland, Me., and a former banker, offered to buy the failed Maine bank, with $1 billion of assets, the smallest of the three banks offered for sale.
BankAmerica was the first to show interest in the Bank of New England, and was talking with the F.D.I.C. about an acquisition even before the Boston-based company collapsed in early January. Profits of more than $1 billion in the last two years have made BankAmerica strong enough that it has been actively buying savings institutions in California and nearby states.
 
 Bank of Boston's Bid
 For the Bank of Boston, which analysts said had arranged for fresh capital to be supplied by other investors if its bid was accepted, an acquisition of the Bank of New England would be an opportunity to sharply reduce the costs of operating the two companies by closing duplicated branches and computer operations. Without additional capital, the Bank of Boston satisfies the minimum standards set by regulators, but would fall short of the extra-strong capital position regulators would expect of a buyer.
Fleet/Norstar, which has also suffered loan losses since the New England economy stumbled, has combined forces with Kohlberg, Kravis, Roberts & Company, the Wall Street buyout firm.
It is expected that the winning bidder will pay more than the $750 million the Government invested in the three banks when they were seized in early January. To protect themselves from losses on bad loans made by the Bank of New England units, bankers are expected to insist on the right to return bad loans to the F.D.I.C. Bankers who have talked with the F.D.I.C. say the agency is willing to give banks a three-year period during which bad loans might be returned.
 
Terms of Deal

In selling the failed banks, which it has been operating since they were seized in early January, the Government will transfer to the buyer the loans and deposits of the failed banks. But because the amount of good quality loans is expected to be less than the deposits, the F.D.I.C. must provide cash or short-term notes to make up the difference. The amount of the cash or notes, adjusted for what the F.D.I.C. makes on bad loans, which are not part of the purchase, determines its loss.
At the end of 1990, the Bank of New England said it had about $3.3 billion of loans more than 90 days past due, or so deeply troubled that it did not expect full payment. The company has more than $2.5 billion of additional loans that have been criticized by examiners and are considered highly risky by bankers.
Since early January, potential bidders have been circling the troubled bank. But in the last few weeks, some of the nation's best-known banks have dropped out of the bidding.
The Banc One Corporation of Columbus, Ohio, which acquired MCorp in 1989, said this week it would not bid because of worries that the New England economy had not bottomed out, and that it expected more attractive acquisition opportunities to come along. The Chase Manhattan Corporation said it would not bid when it became clear that the F.D.I.C. wanted to sell the three banks as a unit, or as single banks, and not split off the southern Connecticut branches that interested Chase. Others that dropped out included the U.S. Trust Corporation of Boston and the Corestates Financial Corporation of Philadelphia.
Bert Ely, a bank analyst in Alexandria, Va., said he was not troubled by the smaller number of bidders but was far more concerned about whether a strong, newly capitalized Bank of New England would be so competitive it might push other regional banks into insolvency.
L. William Seidman, chairman of the F.D.I.C., has said that when the bids are evaluated he will consider the impact on other banks in the area -- and the cost of their failure to the F.D.I.C. insurance fund -- when deciding which bid results in the lowest cost to his agency.
Ira Stepanian, chairman of the Bank of Boston, says that Federal regulators should give preference to bids from banking companies already in the region. Local comapnies will do most, he contends, to increase the availability ot credit to local businesses and help to revive the region's economy.

HEADLINE: Yeltsin Assails Gorbachev Rule For 'Stagnation'

BYLINE: By SERGE SCHMEMANN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MOSCOW, March 29

 BODY:
Flush with the victory of his followers against the Kremlin's strong show of force a day ago, Boris N. Yeltsin assailed the central authorities today for seeking to restore the "period of stagnation" that preceded perestroika.
Mr. Yeltsin, president of the Russian republic, issued the attack in a 90-minute report to a special session of the republic's Parliament. The meeting resumed today after the Kremlin withdrew the cordon of military vehicles and troops that was placed around the city center on Thursday in an attempt to block a demonstration by Yeltsin followers who took to the streets anyway, rallying peacefully in a crowd of more than 100,000. 
 After that setback for President Mikhail S. Gorbachev, Mr. Yeltsin's chief target, the contest today featured intensive parliamentary scuffles between Mr. Yeltsin's Democratic Russia bloc and its Communist-led opposition.
 
Victories for Both Sides
 The voting seesawed between the two sides, with both scoring some tactical victories. Mr. Yeltsin's most significant triumph came in such a vote, which demonstrated that his opponents are not likely to muster enough votes if they try to oust him as president of the republic's Parliament.
Mr. Yeltsin's report was a sustained attack on the Communists and the central Government and a broad reaffirmation of his program of radical economic and political change, based on the "500-day" program for a transition to a market economy that Mr. Gorbachev rejected last fall. In referring to the "period of stagnation," Mr. Yeltsin turned a phrase back on Mr. Gorbachev that the Soviet leader has often used to refer to Leonid I. Brezhnev's nearly two decades in power from 1964 to 1982.
The speech, however, gave no timetable or specific plan for putting the programs in effect, underscoring the fundamental fact of the political contest in Moscow -- that Mr. Yeltsin has considerable popularity but not the levers to achieve his will, while Mr. Gorbachev holds huge formal powers but lacks the mandate to wield them.
Mr. Yeltsin did not directly reiterate his call last month for Mr. Gorbachev's resignation, but the demand was implicit in his insistence that the existing central government had demonstrated itself incapable of any significant reform.
"The objective results of the past six years demonstrate that what we have been witnessing is not perestroika, but rather the last phase of the period of stagnation," he declared.
Mr. Yeltsin won the loudest cheers when he called on all political forces, including the Communists, to gather in "round table" discussions on the future of the Soviet Union, much as the East Germans or the Hungarians did.
 
Posing the Issues
 He declared that two directly opposed policies were contending in the land. One was to "achieve thorough change in every aspect of life, to drastically renew the U.S.S.R. and transform it into a union of sovereign states."
The other, he said, was "to preserve and even enhance the economic monopoly of the center" and thus bring economic catastrophe.
The notion of two competing policies was hardly surprising to the delegates gathered in the Kremlin after the tense confrontations on Thursday and after the entire morning session was taken up by political maneuvering over the agenda.
The skirmishes indicated that the five-day session of the Russian parliament was not likely to achieve much beyond fanning the political feud between the forces arrayed behind Mr. Yeltsin and Mr. Gorbachev. But it did show that the fledgling parliament of the Russian federated republic, the largest and most powerful of the nation's 15 constituent parts, had come to appreciate some parliamentary traditions.
Legislators from both sides shouted and interjected as lustily as any British member of Parliament, and sharp tactical maneuvers were concealed behind arcane procedural motions.
One vote was whether to term Mr. Yeltsin's address a "report," which would indicate a presidential survey of the state of his republic, or an "accounting," which would imply a response to the accusations of the opposition. "Report" won by 612 to 341, demonstrating that a majority of the legislators stood behind Mr. Yeltsin.
At the same time, the parliament showed that Mr. Yeltsin did not have the two-thirds majority necessary to amend the constitution and create the office of a popularly elected president, even though a majority of voters in the Russian republic, which has more than half of the nation's population, approved the idea in the recent referendum. On a procedural vote to discuss the consequences of the referendum, Mr. Yeltsin mustered only 508 legislators, less than the half of the 1,068 total votes required for passage.
 
Move for Special Session
 The special session was initially demanded by Mr. Yeltsin's opponents to censure and possibly impeach him for calling on Mr. Gorbachev to resign. Mr. Yeltsin's followers then called a demonstration for the start of the session, upon which Mr. Gorbachev decreed a ban on all mass gatherings in Moscow for 20 days.
Reporting on the handling of the rallies to the nation's Parliament, Interior Minister Boris K. Pugo insisted that all 50,000 troops used in the operation were "permanently deployed in Moscow," and that "neither the Interior Ministry nor the Soviet Army sent additional troops to Moscow." He also denied that military equipment was used to block any streets or squares.
The statements contradicted what large numbers of Muscovites saw for themselves, that troops were brought in from outside the capital, and that large military trucks parked bumper-to-bumper were used to block streets.
The pro-Gorbachev majority at the Soviet Union's Parliament, which is meeting concurrently with the Russian Parliament, adopted a resolution that "gave a positive assessment of law-enforcement bodies' activities during rallies on Thursday," Tass reported.

HEADLINE: Corporate Underwriting Set Record in Quarter

BYLINE: By KURT EICHENWALD

 BODY:
Despite the recession, American corporations sought huge amounts of cash from the public markets in the first quarter of 1991, with the volume bringing slightly increased fees to Wall Street.
For the quarter, corporations issued a record $104.5 billion in domestic securities, up 30.7 percent from the $79.97 billion raised in the first quarter of 1990, according to the Securities Data Company, a New York research firm. 
 The determining factor in the push to raise capital was the decline of interest rates during the quarter, underwriters said. Many companies had been holding out for lower rates for as long as a year, and, when the drop came, they plunged into the markets.
"After the war began in mid-January without the world falling apart, and then when we got attractive absolute rate levels, there was a huge flow of deals," said Grant Kvalheim, a managing director on the capital markets desk at Merrill Lynch & Company.
Despite the success in the Persian Gulf, lower interest rates and a healthy stream of deals, fees for underwriters did not pick up substantially, although they rose enough to help the troubled financial services industry.
Total fees for the quarter were $625.9 million, a small 5 percent increase from the period last year.
Merrill Lynch was the biggest underwriter for the quarter. In 1988, Merrill moved past Salomon Brothers, which had long held the top spot in underwriting, and has been building a steady lead in the business ever since.
For the quarter, Merrill underwrote $21.59 billion in domestic securities, giving it 20.7 percent of the market, according to Securities Data. Goldman, Sachs & Company was second, underwriting $13.53 billion for a 13 percent share. Next came the First Boston Corporation and Morgan Stanley & Company.
 
Initial Offerings Down
 While a large amount of capital was being sought, much of the cash was raised through debt securities, even as the Dow Jones industrial average surged by 10.8 percent since the beginning of the year.
Initial public offerings did particularly poorly, with only $2.7 billion issued, according to Securities Data. That is down 37 percent from the volume in the first quarter of last year, and a drop of 68.9 percent from the peak volume during the fourth quarter of 1986.
The total for all equity issues was also down, with $6.8 billion in underwritings, compared with $7.1 billion a year earlier. But the figures indicate that with the uncertainties caused by the gulf war resolved, equity underwritings could strengthen. More than half of the volume, about $3.4 billion, came in March alone, after the war, according to IDD Information Services, another New York research firm.
One exception to the surge in debt securities was "junk bonds," where the market collapsed early in the year.
Indeed, some underwriters said that, with the threat of a junk bond-financed takeover unlikely, and with interest rates at a favorable level, the appetite for investment-grade debt climbed, fueled by the Federal Reserve's repeated cuts in interest rates.
In the first quarter, $41.8 billion in corporate debt was issued, up 36.1 percent from $26 billion a year ago. According to Securities Data, more than 88 percent of this debt was issued without call provisions, which would allow the issuers to repurchase the bonds at a fixed price, indicating that corporate treasurers are confident in this market.
After booming for many quarters, the markets for mortgage-backed and asset-backed securities showed tentative signs of slowing growth in the first quarter. The mortgage-backed and asset-backed issues made up 49 percent of the total domestic market, compared with 56.5 percent at the end of 1990. New issues reached $51.3 billion, up 12.2 percent from the 1990 period.
The winner in fees was Goldman, Sachs, which received $120.1 million in the first quarter for underwritings. The first-quarter fees of the firm equaled 49 percent of the $244.2 million it received in all of 1990, Securities Data said.

HEADLINE: Bank Insurance in S.E.C. Study

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
A Securities and Exchange Commission study indicates that the Federal Government supplies banking deposit insurance far below what it is worth.
The study, released on Thursday by the S.E.C.'s Office of Economic Analysis, was requested by the commission's chairman, Richard C. Breeden, who was a key figure in the savings and loan bailout strategy two years ago when he was a White House aide. 
 Concerns about the adequacy of existing bank deposit insurance have been heightened by the enormous taxpayer costs of the savings and loan bailout and a sharp decline in the insurance fund's reserves.
The Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation, which guarantees bank deposits, is running short of money because of record bank failures in recent years and will have to borrow Federal money to bolster the fund. The Administration has proposed that the F.D.I.C. be allowed to borrow $25 billion from the Federal Reserve.
The S.E.C. study concluded that the value of deposit insurance was three to five times greater than the premium being collected from banks.
The flat-rate premiums paid by all commercial banks, the study concluded, lead to safer banks cross-subsidizing riskier banks, which "greatly underprices the aggregate risk" assumed by the F.D.I.C. But the study said a substantial premium increase would not necessarily solve the problem.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

The Economy
The Government's main economic forecasting index climbed 1.1 percent in February, signaling that the recession may be nearing an end. It was the first rise in the index of leading economic indicators since June and its biggest in two and a half years. [Page 1.] 

 Sales of new homes surged 16.2 percent in February, the biggest advance in nearly five years, the Commerce Department reported. Analysts attributed the upswing to lower mortgage rates and the end of the war in the Persian Gulf. [29.]
Trade barriers in Japan have declined from last year, the Bush Administration has found, although there are still more trade complaints against Japan than against any other nation. But a special report cited increasing barriers in the European Community, Canada, Mexico, China, South Korea, India, Australia and Thailand. [25.]
The Administration said it would appeal a tariff ruling that requires the U.S. to lift $18 million of punitive duties on imports of Canadian pork. The ruling by a binational dispute-settlement panel in January caused a storm of protest in the Farm Belt. [25.]
 
Companies
Three banking companies bid for failed banks in Massachusetts, Connecticut and Maine that were once owned by the Bank of New England, the F.D.I.C. said. They are BankAmerica, the Bank of Boston and Fleet/Norstar of Providence. An investor group led by a Portland business executive offered to buy the failed Maine bank. The winner is to be announced April 16. [25.]
An offer to buy out Trans World Airlines was agreed to by the carrier's unions, which would offer cost savings of $137 million a year, and the West Coast financier, Kirk Kerkorian, who would put up $250 million. The plan will die if the Transporation Department gives final approval to the sale of T.W.A.'s London routes to American Airlines. Both sides have yet to discuss the tentative plan with Carl C. Icahn, T.W.A.'s chairman. [27.]
Alcoa said it would have to deduct $1 billion from its earnings because of a change in how companies must account for retiree health-care benefits. Alcoa disclosed the amount in its annual report but said it had not decided on the form of the deduction. It can be taken either in a lump sum or over 20 years. [27.]
 
Markets
Individual American investors bought a record $120.5 billion worth of Treasury securities last year, according to the Federal Reserve Board. They shifted money out of savings accounts and other bank instruments at times of marked concern about the stability of the financial system and bought nearly 45 percent of the $272 billion in securities issued by the Treasury in 1990. [25.]
American corporations sought huge amounts of cash from the public markets in the first quarter of 1991, issuing a record $104.5 billion in domestic securities, up 30.7 percent from the first quarter of 1990. The big underwriting volume at a time of dropping interest rates brought slightly increased fees to Wall Street. [26.]
Prices of Treasury securities were essentially unchanged in extremely light dealings. With New York Treasury dealers closed for Good Friday, along with most European financial centers, there were few transactions. Stock and commodity exchanges and corporate and tax-exempt bond markets were all closed. [28.]
The dollar slipped in New York but rose in Tokyo in extremely thin trading. Gold markets were closed for Good Friday. [29.]
 
Today's Columns
Taxpayers can reduce their tax bite for 1990, even at this late date, by making the right filing choices, being alert to recent tax-law changes and contributing to an I.R.A., if eligible. The "kiddie tax," on income from investments held in the name of children under age 14, is a prime example. Your Money. [26.]
Two California entrepreneurs have patented a building alarm that could give people a few seconds to run for cover when an earthquake is about to strike. Patents. [26.]

HEADLINE: Building Plans for Foley Sq. Are Unveiled

BYLINE: By CRAIG WOLFF

 BODY:
Proclaiming that their project will help lift New York's sagging development industry, Federal officials unveiled the final models yesterday for a new Federal courthouse and office building on Foley Square in lower Manhattan.
The project -- a 27-story courthouse and a 34-story office building -- will cover the sites of three small buildings and two parking lots and probably cost $1 billion by the time it is completed in late 1994. The size of the project, nearly 1.9 million square feet, makes it a major civilian undertaking for the Federal Government. 
 "Look at this way," said Senator Daniel P. Moynihan, who has been the leading backer of the project. "It will be two-thirds the size of the Pentagon."
If nothing else, anticipation of the room the huge undertaking will provide should relieve a cramped judiciary, and the dollar signs that go with it promise to invigorate Foley Square, the heart of the city's legal community.
 
City Has Second Thoughts
 At the unveiling, attended by Senator Moynihan and several Federal judges, Mayor David N. Dinkins said that $1.5 million of the $104 million the Federal Government had already paid the city had been committed to the renovation of the square itself. That will probably include the elimination of some roads, creating a plaza in the middle of today's vale of concrete criss-crossed by asphalt.
The city, though, has apparently had second thoughts about occupying half the office building. In the slumping real-estate market, it has found space in other downtown buildings at lower rents, said William J. Diamond, regional administrator of the Federal General Services Administration.
But Mr. Diamond said the space would be filled easily by moving in the Environmental Protection Agency, the Internal Revenue Service, the General Accounting Office and other Federal agencies that now have offices throughout the city.
The Government began planning the project years before the current slump began, Mr. Diamond said, and in any case, it needs office and courtroom space in the area, not in an uptown building, where space might be going begging. Other offices will remain in the existing Jacob K. Javits Federal Office Building, next to the new one, which will be bordered by Broadway and Duane, Reade and Elk Streets.
The new buildings have generated local opposition. Some Chinatown residents say the courthouse, on Chatham Square between Worth and Pearl Streets, will further encroach on their neighborhood, which has nearly no open or park space nearby. Officials of Community Board 1 in Manhattan say they resent the way the project was approved without consultation with them.
In 1989, worried that public hearings could derail their plans, the Federal government and the city agreed to a Federal "friendly condemnation" of the city-owned sites, which allowed officials to bypass the community board, the the City Planning Commission and the Board of Estimate.
 
In a Parking Lot
 "We don't necessarily object to the new buildings," said Paul Goldstein, district manager for Community Board 1. "But we do object to the fact that the public was denied input."
The $241 million contract for the new courthouse, which will stand on what is now a parking lot behind the State Supreme Court at 60 Centre Street, was won by the development company BPT Properties for a design by the architects Kohn Pederson Fox.
The entrance will not look out on Foley Square because it is blocked by the state court, but the building is designed to "bring the square together" by blending with older buildings, said Eugene Kohn, one of the architects.
The courthouse and the new office building will have both classical and modern details. Both will be of "buff-colored granite with decorative banding" and have glass roofs illuminated from inside at night.
Steven Flanders, head administrator for the Second Circuit of the Federal Appeals Court, said the new courthouse will add badly needed space so that files will no longer be stacked in basement corridors and judges will not have to scrounge for courtrooms on busy days, as they do now. It will have 43 new courtrooms, one ceremonial one, and 42 new judges' chambers.
The office building, designed by Hellmuth, Obata & Kassabaum, will be built by The Linpro Company. The 974,000 square feet will have space for 4,200 workers and will include a day care center, a four-story glass-enclosed galleria, a three-story rotunda and 14,000 square feet of retail space.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, left, Mayor David N. Dinkins, and General Services Administrator Richard G. Austin, at unveiling yesterday of models for planned Foley Square Federal courthouse and extension, in front of Mr. Dinkins, and Federal office building, partially hidden at right. (John Sotomayor/The New York Times) (pg. 22)
 
Map: Map depicts part of Manhattan and indicates the planned Federal office building and Federal court house. (pg. 22)

HEADLINE: Chicago Journal;
To Lessen Their Pain, Mexicans Relive Christ's

BYLINE: By ISABEL WILKERSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CHICAGO, March 29

 BODY:
On this bit terly cold day far from the sands of Jerusalem, an unemployed laundry worker donned a white tunic and a crown of thorns and dragged a wooden cross past the cantinas and tortilla factories of Chicago's Mexican neighborhood.
Young mothers held their babies up to tenement windows and restaurant workers climbed on top of fences to see the recreation of Jesus, surrounded by apostles and Roman sentries, walking to his death. 
 For many of Chicago's 300,000 Mexican-Americans, Good Friday is the most solemn day of the year. Despite the blight of drugs and gang violence, they are held together in part by their staunch belief in a prophet who lived as humbly as they do.
While the Passion play is depicted throughout the world at with more pomp and circumstance, often in the original Latin and with music by Bach, this one is a grass-roots version, imported from poor villages in Mexico to Chicago's Pilsen neighborhood in the shadow of the Loop.
The Mexican-Americans here, who make up the city's largest Hispanic group, have carved out a life for themselves amid the shootings and drug dealing of the neighborhood's half-dozen gangs. For the last 15 years, residents have come together for the Via Crucis pageant, or Way of the Cross, as a way of pulling the community together.
"It means remembering your roots, your faith, your traditions and being proud to be a Mexican again," said the Rev. Manuel Myvett Diaz, a pageant organizer. "It's something where nobody puts you down. You're not under the Americans. It's a time to celebrate being Mexican and Catholic."
The pageant is unpretentious and distinctly Mexican. "This is closer to the people," said Alejandro Aquilera, a factory worker who played the apostle Simon in a previous production. "Just look at who Jesus is."
Salvador Moreno, who played the role of Jesus this year, is 35 years old and has been laid off from his job at a laundry. He is a quiet, modest man with no acting credits, but he was chosen by the pageant's organizers because he is a faithful parishioner and because they knew it was unlikely he would be seen staggering out of the corner cantina the next night.
"No one with his five senses would really want to do it," said Father Diaz. "No one is worthy, but we look for someone who is most worthy, humanly speaking."
Even after Mr. Moreno was chosen, many would-be Jesuses came forward and begged to play the part. But the role is a hard one to live up to, Mr. Moreno said. "You live in the community and they watch what you do," he said. "You're really living the part that you're playing."
Indeed, today he hauled a 150-pound cross in a bitter wind for most of the two-mile route to the crucifixion site, his face weary and stained with mock blood. The crush of onlookers, clutching their scarves and coats, swelled with each passing block as the faithful walked by his side singing "Perdona Tu Pueblo, Senor," or "Forgive Your People, Lord."
Jorge Nieto, a 40-year-old factory worker, portrayed Jesus four years ago and remembers feeling unworthy and overwhelmed. "I was trembling up there on the cross," Mr. Nieto said. "I remember it, and it gives me the chills."
This year, after a series of gang killings, residents used the pageant as a means of praying for an end to the violence. They walked past the beauty shops and the bakeries selling canastas and tostadas and past the driving schools named after Mexican towns but defaced by gang grafitti. The procession paused for prayer at a cantina where some gang members were killed recently.
Some wondered what difference it might make. "The people who are praying are not the ones in the gangs," said Raquel Lavala, a neighborhood resident. "But who knows. Maybe they are among us."
The processional ended on a hill at a community park carpeted with thousands of people. Mr. Moreno was affixed to the cross and promised that one of the thieves next to him would be with him in Heaven. Then his body went limp.
Onlookers wiped their eyes and looked away. Children pointed and said, "Mira, mira," or, "Look, look," at the spectacle before them.
Mr. Moreno was then wrapped in cloth and carried toward the recessional as ceremoniously as the day had begun, and the crowd dispersed.
People went back to work and to their lives. And the streets of Pilsen returned to the men standing idle on street corners, to the homeless people scavenging for something to sell, and to the children, their noses pressed against bakery windows.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The Passion play being performed yesterday in the Pilsen neighborhood of Chicago. Many of the neighborhood's 300,000 Mexicans attend the annual pageant as a way of pulling the community together. (Steve Kagan for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Los Angeles Officers Testify That Motorist Acted Oddly

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, March 29

 BODY:
Police officers present at the videotaped beating of a black motorist told a Los Angeles County grand jury that his behavior after he was stopped made them nervous, a reaction that one defense lawyer said today could have contributed to the actions of the officers.
Transcripts of four days of grand jury testimony, made public today, include statements from two officers who witnessed but did not participate in the beating and who said the behavior of the motorist, Rodney G. King, was unlike anything they had seen before. 
 The videotape, made during the early hours of March 3 by a nearby resident, shows Mr. King, an unemployed construction worker who was stopped for speeding, being clubbed and kicked more than 50 times.
Sgt. Stacey C. Koon and Officers Laurence M. Powell, Timothy E. Wind and Theodore J. Briseno have pleaded not guilty to charges of assault.
Today Officer Briseno's lawyer, John D. Barnett, said of the officers' statements, "I think it is a fair reading of those witnesses' disclosures that they observed extremely bizarre behavior prior to the physical contact."
Asked whether that behavior could have contributed to the reactions of the officers, he said, "I think that is a fair reading of that testimony."
Local statutes require that transcripts of grand jury testimony be kept private for 10 days, unless lawyers ask for an extension.
Officer Melanie Singer of the California Highway Patrol, who stopped Mr. King and handled the arrest until it was taken over by the Los Angeles officers, testified that Mr. King did not seem to be taking his arrest seriously and seemed slow to follow her commands.
"Once he exited, the first impression when I saw him, he appeared almost happy -- well, he was smiling," she told the grand jury.
She said he complied with her command to place his hands on the roof of his car. "He then looked up and smiled at the helicopter and began to wave and then more or less dance around with his feet, like a pitter-patter step," she said.
All the while, she said, "He wasn't laughing like, 'Ha, ha,' to that effect. He was laughing like a smirk, smirking."
Officer Singer said that after she ordered him to lie on the ground, "I seen one of his hands -- I don't know if it was his right or his left, but I believe it was his right hand -- by the rear portion of his butt.
"I told him, 'Get your hands away from your butt.' And at that time he grabbed his right buttocks and turned to me and shook his butt in front of me."
At this point, she said, she unholstered her pistol and pointed it at him and said: "Hit the ground. Get your face on the ground. Spread your arms and your legs."
Asked why she drew her gun, she said: "The way he was acting, I didn't know, when he had done this, exactly what his demeanor was. I didn't know, you know, I had never come into contact with something like this."
Throughout, she said, he complied with commands only slowly and partially, and when he finally lay prone on the ground as instructed, he kept his hands by his chest and his knees slightly bent.
Local residents who witnessed the beating did not describe similar behavior to the grand jury. Nor did such behavior appear on the seven-minute videotape, which began later.
Mr. Barnett, the lawyer, said Officer Singer's testimony painted an unusual picture of a man under arrest.
"Pinching his buttocks, waving his buttocks, waving at the helicopter, laughing, dancing in that particular situation," he said, "if he was on a dance floor it would be different. But he was on the street with a bunch of policemen telling him to get down."
 
Second Officer's View
 Another officer, Paul James Beauregard of the Los Angeles school district's police force, testified that Mr. King had an unusual reaction to shocks from a police stun gun.
"I never saw a person being Tasered twice and struck at least nine times without having some more effect than that," said Officer Beauregard, who had arrived on the scene by chance. "And I was getting a little nervous myself."
A Taser is an electric shock device that fires darts attached to long wires. On the tape, Sergeant Koon is seen manipulating the wires as Mr. King rolls on the ground under the beating.

HEADLINE: News Summary

 BODY:

International 2-4
An irritant in Soviet-Japanese relations, Tokyo's claims to a group of Soviet-held islands, may be addressed in a trip President Gorbachev is making to Japan next month, officials in Japan said. Page 1 
 Boris N. Yeltsin assailed the Kremlin for seeking to restore the "period of stagnation" that preceded perestroika. He spoke at a Parliament session a day after his followers' strong show of force against the authorities. 1
A once ardent admirer of Mr. Yeltsin is now making the Russian President his target. Vladimir Isakov, an official in the Russian Parliament, says he has become irresponsible and radicalized in his approach. 3

HEADLINE: JAPAN EXPECTING TALK WITH SOVIETS ON DISPUTED ISLES

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
President Mikhail S. Gorbachev has said he wants to address Japan's claims to a group of Soviet-held islands when he travels to Tokyo next month, a leader of Japan's governing party said today.
The dispute over the islands, which the Soviet Union seized at the end of World War II, is the most important irritant in the uneasy Soviet-Japanese relationship. The Japanese official, Ichiro Ozawa, described Mr. Gorbachev's position in an interview here after visiting Moscow to prepare for the Soviet leader's trip to Tokyo on April 16. 
 American officials, who support the Japanese claim to the islands, have said a resolution of the issue would help insure stability and expand trade and prosperity in the northern Pacific.
 
Seeking Siberian Development
 Some Soviet leaders are said to hope that a warming of relations with Tokyo could open the way for large-scale Japanese investment in forestry, mining and oil exploration in Siberia. Other Soviet officials argue that the trade and investment benefits are not worth giving up the islands, which lie at the southern end of the Kurile chain, just north of the Japanese archipelago.
Mr. Ozawa, Secretary General of the Liberal Democratic Party and an adviser to Prime Minister Toshiki Kaifu, said the Soviet leader "was very enthusiastically desirous of breaking the impasse in the present Japanese-Soviet relationship."
"For the first time, officially, the territorial issue has been recognized as the agenda item for the two nations," Mr. Ozawa said.
 
Instability a Problem
 But he warned that Mr. Gorbachev's ability to negotiate a settlement might be hampered by "the unstable politics" surrounding the Kremlin now.
The shift in the Soviet position was signaled in September when Eduard A. Shevardnadze, then the Soviet Foreign Minister, visited Tokyo and announced that Moscow was willing to negotiate the Kuriles dispute. But Mr. Shevardnadze insisted that any border "amendments" would be considered only as part of an overall improvement in relations as the two nations negotiate a peace treaty formally ending their hostilities in World War II.
Mr. Ozawa said today that the island issue would be a primary concern raised during Mr. Gorbachev's trip, the first by a Soviet leader to Japan. He added that he expected Mr. Gorbachev to publicly express his desire to resolve the issue, but cautioned that he did not think there would be a negotiated settlement soon.
Mr. Ozawa, who is widely considered a powerful political force independent of the Prime Minister, arrived in Washington on Wednesday to prepare for Mr. Kaifu's meeting with President Bush in California next Thursday.
His conciliatory position toward the Soviet Union has attracted criticism from some senior officials in the Japanese Foreign Ministry, who have said repeatedly that there can be no improvement in relations until Mr. Gorbachev unconditionally forfeits the islands.
In Tokyo, the Soviet Foreign Minister, Aleksandr A. Bessmertnykh, is meeting today and on Saturday with his Japanese counterpart, Taro Nakayama, to hammer out details of accords on trade and technology that could be signed when Mr. Gorbachev visits Tokyo next month. The draft agreements also reportedly call for cooperation in aiding victims of the Chernobyl nuclear accident and for cultural exchanges.
The Japanese press in recent weeks has speculated that Japan is preparing aid packages for the Soviet Union valued at $10 billion to $30 billion to persuade Moscow to compromise. The Foreign Ministry has denied the reports.
The islands at issue are called Etorofu, Kunashiri, Shikotan and the Habomai group by the Japanese. The Soviet names for the first two are Iturup and Kunashir.
The Soviet Union seized them shortly after Japan surrendered to the United States on Aug. 15, 1945. Japan says that the act was illegal.
A State Department official who monitors Japanese-Soviet relations said the possibility of a settlement on the islands was "better than it has ever been before, because the two sides are specifically discussing the issue now." He said "all sorts of scenarios" exist for a settlement, including the transfer of two or three of the islands over time.
But two questions, he said, are "how willing is Gorbachev to put his imprimatur on a deal, and how able is he to implement a deal?"
The Soviet position is that the islands were ceded to the Soviet Union in the Yalta agreement, in which Roosevelt, Stalin and Churchill charted the postwar order. But the State Department official said the language of the Yalta accord was far from clear on the status of the islands. In any event, Japan was not party to the accord.
Mr. Ozawa said the Kurile dispute and possible future Japanese investment in the Soviet Union "are separate issues." But he added, "If the territorial-political obstacle is removed, economic cooperation, cultural interchange and technical exchange can start simultaneously.".
 
Companies Sound Out Soviets
 He said several Japanese companies were involved in preliminary discussions with Soviet agencies on investment issues.
