HEADLINE: Products That Are Wrapped in Messages

BYLINE: By BARRY MEIER

 BODY:
Companies are increasingly turning to environmental themes to sell products. But now some have taken that trend a step further: they are using packaging and other promotional material for political purposes.
The effort involves, among others, Ben & Jerry's Homemade Inc., the ice cream producer; Body Shop International , a maker of cosmetics, and Rhino Records Inc., a record distributor, who have banded together in a group called Act Now. The group's first project: to support Federal legislation sponsored by Senator Richard H. Bryan , Democrat of Nevada, that would require automobile makers to improve fuel efficiency significantly. 
 "We are companies that are consumer-oriented and want to use their communications power to affect social change," said Bennett Cohen, the chief executive officer of Ben & Jerry's, which is based in Waterbury, Vt. Other companies involved include Stonyfield Farm Inc., a yogurt producer; Smith & Hawken and Seventh Generation Inc., both mail-order distributors; the Utne Reader , a magazine, and the Calvert Funds and Working Assets , two financial services companies.
Act Now's immediate goal is to compile public petitions in support of the Bryan measure, which would require that new cars sold in this country achieve an average fuel efficiency level of about 40 miles per gallon by 2001. Currently, Federal law requires that automakers sell cars that get an average of 27.5 miles per gallon.
 
Car Makers Are Opposed
 The bill is opposed by car makers and some Government officials and car-safety advocates who say that the bill will cause producers to make smaller cars that will be more dangerous.
"We think that fuel economy is a great idea, but the environmentalists just have not done their homework on safety," said Chuck Hurley, a spokesman for the Insurance Institute for Highway Safety, an industry-supported group in Arlington, Va.
Many of the companies involved in Act Now have long promoted their involvement in environmental and social issues. Ben & Jerry's has been among the most active, using its ice cream cartons to promote public interest in topics ranging from rain forest protection to opposition to bovine growth hormone, a genetically engineered hormone intended to increase milk production.
But a year ago, the companies involved in Act Now decided to join together with the aim of affecting Federal legislation through their customers.
"We wanted a way to have a measurable impact for our efforts and the way to do that is to affect Congress," said Alan Newman, president of Seventh Generation, a mail distributor of household products in Colchester, Vt.
Mr. Newman said the group discussed several proposal campaigns, including one to end funding for the Stealth bomber. But with the breakout of hostilities in the Persian Gulf, the group settled on the Bryan measure because its aim of reducing fuel consumption could have environmental benefits like lowering oil demand and reducing air pollution. "It seemed like a natural for us," he said.
Businesses routinely lobby Congress on proposed legislation that affects them. And in the past, some companies have tried to rally consumer support for or against laws in which they had a financial interest, as when cigarette producers called on customers to oppose laws that would restrict smoking. But none of the companies involved in the Act Now campaign seem to have a financial interest in fuel economy issues.
 
Labels and Inserts
 Some of the companies, like Ben & Jerry's, Seventh Generation and Smith & Hawken, will distribute postcards urging support of the Bryan bill through their stores or mail-order operations. Stonyfield Farm will print messages on yogurt containers urging consumers to call a 900 number to voice support. Rhino Records will insert a similar message on cards put inside CD packages.
Seventh Generation, which began its campaign several weeks ago, is getting responses from about 15 to 20 percent of its customers, Mr. Newman said. Other companies, like Rhino Records and Stonyfield Farms, expect to start their efforts soon.
While company executives are entering the campaign enthusiastically, some believe they shouldn't go to the political well too often with their customers. "We have to be careful about our credibility or else we become one of the usual suspects," said Gary Hirshberg, president of Stonyfield in Londonderry, N.H.
David Hopkins, a spokesman for Senator Bryan, said that major fuel-economy achievements can be realized without making cars smallers, the prinicipal complaint of opponents of the bill. "It can be done with technology that is on the shelf," Mr. Hopkins said.
Officials of the Department of Transportation and the National Highway Traffic Safety Administration say they will urge President Bush to veto the bill if it passes. That prospect is uncertain: the bill was killed in the last session of Congress on a parliamentary vote and currently has only 34 Senate sponsors.
Act Now members do not seem disturbed by the weighty opposition. And some company executives see the effort as more of a test, a way of determining which Act Now strategies will prove effective in galvanizing customers' interest for future campaigns involving energy-related legislation.
And what if General Motors should decide to retaliate by urging its employees to boycott Ben & Jerry's Rain Forest Crunch or lobby for legislation requiring explict cholesterol labeling on ice cream products?
Mr. Cohen, for one, does not seem bothered. "The only people who don't support the Bryan bill are car manufacturers and the oil dudes," he said. "And they are the ones who are using their disproportionate clout to affect the outcome."

support of the Bryan bill, which would mandate greater fuel economy.

HEADLINE: House Prices Are Down: It's a Good Time to Rent

BYLINE: By LEONARD SLOANE

 BODY:
Anyone who wants to rent a single-family house will find a lot to choose from these days. That is largely because many homeowners who want to sell aren't doing so because they can't get the prices they want. Some put their houses up for rent, and that can mean a good deal for the renter.
About a third of all single-family homes have traditionally been rentals, but in most parts of the country that proportion has increased, said H. James Brown, the director of the Joint Center for Housing Studies at Harvard University. 
 "Now is a very good time to rent a house," said Herve Kevenides, the president of the Metropolitan Analysis and Forecasting Corporation in Red Bank, N.J. "Renting is a very good investment when property values are not increasing."
Mr. Kevenides offered, as an example, a person who rents a $150,000 house rather than buying one and therefore does not have to make a down payment of 20 percent, or $30,000. That $30,000, invested at 8 percent annually, would bring $2,400 a year. On the other hand, a buyer would have no return on a house whose value did not rise, and might even suffer a loss if its price declined.
 
Avoiding Misunderstandings
 For renters who have lived only in apartments, there are special advantages and some problems in house rental. "Renters are going to be dealing with homeowners who are not in the business of renting, and therefore renters need to be very careful in minimizing misunderstandings," said Prof. Susan Wachter of the Wharton Real Estate Center at the University of Pennsylvania.
Mr. Brown added: "The real difference with renting a single-family home is that the renter takes a lot more responsibilities, similar to those of an owner. There isn't usually a superintendent on site, and you need to worry about mowing the lawns, snow clearing and some of the exterior repairs."
Unlike many apartment dwellers, house renters also normally pay for heating and perhaps even for garbage collection, as well as for basic utilities like gas and electricity. The tenant might have to provide certain appliances, too. And houses are generally larger than apartments, so utility costs are likely to be higher.
Renters who hope someday to buy a house should keep in mind that house prices now seem to be starting back up in many areas, so this might also be a good time to buy. But renting is a good way to experience home ownership before taking the plunge.
Most house renters get more room and comfort than in an apartment. Some owners hire management companies to oversee the property and respond to tenants' complaints. House renters willing to commute longer distances to work may also have a greater opportunity to enjoy the outdoors and to have a greater choice in the schools their children attend. In addition, house leases are typically written for longer periods than for apartments because tenants often want assurance that their dwellings will not be sold from under them.
"Regardless of the lease period, you should ask the owner whether the rental is viewed as long term or short term," said Martin Rueter, a senior vice president of Century 21 Real Estate at its headquarters in Irvine, Calif. "If his intention is to sell the house, maybe you would like to have an option to purchase it."
 
Options Have a Price Tag
 A lease option, like all options, gives you the right, but not the obligation, to take a particular action: in this case, to buy the house at a set price within a set time, generally six months to two years. You pay for this option, frequently in two ways: an initial payment of up to 5 percent of the house price, and a monthly payment of up to $300 added to the rent. If you exercise the option, all of these extra payments are subtracted from the purchase price; if not, the money is not refunded.
Like apartment renters, house renters are advised to buy rental insurance to protect their personal property against loss or damage and to cover their potential liability to others, even though the homeowner already has a homeowner's policy for the structure. Insurance companies sell a tenants' policy known as Form HO-4 that provides coverage for personal belongings, liability and additional living expense when the home cannot be occupied because of damage. The Insurance Information Institute, a trade association, said premiums depend on the type of coverage and where you live.
For instance, the annual premium is $325 for a standard policy sold by the Kemper Group with $50,000 coverage for personal property and $100,000 liability for a rental house in Queens. Liability coverage of $200,000 would add $8 to the premium. "If you live in a $200,000 home, you should normally have $200,000 worth of coverage," said Barry McCall, a property underwriter at Kemper headquarters in Long Grove, Ill.

GRAPHIC: Photo: "Now is a very good time to rent a house," said Herve Kevenides, an economic forecaster (William E. Sauro/The New York Times); "Renters need to be very careful in minimizing misunderstandings," said Prof. Susan Wachter. (Sal DiMarco Jr./The New York Times)

LENGTH: 575 words

HEADLINE: A Mean Army, Made Leaner

 BODY:
America's victory in the Persian Gulf war brought glory to all branches of the armed forces. But it provided special vindication for the U.S. Army, which brilliantly exploited its firepower and mobility and in the process erased memories of its grievous difficulties in Vietnam.
The question now is how the Army chooses to capitalize on success. Will it waste time and energy in a futile struggle with the Defense Secretary, who is starting to put the cold war behind him and hold down spending? Or will it take a wiser course -- addressing weaknesses that remain, particularly in its reserve units, to maintain its strength? 
 Vietnam left America divided and disturbed, and left the Army even more so. The Air Force and Navy fended off most of the blame; the Army absorbed most of the casualties and costs. It spent years begging for funds to attract qualified recruits and to modernize its weaponry.
Compared with high-tech weapons, recruitment, readiness and logistics lack glamour and cost plenty. Most members of Congress would rather watch missile launches than maneuvers. Still, the Army persevered. And that paid off in the gulf. True, the gulf wasn't Vietnam, where the Army was ill prepared for counterinsurgency warfare. Iraq, with its Soviet-style strategy and Soviet-made arms, was the kind of opponent the Army has spent decades preparing to fight.
The Army also put the Reagan years of budgetary excess to good use. It took advantage of the pay raises and recession of the early 1980's to recruit and train qualified volunteers. It spent billions on exercises that devour equipment and ammunition but yield huge dividends in terms of readiness. And it beefed up its ability to deploy forces to remote spots and keep them supplied. Meanwhile, the Army broadened the social base of its leadership, with the Reserve Officers Training Corps securing at least equal billing with West Point.
Colin Powell typifies the new general officer. He was born in the Bronx to Jamaican immigrants and graduated from City University -- and is the first Chairman of the Joint Chiefs to receive his commission from the R.O.T.C. Gen. Norman Schwarzkopf took a more traditional path to the top, by way of West Point. But Gen. Gus Pagonis, who directed the massive gulf supply effort, earned a B.S. in transportation and traffic at Penn State, along with a reserve commission.
Oddly, the reserves themselves have been neglected. The reserves provide many of the Army's specialists, from doctors and nurses to supply officers. Yet if the Army of the future is to be as successful as it was in the gulf -- with reduced Defense spending -- the reserves will also have to fill out the fighting units.
At the moment, they're not up to it. Three brigades of combat reserves in the National Guard stayed home because the army judged them unready for the gulf war. That's because the top brass has preferred to beef up the regular army.
The Army's first response to victory was not encouraging. It continued to fight planned manpower cuts as though the Russians were still coming. Mr. Cheney wisely stood his ground. Even after the cuts, the Army will have 18 divisions in 1995, 12 on active duty. It sent about eight divisions to the gulf, more than most third world contingencies would require. Reserve units, kept in tip-top shape, could provide ample insurance. Instead of greener, tomorrow's Army is better off meaner, and leaner.

SECTION: Section 7; Page 9; Column 1; Book Review Desk

LENGTH: 1364 words

HEADLINE: American on Their Own Terms;
'The Complete Other Side of the World'

BYLINE: By A. G. Mojtabai; A. G. Mojtabai's most recent book is "Ordinary Time," a novel. She is a writer in residence at the University of Tulsa.

 BODY:

TYPICAL AMERICAN
By Gish Jen.
296 pp. Boston:
Seymour Lawrence/Houghton Mifflin Company. $19.95. 
 Migration and mutation form a persistent, characterizing theme of our unsettling time. In her first novel, "Typical American," which explores the transformation of Chinese immigrants in the United States, Gish Jen has engaged this theme with distinction. More than that, she has given us "natives" (that is, the less recently settled) back to ourselves with a jolt of sharpened self-recognition.
"It's an American story," so the tale begins. "Before he was a thinker, or a doer, or an engineer, much less an imagineer like his self-made-millionaire friend Grover Ding, Ralph Chang was just a small boy in China, struggling to grow up his father's son."
The novel charts the progress of "Ralph" (an Americanization of Lai Fu -- "Come Fortune"), who leaves China in 1947, amid the "wreckage, and inflation, and moral collapse" that follow the war with Japan. Ralph's aim is to pursue engineering studies in New York.
He has six subsidiary goals, carefully listed. Two are resolutions to cultivate virtue and bring honor to his family. The succeeding four, culminating in a resolve to have nothing to do with girls, are abandoned without recall as Ralph comes to realize that he is alone, that he has journeyed "to the complete other side of the world."
It is during his first year abroad that Manchuria falls to Communist control -- the opening in a series of moves that will utterly transform China, sundering Ralph's link with his parents and ancestral traditions and leaving him unmoored, adrift in a new world.
Then, one day, Ralph is found slumped on a park bench, in the depths of despair. The finder is none other than Theresa, his long-lost older sister, who had emigrated before him. " 'Was miracle.' This was Ralph's version of the story. 'Miracle!' " There's yet another miracle in store: Theresa happens to be rooming with a beautiful young Chinese woman named Helen, who is destined to become Ralph's wife. Together Ralph, Helen and Theresa set up housekeeping and, as they progress from permanent residents to citizens, each -- in his or her own way -- begins to undergo a sea change.
It is greed that most powerfully transforms Helen and Ralph. From their former playfully defensive recital of "typical American" characteristics ("typical American no-manners," "typical American no-morals," "typical American use-brute-force," "typical American wasteful") comes a new chant. Helen's is a litany of enraptured consumerism; Ralph's, of high-venture capitalism. Hers: "Double garage with separate entrance. Finished basement. Sliding glass doors. . . . breakfast nook with built-in benches. . . . wall unit. . . . extras galore." His: "Got to spend money to make money. . . . What you can conceive, you can achieve. . . . The whole trick is. . . . Sky's the limit. . . . Can do, can do, can do!" And, in time, as his own restaurant -- Ralph's Chicken Palace -- takes tangible shape, Ralph's dream of America as "every wish come true" seems to be realized.
I won't reveal what comes next, although no mere plot recital could possibly spoil the rest. No paraphrase could capture the intelligence of Gish Jen's prose, its epigrammatic sweep and swiftness. There's no pause, no underlining, no winking aside to the reader to signal how clever this is, how humorous that is. The author just keeps coming at you, line after stunning line. Even her incidental description seems new-minted -- purely functional, bone clean yet lustrous -- whether it's focused on the wilting of an elaborately curled hairdo in a discouraging social situation, or on children facing their first move, kissing the walls of their old apartment goodbye, or on death threatening one of the family members as "a man to sit at supper and never eat -- a man with his eye on everyone."
Here is Theresa, succumbing to the seduction of a pair of highly colored, voluptuously curved new shoes:
"These shoes had snagged her -- so vital, there in the store window, that they did not look like shoes so much as some highly adapted life form, mimicking shoes the way lizards mimicked desert rocks. Whereas the shoes she'd had on were plainly the real thing: worn out, dried up, cracking. Like their owner -- her reflection in the window was spindly and stiff, separated just this way, by a pane of glass, from some more vibrant world. At the center of her image, the red shoes had seemed to pulse, like her own true heart."
My only quarrel with this book is a backhanded tribute to its virtues, to the interest created in character and situation. There were times when I wanted to know more -- deeper and more -- moments when Helen and Ralph's capitulation to all-American greed seemed too easy, too headlong, verging on the simplicity of fable. This simplicity is clearly not wrong in itself, but it struck me as fundamentally at odds with the complexity, the multifaceted richness, of the novel as a whole.
Were there no contending forces? No dim remembrance of Confucian harmonies or Buddhist detachment before Ralph's obsession gained full sway? Wouldn't even passing reflection on the austerities of life under a Communist regime in China have raised a little point-counterpoint, exerted a chastening influence? There were numerous references to church (Ralph, unconverted, attending with Theresa), rosaries, prayers, parochial school for the Chang children, yet not a word of admonition, not a whisper, concerning thralldom to material things. Was the church completely captive to culture, then? It would seem so. Two perfunctory lines -- slogans, really -- from a homily ("Risk faith. Doubt doubt.") would suggest that the priest speaking was well attuned to typical American one-sound-bite attention spans. I wanted to know more.
It may be that this reader's lingerings and wonderings stem, in large part, from just plain reluctance to close the book on the Changs. For I came to care about all of them -- Theresa at first meeting, Ralph belatedly. And as the Changs seemed so hopelessly to drift apart, each one off in pursuit of some bright fragment of the American dream, I waited eagerly for them to return -- quan jia tuan yuan, the whole family together -- quite as if they had become my own family.

 
 
'I DREW ON WHAT I KNEW'
 Gish Jen was once asked if the publishers of her first novel, "Typical American," had pressured her to change her name to something sounding less Chinese. "My real name is Lillian," she said, laughing, in a telephone interview from her home in Cambridge, Mass. She playfully adopted Gish -- as in the actress -- in high school. "It was part of becoming a writer," she said, "not becoming the person I was supposed to be."
That person was the dutiful daughter of Chinese immigrants, who expected her to get a practical job. Ms. Jen did attend business school briefly, and even took a typing test after marriage and house renovation had led her to put fiction aside. But before she became a secretary, Ms. Jen, an alumna of Harvard and the Iowa Writers' Workshop, successfully applied for a fellowship at Radcliffe's Bunting Institute. Her submission -- a story about the immigrant Changs -- grew into "Typical American."
"I drew on what I knew," she explained. "But even though I can see the elbows and knees and noses of people from my past, the relationship is like that between a dream and life. It's similar, but highly transformed."
Ms. Jen, who is 35 years old, once lived in Yonkers, where her family was called names and pelted with stones. But the experience, she said, did not define her childhood, and she refuses to emphasize it. In fact, the comedy of "Typical American" came most naturally. "I felt like a stereo with no bass," she said. "I had to learn to hit the low notes."
Although "Typical American" does include sadness, Ms. Jen still feels it is upbeat. "Not everyone has to be like Ralph," she said of her hero. "You can use the freedom of America differently."
When asked if she was working on a new book, Ms. Jen, who is now pregnant, replied simply: "I'm hatching." -- LAUREL GRAEBER

SECTION: Section 7; Page 3; Column 1; Book Review Desk

LENGTH: 1613 words

HEADLINE: Empires on the Sand

BYLINE: By L. Carl Brown; L. Carl Brown, the author of "International Politics and the Middle East: Old Rules, Dangerous Game," is the director of the Program in Near Eastern Studies at Princeton University.

 BODY:

A HISTORY OF THE ARAB PEOPLES
By Albert Hourani.
Illustrated. 551 pp. Cambridge, Mass.:
The Belknap Press/Harvard University Press. $24.95.  
 With his customary modesty Albert Hourani announces this major work as intended for beginning students and general readers. He does not even add the hope, so often expressed in such cases, that the book may be of some interest to specialists.
Let us set the record straight: This book by one of the most distinguished scholars of the Arab world and the Middle East is a splendid achievement that can be read with profit by rank beginners and jaded specialists. It is, moreover, written with the grace and wisdom that those who know Mr. Hourani's works have come to expect.
General readers will need to give the story their full attention, however. The book is the literary equivalent of a Canaletto painting in all its intricate detail. Only in such detail can the author faithfully convey 14 centuries of history for a people who today number some 200 million and inhabit lands roughly double the area of the contiguous United States.
Mr. Hourani's approach is good, old-fashioned narrative history, starting with the Arab world as it existed just before the advent of Islam early in the seventh century, and ending with the Arab world through the 1980's. This is no chronicle of kings and battles, how ever. Political events are presented only to the extent necessary to set in context the rich history of an entire people and culture.
"A History of the Arab Peoples" is divided into five parts, treating the 7th through 10th centuries, the 11th through 15th centuries, the 16th through 18th centuries and what Mr. Hourani calls "The Age of European Empires (1800-1939)" and "The Age of Nation-States (Since 1939)." This very breakdown indicates an important way in which his interpretation of Arab history corrects misperceptions and half-truths once held by many scholars and still often embraced by the general public. Conventional accounts tend to concentrate on the Arab period of early Islam and then switch to modern times, showing the Arabs struggling against first Ottoman and then Western colonialism, the intervening millennium being deemed an unfortunate interlude. Not so Mr. Hourani, who devotes twice the number of pages to the period from the 11th through the 15th centuries than to the four previous centuries. Rightly so. Even though the early Islamic period is important and a proper source of Arab pride to this day, many of the most distinguished accomplishments by Arabs and others associated with Arab culture (everything from architecture to philosophy and even theology) came later.
Mr. Hourani also integrates Arab and Ottoman history in accounting for the roughly four centuries in which almost all of the Arab world was part of the Ottoman Empire; he writes of the development of "an Arab Ottoman culture." In this way, anachronistic treatment of modern phenomena, such as Arab nationalism, is avoided. Ottoman rule was not a matter of alien Turks ruling over colonized Arabs. Instead, Arab nationalism, and for that matter Turkish nationalism too, arose in response to the encroaching West.
Mr. Hourani also corrects the stubbornly resistant Western notion that almost everything Arab can be traced back to bedouin origins. He demonstrates the rich urban legacy of Arab culture from the rise of Islam to the present. Mohammed himself was an urbanite from Mecca. And the political geography of the Muslim world, including its Arab portions, reveals a network of cities and their sedentary hinterlands linked -- politically by the age-old Western Asian institutions of bureaucratic empire, economically by elaborate long-distance trade and culturally by ideas transmitted among such major cities as Fez, Tunis, Cairo, Damascus and Mecca. Nomads or mountaineers, always on the margins of the urban-centered world, could at times offer political and military challenges that might well overthrow regimes, but the victors would then replace the vanquished in the conquered cities.
This is a pattern of interpretation as old as Ibn Khaldun (1332-1406), and Mr. Hourani uses the ideas of that master philosopher of history from the Maghreb. Indeed, in his prologue he evocatively sketches Ibn Khaldun's life as an epitome of the cosmopolitan high cultural tradition of the medieval Arabic Muslim world. On this point it might be added that a major strength of Mr. Hourani's history is his ability to draw upon and blend into a coherent account the contributions of countless other scholars, medieval and modern. In a word, "A History of the Arab Peoples" is to be valued not as a revolutionary or revisionist interpretation but as a brilliant bringing together of disparate scholarly parts into an ordered whole.
Mr. Hourani handles the unit-of-analysis problem with skill. Why Arab history? Why not Muslim history? Middle Eastern history? A history of an individual country like Egypt? Or of regions, such as the Fertile Crescent? A case can be made for any of them. The challenge is to avoid distortion in the choice made. The linguistic and cultural unity of today's Arab world did not exist in the seventh century nor did Arab nationalism in the 17th. In tracing this historical evolution Mr. Hourani keeps clearly in focus the extent to which Arab history is one part of Islamic history; the Arabs draw from the Western Asian and Mediterranean reservoir of ideas and institutions, become part of the Ottoman world in its time, and share in modern times the fate of much of the third world. By choosing as the subject of his book the Arab world as presently constituted, "from the ocean to the gulf" in the contemporary Arab slogan (that is, from Morocco on the Atlantic to the eastern borders of the Arabian peninsula), Mr. Hourani is able to offer equal treatment to the Arabs of Africa (the clear majority) and the Arabs of Asia. The result is a balanced treatment of Maghreb and Mashriq, of the Arab West and the Arab East.
Mr. Hourani, the author of the classic "Arabic Thought in the Liberal Age," is strong on the evolution of political, theological and philosophical ideas in the Arab world from the seventh century to the present. Anyone seeking a short, clear appreciation of such recondite thinkers as al-Ghazali, al-Farabi and Ibn Taymiyya (the last is important to understanding the thinking of present-day Muslim fundamentalists) should turn to this book. Yet, he gives almost equal space to social and economic developments, especially to the texture of city life and urban ties to the countryside (strongly tilted in favor of the former) and to the evolving role of women throughout the centuries.
Somewhat like the French scholar of the Arab world Jacques Berque (whom he rightly admires), Mr. Hourani has a good eye for the kind of seemingly prosaic detail that indicates major social trends. Two examples: discussing the impact of Western ways on modern Arabs he writes about changes in taste and attitude, including "different ways of furnishing a room, hanging pictures on walls, eating at table, entertaining friends; different modes of dressing." Or, concerning the post-colonial Arab states, he writes, "To drive a wide boulevard through a quarter of the old city became almost a symbolic act of modernity and independence."
The coverage of more recent Arab political history seems a bit breathless, with a few assertions about the politicized present that will not sit well with some. But that is the problem of taking a centuries-long account of a people right up to yesterday. Sir Walter Raleigh put it well, warning that "whosoever, in writing a modern history, shall follow truth too near the heels, it may haply strike out his teeth." Those who might be tempted to kick at this point would be well advised to look to the larger picture being presented.
At one point the author does speak of a recurrent pattern in Arab history, but he seems reluctant to highlight such possible patterns. One example is the venerable tendency in Arab and Muslim culture to separate the elite from the masses, the knowledgeable from the unlearned, the rulers from the ruled -- all this existing in uneasy juxtaposition with the strong Muslim precept concerning the equality of all believers. One would like to see Mr. Hourani address this question directly.
Also, the book lacks a real conclusion, a summing up of the 14 centuries covered. Yet, on balance, such is the Hourani style. His is not a history that presents a grand design, an unfolding of God's or Nature's plan. It is worth recalling that Mr. Hourani, the gentlest of scholars, years ago wrote an uncharacteristically severe criticism of Arnold Toynbee, with his penchant for finding patterns in history. To Mr. Hourani as to the great 19th-century historian Leopold von Ranke, every age is equally important, equally close to God or to truth.
Finally, in reviewing a book representing a lifetime of scholarship it may seem inappropriate to link it too closely to current events. Yet, Americans can hardly turn a blind eye to the way in which politics and public perceptions, beginning as long ago as the 1950's, have produced a dangerously distorted image of the Arab world, distortions made perhaps even worse by well-intentioned but inaccurate earlier romanticized notions of Arabs. "A History of the Arab Peoples" is a scholarly account, not an apologia for the Arabs but a book written with just the right mix of empathy and sensitivity, and a feel for the irony of human history. This is history in the grand style. It can lead to a better understanding of the Arabs, past and present.

SECTION: Section 6; Page 27; Column 2; Magazine Desk

LENGTH: 3085 words

HEADLINE: Inside Gitmo

BYLINE: BY ANN BANKS; Ann Banks has written extensively on military families; her book "First Person America" has just been reissued by Norton.

 BODY:
SUBTRACT THE PROPS, AND THE FACE ON the T-shirt could be Milton Berle. But with the fatigue cap, wooly beard and jumbo Havana cigar, it's clearly Fidel Castro, the man who rules all Cuba -- except, that is, for the 45-square-mile stronghold on the shore of Oriente Province where the T-shirt is sold. For more than 90 years, ever since the Spanish-American War, this tip of the island has been under the jurisdiction of the United States military -- which is the point of the T-shirt. In the caption under the caricature, Fidel demands indignantly, "Where the hell is Guantanamo Bay, Cuba?"
The Maximum Leader would have no trouble finding the American naval base, of course. He and his guerrilla fighters launched their revolution from the Sierra Maestra Mountains overlooking Guantanamo Bay. Cuban employees sympathetic to the cause smuggled weapons from the Naval Supply Depot to the rebels' mountain camp. When Castro's supporters raised their flag in Havana 32 years ago, Guantanamo became a cold-war curiosity: the only American military installation in Communist territory. Castro called the base "a dagger plunged into Cuban soil." 
 Today, with the decline of Communism elsewhere around the globe, Guantanamo has grown curiouser still. Gitmo, as it's familiarly known, is nearly the last preserve of the Red Menace mentality -- a geopolitical equivalent of the Galapagos Islands. Chunks of the Berlin Wall can be purchased in art galleries, but on Guantanamo's heavily fortified perimeter fence, marines in battle dress stand 24-hour guard.
The fence is discussed incessantly on the base. When I visited recently, the first question everyone asked was: Had I been to the fence yet? It is considered so enlightening to behold that schoolchildren are taken there on field trips. Actually, the entire base, together with its Cuban surroundings, is something of an instructional marvel -- an authentic, impeccably preserved, perfectly functioning scale model of the cold war.
Guantanamo is harder to get into than Havana. No American can go either place without the official O.K. of the United States Government, but the Navy imposes special restrictions on who may visit the base. Access is particularly tight for journalists. Formal letters of application are submitted, follow-up calls made, months pass. All this to get permission for a two-and-a-half-day stopover: the Navy charter flight to Guantanamo departs from Norfolk, Va., on Tuesday and returns on Friday. During the prescribed quick tour, visiting journalists are accompanied everywhere by a Navy minder.
For my excursion to the fence, I also got a special escort, Marine Col. Gary Blair. He relishes conducting fence-line tours -- those are his men out there -- and he brings a sense of drama to the task, strapping on a 9-millimeter pistol before we set off. "I don't like to take people out on the fence without a weapon," he says. He makes sure I'm seated on the side of the jeep away from Cuban territory. "They'll be taking your picture, so why not make them work a little harder?" he explains.
We climb 79 steps to the top of Tower 21. There, on a 10-by-10 wooden platform, Timothy Fulps, a marine from Indiana, is midway through an eight-hour shift observing a postcard panorama of fluffy clouds, distant purple mountains and bright Caribbean Sea. On request, Fulps recites the Rules of Engagement that sentries must follow: "Shots will not be fired across the perimeter fence line. The only exception is self-defense." A few sentences later he fumbles the exact wording, but the gist is simple enough: don't shoot at them unless they shoot at you, and sometimes not even then.
"They" are Cuban soldiers -- members of the elite Frontier Brigade -- who occupy their own watchtowers on the other side of the barbed wire. I take a turn with the binoculars, but across the fence nothing much is happening. Nights are better, according to Fulps. "Sometimes they have parties and you can hear the music," he says rather wistfully. As Colonel Blair and I climb down from the tower, I recall a line of dialogue from "A Few Good Men," the recent Broadway play set at Guantanamo. Onstage, Blair's fictional counterpart slams down a fist and declares: "We live in a world that has walls, and those walls have to be guarded by men with guns."
 THE RIDE BACK TO town is a hilly drive of no more than 10 minutes. On the way, Colonel Blair points out another of Guantanamo's claims to fame. Lining the roadway are triangular red signs that caution in English and Spanish "Danger, Peligro, Mines, Minas." These warn the base's 7,000 residents away from the nearly 50,000 mines that ring its boundary. The Guantanamo mine field is the largest in the world, Colonel Blair declares. The mines, laid down by the United States during the Cuban missile crisis, have claimed numerous casualties over the years: five seamen on shore leave, a dozen marines from the Mine Field Maintenance group and as many Cuban "fence jumpers," several identified in histories only as "skeletal remains."
To hear many residents tell it, Guantanamo is the most secure and sheltered place in the hemisphere. They picture it as a perfect American small town, the kind that vanished 30 years ago back home. The streets are almost shockingly safe. It isn't unusual to see kids hitchhiking. Parents dread the day when they must return to the States and instill fear in their offspring. Says one contented Gitmo-ite: "You get spoiled. There are no drugs, no crime. Every once in a while, a kid gets into trouble, but there's nobody shooting at each other, nobody killing each other, nobody really hurting each other."
Nearly all the traditional institutions of American community life can be found here. Yellow school buses ferry children to a cheerful elementary school where cutouts of apples decorate the bulletin boards. High-school students run a student council and a prom. (Last year, the school organized two competing football squads, but gave up when it became clear that nobody much cared who won.) The family houses range from pristine suburban to Caribbean ramshackle; swing sets and backyard barbecue grills abound.
A white-steepled chapel holds Sunday school classes and religious services for worshipers of various denominations. The base has a hospital, a well-stocked library and a Christian coffeehouse. This being a military installation, there is no shortage of places to buy a drink, including a West Indian-style officer's club with ceiling fans and a clay-tiled veranda overlooking the bay. The Guantanamo Daily Gazette reports on the doings of base organizations as diverse as the Boy Scouts, the Bridge Club, the Little Theater, Alcoholics Anonymous, the Knights of Columbus and the Women's Bowling Association. And, of course, there is a McDonald's, just like the ones back home, except that it's partly staffed by off-duty marines whose other job is fenceline duty.
So many and varied are Gitmo's claims on one's leisure time, jokes Bill Dougherty, the base's assistant public affairs officer, that "it can get to the point where your job becomes an interference with your life style." The landscape is dotted with tennis, racquetball and basketball courts, swimming pools, fitness centers, outdoor movie theaters and hobby shops. For $6 an hour you can rent a horse at the corral or a pontoon boat at the marina. (A special license is required, certifying that you know enough not to stray into Cuban waters.) No less an authority than Jacques Cousteau has extolled the bay's marine life, and hundreds of base residents scuba-dive among the sea turtles and manatees.
Nothing quite matches the local brand of golf. According to Jack Kennedy, Gitmo's athletic director, the 18-hole Yetera Seca Golf Course was once among the finest in the Caribbean. Now it's the strangest -- a parched casualty of the cold war. Water piped in from Cuba greened the course and the rest of arid Guantanamo before the revolution. But Castro turned off the supply in 1964, and since then the base has depended mostly on less-than-reliable desalting equipment. In the worst of times, officially known as Water Condition Bravo, rationing was so stringent that family quarters were without water during the day and laundry could be washed only on alternate nights. At best, there has been only enough water to keep Yatera Seca's greens and tees green. The fairways are moonscapes of crushed coral and limestone, with the odd cactus or mango tree for vegetation. To simulate a grassy surface, players carry a foot-square piece of Astroturf and take their shots on that. The pro shop carries a supply of the green squares, but dedicated Gitmo golfers use their own artificial-grass doormats, purchased in the States.
Guantanamo itself is like Astroturf -- a portable square of artificial America plunked down on foreign soil. To furnish this illusion, a supply barge arrives twice a month from Florida. Next to mail call (which the post office signals by hoisting a happy-face flag), the barge's appearance is the most eagerly awaited event on the base. It bears to Cuban shores the material culture of late-20th-century American life: Teen-Age Mutant Ninja Turtle toys, distributor caps, frozen waffles, disposable diapers, home pregnancy tests and plastic Christmas trees that retail for $180. A popular Gitmo T-shirt spells out in bold letters, "It's on the barge," which is the local way of saying, "Don't hold your breath."
Presiding over this queer (residents say unique) outpost is Navy Capt. William McCamy. Everyone calls him "the Skipper." A progressive man, McCamy would like to be remembered as the commanding officer who improved Guantanamo's quality of life. "I want to cut down on the red tape that drives people mad," he says. McCamy compares his job to that of small-town mayor. But since he commands a garrison over which the military has sole jurisdiction, he's considerably more powerful.
In his regular column for The Guantanamo Daily Gazette, McCamy recently exhorted residents to be more security conscious. Calling the fence line a "last vestige" of the walls of Communism, he reminded Gitmo-ites of the upcoming Defense Exercises, one of several mock invasions the Navy stages each year. These drills call for nonmilitary personnel to pack a suitcase and prepare to leave pets tied in the yard with food and water. (When military dependents were evacuated during the Cuban missile crisis, their house cats ran wild, and today scores of feral cats roam the base.)
Hardly anyone admits outright to fearing Cuba, but it isn't hard to detect tremors of vulnerability in local scuttlebutt. Gitmo has a rumor mill that rivals wartime Casablanca's. The precarious supply link to the mainland is a common theme. Not long ago, word got around that all the food on the barge had spoiled. It was due on Monday, and lines at the commissary were backed up all weekend. "We just couldn't convince people that it wasn't true," says the store manager.
Jolynn Wiley, a Navy wife who teaches first grade, says she was "scared to death" when she first arrived, but has come to feel more secure in her two years on the base. "I have faith that if there was the slightest danger, we'd be evacuated before anything happened," she says. "Of course, you can look at that two ways: that I really believe it or that I have to believe it because I'm raising a child here."
Wiley waited a year before visiting the fence line. "I found the courage to go because I took my class there on a field trip," she says. "The kids played Frisbee near the fence with some of the marine guards. It made me feel funny that the Cubans could see us. What if the Frisbee went over? Would it be an incident? Of course, you know it wouldn't, but you worry anyway."
Wiley admits to wondering "how I would protect the children if a Cuban showed up at the door of my classroom with a gun." For her own peace of mind, she does her best to tune out the constant rumors, but it's impossible to escape them altogether. Recently, Wiley stopped by her baby sitter's house and found packing boxes everywhere: "She told me she'd packed her grandmother's quilt and her photo albums and was sending them to her mother. She'd heard that Castro's men were coming over the fence and that at any moment dependents would be sent home."
 GIVEN PRESENT conditions in Cuba, that scenario is less likely now than at any time in the past three decades. The island's worsening economy is reflected in logbooks of fence-line activity, where Marine sentries report diminished truck and ship traffic on the other side. Another index of Cuba's poverty, I was told, are the banana rats. The unlovely creatures, officially named hutia, look like overgrown guinea pigs. Gitmo may resemble a "Leave It to Beaver" set, but the banana rats add a David Lynch touch. "They leave droppings all over the place," Captain McCamy says. "When it's really dry around here, the smell outside is of banana-rat feces."
On Guantanamo, hutia are a quality-of-life problem; in Cuba, they are said to be dinner. According to Colonel Blair, "We've had reports that, because of the shortages in Cuba, banana rats are now a regular part of the diet." True or not, this story is widely believed and sometimes offered in explanation of the recent banana-rat population explosion. They invade the base to evade the skillet, the theory goes. Bill Dougherty even claims to know the recipe for hutia stew: saute with green peppers, onions, tomato sauce and lots of garlic.
The most compelling evidence of hard times across the fence is the unceasing flow of refugees. Some say this is at its highest level since the early years of the revolution, when thousands of Cubans escaped to the base. "There have been many more swimmers lately," Colonel Blair admits. Given the well-known deadliness of the land mines, most of those who flee swim down the Guantanamo River from Cuban-controlled territory, a distance of as much as 10 miles.
Officially, the United States keeps quiet about the refugees. Those who make it remain at Guantanamo "until such time as the intelligence people get the information they need," says Colonel Blair. Then they're transfered to the States. Meanwhile, they are grist for the rumor mill. Elaine Frazier, a high school senior, heard that a dripping-wet Cuban swimmer walked up to the door during a prom party last year.
Though some Cubans risk death to reach the base, for a handful of others commuting across the fence is a daily routine. Twenty-nine are employed at Guantanamo -- the remnant of a labor force that once numbered in the thousands. They pass through the heavily guarded northeast gate on their way to and from jobs they've held since before the revolution. (As their ranks have dwindled, non-Cubans -- mostly Jamaicans -- have replaced them as civilian employees.) The Navy discourages journalists from talking to these men, since it might cause them to fall under suspicion on the other side. For the same reason, a Cuban exile who has lived on the base for 30 years never so much as acknowledges his commuter brother when they run into each other on the street.
A bizarre ritual takes place when a commuter reaches retirement age. On his final crossing of the fence, he is permitted by the Cuban Government to bring as much as he can carry. Typically, he loads a hand cart with consumer goods -- a stereo, television set, even a refrigerator. As friends from Guantanamo serenade the departing man, fellow commuters help push the cart up over the hill and into the Republica de Cuba.
 UNTIL RECENTLY, IT wasn't hard to foresee the day when the last commuter retired and the gate would be permanently sealed. But the decline of cold-war tension elsewhere has given rise to the opposite hope: that the "cactus curtain" will go the way of the iron curtain. Captain McCamy expresses a popular base sentiment when he says, "I would be very excited to see us shake hands again."
But such a turn of events could presage the end of the United States Naval Station, Guantanamo Bay. Both Castro and American military experts agree that the base has no real strategic importance. Under the terms of an indefinite lease negotiated in 1934, the land reverts to Cuban control only if abandoned or by mutual consent. Castro, who maintains that the lease is invalid, doesn't cash the $4,085 rent checks the United States sends each year.
When he or his successor finally sits down to negotiate with the United States, the future of Guantanamo Bay will be high on the agenda. The most powerful rationale for the United States to hang on to Guantanamo has already evaporated. Navy officers used to justify the expense of the base ($34 million last year) on the grounds that America needed to keep it out of Soviet hands. Since the Russians have gone home, one is more likely to hear about Guantanamo's virtues as a training site. For navigational reasons, it's considered ideal -- ships can go from pier to deep blue water in just 30 minutes. Every year, about 80 warships come for four to six weeks of intensive training.
There are those in Congress and in the Pentagon who contend that the same training could be accomplished more cheaply elsewhere. So the Navy's immediate challenge is to find a post-cold-war rationale for continuing to pour tax dollars into Guantanamo. I saw a memo while I was on the base that framed this problem in public relations terms. The basic point: since it no longer makes sense to portray Guantanamo as a bulwark against Communism, the spotlight should be directed at its fleet-training mission. Meanwhile, to improve morale on the base, the "welcome to Gitmo" slide briefing should depict "happy people working in a beautiful, tropical, sun-splashed environment."
The evocative tone of this description suggests another possible disposition of the base. Should the United States ever return the property to Cuba, it could play a part in the crash program recently announced by the Cuban Government to bolster tourism. There is a particular vacation niche that the Guantanamo complex could fill -- possessing, as it does, functional architecture, extensive facilities for organized recreation and great natural beauty. Now that the Soviets are no longer poised for the grab, one can easily imagine Club Med moving in. Picture tourists drinking Cuba libres on the veranda of the Gitmo officers' club -- and paying the tab with pop-beads.

HEADLINE: Cuba's New Hotels Dream of Dollars

BYLINE: By HOWARD W. FRENCH; HOWARD W. FRENCH is the Caribbean correspondent of The New York Times.

 BODY:
EACH winter the number of jets unloading Canadians, Germans, Spaniards and Italians for stays in the hotels and bungalows that are sprouting all over Cuba seems to increase. Many of the visitors head for the seaside resort of Varadero, which has become the center of Socialist Cuba's headlong rush to develop a mass tourism industry. So far, this expansion has worked well. Over 320,000 tourists visited the republic in 1989 -- more than in any year before Fidel Castro came to power in 1959 -- and the annual growth rate over the past six years has been close to 10 percent.
Americans, however, are largely absent from the crowds of bronzing foreigners who lounge on long stretches of white powder beaches by day and dance to fast-paced rumbas at night. In the 1950's, nearby Cuba was the favorite tropical vacation destination for many Americans. 
 Because of Treasury Department regulations that effectively ban leisure and business travel to Cuba, Americans curious to visit one of the world's last redoubts of Communism, or simply relax in a highly affordable setting that offers a mixture of typical Caribbean resort diversions, as well as a wealth of historical and cultural attractions unmatched in the region, have had to bend or break the law by traveling through Canada.
Visitors to Cuba these days are finding thatthe hotels can be neatly divided into two groups: the old and the new. At the older establishments, like the Havana Libre, a vast high-rise that bore the Hilton logo before the Communist revolution, telephone lines crackle, water drips rather than gushes from faucets, elevators creep and dowdiness is the watchword. At the newer hotels, like the rebuilt Comodoro in Havana, or the Sol Palmeras in Varadero, if it weren't for a few relatively minor details, it would be hard todistinguish the accommodations from medium-size resort hotels elsewhere in the Caribbean. Guests at the Mediterranean-style Comodoro, for example, occupy terraced three-bedroom bungalows, with kitchens and room service, next to a large pool. They can swim up to their patio door. The bungalows rent for $150 a night.
For some travelers, however, the details can be irksome. Direct-dial international calls, for example, are impossible in a country where a premium is placed on tight political control. In the gift shops, the selection runs prosaic, from cigars, cigars and more cigars, to pictures of Ernesto (Che) Guevara, the revolutionary leader, and the collected speeches of President Castro.
Canadian visitors amid the marble and glass of the Sol Palmeras, which opened last year, said their weeklong package deals including air fare and two meals a day cost $700 a week. For a few extra dollars they were also indulging in the sort of activities that vacationers enjoy on other islands: hang-gliding, water skiing, tennis lessons, massages, martial arts courses and fishing.
Restaurants that cater to tourists tend to emphasize seafood. At the Hemingway Marina, a seaside spot on the outskirts of Havana, huge lobsters and shrimp (foods few Cubans ever see because of their high export value) are grilled nicely and served along with European wines. A good lobster dinner can be had for about $20, including wine.
And for night life, there is the standby from the 50's, a side trip to the Tropicana for a flashy night of sequined and elaborately choreographed song and dance.
The Cubans have good reason to court tourists. Left strapped for cash as never before by a reduction in aid from the Soviet Union, Cuba desperately needs reliable new sources of foreign exchange. Many tourism experts believe, however, that the country's ambitious expansion plans for tourism cannot succeed without a sizable increase in the number of Americans tourists.
While the State Department does not formally forbid Americans to travel to Cuba, the Treasury regulations prohibit private trade and investment by United States companies or citizens. An American can go to Cuba, but cannot spend any money there -- whether for a hotel room or a roadside lunch -- unless he or she is a scholar, journalist or Cuban-American visiting relatives on the island. The penalties range up to 12 years in prison and $250,000 in fines.
Some Americans flout those regulations by going to Canada, Cuba's biggest source of tourists, and joining one of the tour companies there that offer low-cost packages. The major operator is Air Canada Vacations, the tour arm of Air Canada, which runs one-week trips from Toronto and Montreal throughout the year, using the airline's scheduled services to Varadero. Other American visitors may be taking advantage of an increasing number of flights to Cuba from nearby Caribbean islands. Jamaica, for example, has begun to offer visitors to Montego Bay daylong excursions to the Cuban city of Santiago.
Although Cuban officials will not provide figures on the number of Americans who fly to Cuba via Canada or any other country, they say that the total has gradually increased in the past few years.
"This blockade is really an inefficient thing," said Luis Lieva, an official of Cubanacan, a state tourism agency, referring to United States sanctions. "If you want to come here to relax we will greet you without stamping a visa in your passport. We just give you a piece of paper at the airport that says you have entered the country legally."
A Treasury spokeswoman said the department had not prosecuted any Americans who traveled to Cuba on tours from Canada.
Cuba gets plenty of European and Canadian tourists, and travel agents say that a major reason for its popularity, aside from its novelty or historical assets, has been competitive package rates. They make the island cheaper than many Caribbean destinations for Canadians, and affordable even for Europeans, even with their air fare. The Canadian packages -- air fare, seven nights' hotel and airport transfers -- cost from about $545 to $680 a person.
 The Cuban Government has pledged, by 1992, to more than quadruple the number of hotel rooms devoted to Western-style tourism. In addition to Varadero and Havana, each of which is undergoing major expansion, tourism centers are being created virtually from scratch in various parts of the island. So far the north coast has received the greatest attention, with joint ventures with European developers under way in places like Santa Lucia and Holguin. One investor is SuperClubs, the operator of five all-inclusive resorts in Jamaica and one in St. Lucia. It is renovating Cuba's 160-room Cactus Hotel, which it acquired last December, and plans to break ground this June on a 250-room Cuba Cuba. Both properties will be all-inclusive and share a site on Varadero Beach.
Cubanacan says it has already formed six joint ventures, with hotel companies in Spain, Italy, Austria, France and Finland. Because of the Treasury Department restrictions, United States developers have not been involved in any of the projects.
Western economists, and travel agents who specialize in trips to Cuba for the Cuban-Americans who are allowed to visit each year, say that Cuba has had encouraging results with its new resort hotels. Francisco G. Aruca, president of Marazul Charters, a New York-based travel company that operates flights between Miami and Havana, believes the investment is very much a long-term strategy.
"They'll draw enough visitors so that they won't be losing large amounts of money," he said, "but people are really holding on for when the situation changes and they can make a killing." (Marazul offers Cuban-Americans two flights a week on chartered 747's; the round-trip fare is $225.)
 In interviews, European hotel industry officials involved in Cuban projects declined to speak on the record. However, they say privately that although there are no signs of collapse, beyond the shortages and economic hardship faced by most Cubans, the Castro Government, like those of his former Communist allies in Eastern Europe, could fall after two or three years, leading to a boom in foreign investment and in tourism.
The business executives say that dramatic policy changes in Cuba recently have resulted from the island's economic difficulties. The changes allow majority foreign ownership of new construction and complete discretion over management practices. More changes, particularly in the area of political freedoms, they hope, could encourage the United States to relax its trade restrictions. A State Department official declined to comment on whether the regulations limiting trade with Cuba are likely to be relaxed.
ANOTHER hope is that Cuba will lift its own limitations on the number of Cuban-Americans allowed to visit their relatives on the island. In 1979, more than 100,000 Cuban-Americans were given visas, but then Cuba began refusing many visa applications and has reduced this traffic considerably. For their part, Canadian and European tourists are experiencing their first, and sometimes awkward, encounters with a society that has lost many of the reflexes of the service economy.
Visitors cannot find Western-style entertainment or casinos or buy good quality clothes, cameras, jewelry and watches. Despite official pledges of openness and free exchange between visitors and residents, tourists repeatedly tell of the feeling of being trailed in their wanderings around towns, and of feelings of fear and suspicion conveyed by the Cubans they manage to meet.
"People wanted very much to come up and meet us; they wanted information about the outside world, but they were afraid," said Birgit Kraus, a 26-year-old German visitor. "It is natural to want to meet people and share experiences with them, but things have not yet relaxed to that point."
For years, Cuba championed what officials called social tourism, in which ideologically sympathetic Westerners and Communists from all over the world were invited to visit and offered tours of model communes, sugar plantations and other workplaces where the achievements of Socialism were put on display.
Eager to generate foreign exchange like never before, Cuba has dropped social tourism from the lexicon and embraced hedonism. In the lobbies of all the newer hotels, bulletin boards are stuffed with notices about activities -- exercise classes, diving, helicopter tours -- that are always available for a dollar fee, for the German or Canadian or Italian who is sated with fine beaches and eternal sunshine.

SECTION: Section 2; Page 19; Column 1; Arts & Leisure Desk

LENGTH: 2076 words

HEADLINE: CLASSICAL MUSIC;
Do Electronics Have a Place In the Concert Hall? Maybe

BYLINE: By TOM MANOFF; Tom Manoff is a music critic for "All Things Considered" on National Public Radio.

DATELINE: EUGENE, Ore.

 BODY:
Architectural history may one day honor Silva Hall in the Hult Center for the Performing Arts in Eugene. Visually striking, it also affords first-class sound on most evenings. Not incidentally, the hall, completed in 1982, brings home perhaps the most controversial issue for the future of concert-hall design: "electronic architecture," combining traditional construction techniques and electronic "enhancement."
Because Eugene could afford only one hall to accommodate a variety of musical styles, the architectural firm Hardy Holzman Pfeiffer Associates of New York proposed a multipurpose acoustic design by Jaffe Acoustics of Norwalk, Conn. After a long debate as to whether there is any place in the concert hall for electronics, the original plan was approved. Silva Hall thus became the world's first electronically enhanced concert space conceived as such from the ground up. 
 Since opening night, the acoustics of Silva Hall have evoked both excitement and controversy. Rock, pop and Broadway musicals sound wonderful there. Opera also sounds fine. But for most symphonic concerts, the acoustics have proved unsuccessful.
When the talented young conductor Marin Alsop took over the Eugene Symphony in 1989, she requested a full demonstration of the Jaffe system and found it wanting. "I think it's a major problem," she said recently. She added that she found the enhanced sound lacking in "bottom," and worse, the orchestra sounded amplified. As a result, she turned the system off and began to experiment with the configuration of the orchestral shell.
By now, of course, the Eugene system is almost 10 years old, and its technology has grown antiquated. In the meantime, a second-generation Jaffe system used in the Alaska Center for the Performing Arts in Anchorage and a third-generation system in the Tennessee Performing Arts Center in Nashville have met with far greater success.
Vital to the rapidly developing technology in Anchorage and Nashville is an internationally known sound technician named Gary Hardesty. As it happens, Mr. Hardesty lives in Eugene. When he saw the orchestra experimenting with Jaffe's shell, Mr. Hardesty offered his expertise. The orchestra, he said, politely ignored him. The management of the Eugene Symphony declined to discuss this and other issues connected with the hall.
With Silva Hall's electronics turned off, problems of another sort arose, since without enhancement the hall is, by design, relatively short of the reverberation that gives "warmth" to sound. Rock, pop and much theater, which use amplification, sound best in a very "dry," unreverberant, even "dead" hall. For these events, the Jaffe system is not used. Opera needs some reverberation, lasting 1.3 seconds, perhaps -- enough for warmth but not so much that vocal clarity is lost. The Jaffe system in Silva has variable settings; the one for opera is quite subtle and successful. But a symphony hall requires a reverberation time of from 1.8 to 2.2 seconds. Without it, orchestras sound dry, and symphonic music, which depends on a beautiful wash of sound, cannot succeed.
Ms. Alsop said she is willing to try anything. But she leans toward a purist approach. "To rely on a sound technician for your balance," she said, "is completely antithetical to the role of a conductor."
Her attitude is apparently not shared by the German conductor Helmuth Rilling, who comes to Eugene each summer to direct the Oregon Bach Festival. Mr. Rilling works closely with Mr. Hardesty, and together they occasionally coax a decent sound from the hall.
The Jaffe system, called ERES (Electronic Reflected Energy System), is the brainchild of Christopher Jaffe, who, as the president of Jaffe Acoustics, has been the main proponent of electronic architecture in the United States.
His work is well exemplified at Ravinia, the summer home of the Chicago Symphony, in Highland Park, Ill., where he designed Bennett Hall along traditional lines as well as the electronically enhanced Ravinia Festival Pavilion. Mr. Jaffe also included electronic enhancement in the restoration of the Circle Theater in Indianapolis, whose acoustics Harold Schonberg of The Times described as admirable.
"I don't come to a hall with a preconceived notion," Mr. Jaffe said recently. "I offer the options of either traditional or electronic design."
The Evangeline Atwood Concert Hall in the Alaska Center for the Performing Arts was completed in 1988 by the same team that built Eugene's hall, and designed along similar lines. One look at the ceiling's reflective plaster panels (a distinct contrast with Silva's sound absorptive "basket" design) on a recent visit indicated that the sound, even without electronic enhancement, would be far livelier than Silva's. The first chords of Mozart's Overture to "The Magic Flute" from the Anchorage Symphony sounded round, rich and true. Although a longer reverberation time might be preferable, the natural sound, blended with the newer Jaffe technology, made for a beautiful orchestral acoustic.
Unlike Eugene's system, Anchorage's has no variable settings. It is either on or off. The involvement of a sound technician in orchestral balance is not an issue. Stephen Stein, the orchestra's conductor, said he was delighted with the Jaffe sound: "For a community our size, we feel no less than blessed."
Later in the week, the Anchorage Concert Association presented the pianist Garrick Ohlsson. Ira Perman, the association's executive director, said he considered the hall's natural acoustic fine, and didn't generally use ERES. But with the consent of the pianist, Mr. Perman agreed to an experiment. Mr. Ohlsson played the first half of his all-Chopin program with the system off, the second half with it on. In this test, heard from a "worst-case" seat, far left, under the first balcony and directly beneath one of the hidden speakers, the system was not entirely successful, seeming to vindicate Mr. Perman's decision to leave it off for solo performances.
Mr. Ohlsson began with the B-minor Scherzo. From the first notes, one knew the evening would be memorable. But by the time the pianist played the C-sharp-minor Nocturne (Op. 27, No. 1), acoustical problems were apparent. The lower register, from middle C down, was warm, reverberant and perfectly responsive to the pianist's touch. The high octaves were brittle but acceptable. Least audible were the two octaves above middle C, no small problem in Chopin.
The second half of the program consisted of Chopin's 24 Preludes. With the system on, the troublesome octaves became more audible. But unexpectedly, the beauty of the lower registers disappeared into what could only be called an artificial sound.
Atwood Hall is a splendid auditorium for symphonic music, but an upgrade to the latest technology might some day be advisable, if the new sound of Andrew Jackson Hall at the Tennessee Performing Arts Center is any indication of the possibilities. Soon after the hall was built in 1981, the Nashville Symphony and its conductor, Kenneth Schermerhorn, became unhappy with its dry and unresponsive sound. In 1990, Mr. Jaffe was called in to renovate it. The Nashville system, designed by Paul Scarbrough, a young architect on Mr. Jaffe's staff, was installed in less than three months for under $300,000.
When it was first demonstrated, the players reportedly broke into applause. "Just imagine if we were stuck with what we had," said Mary Vanosdale, the concertmaster, in a recent interview. "You can't imagine what it's like to be giving your all on stage -- and for what?"
To purists who criticize Mr. Jaffe's use of electronic enhancement, Mr. Schermerhorn said, "Well, more power to you if you have the unlimited funds to tear down this hall and build a new one."
In a concert performance of Mozart's "Abduction From the Seraglio," the orchestral sound from midparquet was excellent, rich, clear, not in the least artificial. The system dealt honestly with the soloists. Kenneth Cox, a world-class Osmin, sounded rich and powerful, although the acoustics were less kind to the tenor who sang Pedrillo. In the parquet, where the Jaffe system was most active, the acoustics revealed his inadequacies of style and manner and poor vocal quality. From the highest balcony, he could scarcely be heard.
Despite minor problems that will be corrected when adjustments are complete, the Nashville system seemed to represent not merely a local success but a significant new technology for the world. One reason for the improved quality was a higher digital sampling rate.
The sophistication of the Nashville system can best be explained in comparison to a CD player. Put simply, CD technology measures sound 44,100 times per second. This number is called the sampling rate. In digital technology, the higher the sampling rate, the better the sound. Those critical of CD sound have suggested that this sampling rate is too low. Higher rates are now being discussed --100,000 times per second, perhaps.
The first-generation system in Eugene doesn't even use digital sampling. The second-generation system in Anchorage samples the sound 68,000 times a second. And the up-to-date technology in Nashville includes a device that samples 3 million times a second. That device was invented and built by Mr. Hardesty.
The future of the Jaffe system seems clear. In a world of scarce resources, this technology offers an economical alternative to traditional modes of construction. But what about Eugene? Are its citizens, having paid for the prototype of a successful technology, stuck with a magnificent but flawed monument to the science of acoustics?
Some people question whether the Eugene hall should be fixed at all. Except for its symphonic sound, it is a success. Eugenians have a strong populist bent, and a city government hard pressed to maintain basic services has little extra money for the arts. Any expenditure that didn't come from private contributions might be criticized as elitist. And even if money were available, the question would remain how to fix the hall. Structural renovation would be expensive, but a third-generation Jaffe system could be installed for under $200,000.
Yet a far greater opportunity is available to Eugene, in the person of Mr. Hardesty. Although he has invented some of the world's most advanced electronics, he has never held an official position at the Eugene hall. His status is "stagehand." He has spent hundreds of hours, often unpaid, working to improve the hall. Asked recently why he bothers, he said simply, "It's my home. I care about the hall."
Edwin R. Smith, the city official who was in charge of building and for a time operating the Hult Center, has only praise for Mr. Hardesty. When asked why Mr. Hardesty has never been recognized officially, Mr. Smith replied, "That's a good question."

 
 
THE JAFFE SYSTEM
 The Jaffe Electronic Reflected Energy System (ERES) bears virtually no relation to traditional amplification, in which an audio signal (a voice or instrument, for example) is projected into a hall at a volume louder than the original source. In ERES, the primary and loudest sound the listener hears is the direct, natural sound from the stage, which mingles with very low levels of electronically manipulated sounds from hidden speakers. The result re-creates the acoustic characteristics of a traditional concert hall.
The sophistication of the latest ERES technology, which samples and reproduces sound three million times a second, brings to mind certain philosophical quandaries: How many dots, for example, must be drawn and connected by lines to achieve a perfect circle? In a comparable way, the quality of this sampling system tends to break down the esthetic oppositions between "natural" and "electronic," "acoustic" and "artificial" and "real" and "reproduced."
A similar and related quandary: how many speakers would have to be mounted to re-create an infinite number of sound-reflecting points on the wood and plaster surfaces of a traditional hall? Christopher Jaffe has invented a means of processing the signals so a speaker that would normally be heard as a single source of sound flattens out into what the ear perceives as a section of wall. Theoretically, a complete Jaffe installation, with its multitude of speakers and interrelated systems, can be adjusted to re-create the acoustic response of any architectural design.

GRAPHIC: Photos: The interiors of Silva Hall in Eugene, Ore., left (Hardy Holzman Pfeiffer Assoc.), and Atwood Hall in Anchorage--One look at Atwood's ceiling indicated that this hall's sound, even without electronic enhancement, would be livelier than Silva's. (Christopher Little)

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (92%);
