HEADLINE: Plus Ca Change, More She Is the Same

BYLINE: By DAVID FINKLE; DAVID FINKLE, who writes about the arts, travels frequently in the United States and abroad.

 BODY:
WORD has been coming back for -- I don't know -- a decade or more that Parisians have stopped curling their lips at Americans who try to speak to them in French with anything less than flawless grammar and pronunciation. I'm back from Paris, and all I can say is plus ca change, plus c'est la meme chose, or, as some French person might have remarked to me at any point during my vacation, "More this changes, more this is the same thing."
That's right. I discovered that, much as in the past, almost any conversation I tried to hold in French was bound -- sooner rather than later -- to become a conversation carried on in English. That's assuming the conversation even got started in French. Sometimes I didn't get that far. 
 In fact, one of the most memorable aspects of this visit, which began for me on the train-hovercraft-train from London, was that there was just about nothing, once we'd reached Boulogne-sur-Mer, to indicate we'd crossed a body of water and passed a border. The man selling sandwiches and drinks on the train during the Boulogne-Paris leg of the trip addressed everyone in smiling and accommodating English and was happy to take money in dollars, pounds or francs.
While he was doing this, what I was thinking about was my prep school French professor, Monsieur Banta, who only allowed us to speak French in class -- the last environment I was ever in, now that I think of it, where French was spoken exclusively.
There was for me, however, a long time -- despite M. Banta and the facility he tried to drill into me -- when I was so sheepish about pushing vowels through my nasal passages to overcome a recalcitrant New Jersey accent that I was glad to capitulate to any French citizen the minute he rolled his eyes skyward and addressed me in my native tongue. During that long time I was so impressed with the way French academicians kept tabs on their language -- battling encroachments like le weekend and le drugstore and, more recently, la McDonald's -- that I was ready to bend over backward not to be branded a polluter.
I'm pleased to say that time is ended, and now I try to barrel along in fractured -- or, in my case, sprained -- French no matter what. You see, I've learned something about the French that until this trip I had always been too cowed by them to see or hear. I learned it the time I checked into my hotel, announced myself in French, asked if my room was ready and was informed that it was and "I give you here the key."
So now I'd like to pass along the tourist tip that's been revealed to me after so many years and half a dozen trips to the City of Light -- if not absolute enlightenment. This is it: MORE OFTEN THAN NOT, THEIR ENGLISH IS NO BETTER THAN YOUR FRENCH.
The hotel clerk, otherwise an agreeable guy, wasn't the only citizen I encountered who maximized the number of occasions on which he could transfer out of his language into mine. Clerks in stores also denied me the right to speak their tongue if they thought for a second that I was lost for their words.
For instance, I went to Fauchon's to have lunch in the new cafeteria built in the basement of one of the buildings the fancy-schmancy food store occupies facing the side and rear of the Madeleine.
I was at the pastry counter, trying to decide which of at least a dozen exquisite-looking desserts was most worth my forking over 25 francs (about $5 at the current jaw-dropping rate of exchange). The architectural victory I was leaning toward had a chocolate exterior shaped like a brown bag gathered at the top.
I asked the saleswoman what was inside and she said, "mousse aux pralines." I began to think that over, and, in contemplating just how delectable mousse aux pralines was likely to be as an ingredient, I must have made some inscrutable moue. She jumped feet first into the dramatic pause, fixed me with a severe expression and said flatly, "Praline mousse."
Irritating, at least to me. But, actually, this was small potatoes. The occasion that really got my multilingual goat took place in the Orangerie. If you don't know about the Orangerie, it's a compact and elegant museum at the southwest end of the Tuileries in which are housed some mighty fine Renoirs, Cezannes, Vlamincks, a truly magnificent Picasso bather and, most especially, a series of eight Monet renditions of that mysterious lily pond of his at Giverny.
I was standing in one of the two lower-level oval rooms Monet himself approved as the eternal home for these superlative works, thinking about how I could stay there forever and be content when I became aware of a group of people nearby. They were being guided by one of those thin Frenchmen with an angular nose, high cheekbones and sunken cheeks who looks like the end-product of everything Gallic.
He was gabbing in suave tones about the musical attributes of each of the paintings, saying they were comparable to compositions by Debussy or Falla. Not surprisingly, he ventured, since Monet was aware of these contemporaries. Suddenly a light whitened the room and faded; someone had taken a photograph. The lecturer's expression hardened, and without turning his head or a hair, he raised his voice theatrically and said -- in English -- "No flash please."
I don't know about the rest of the people in the room, but I felt as if I'd been slapped for misconduct. I also made a mental note that, while his was acceptable English, it wasn't so impeccable as the French in which he'd been doing his cultural commentary. I went up to the culprit with the camera and, guess what, it wasn't English in which he was chattering to his friend but fluent French.
O.K., perhaps I'm being too sensitive about this. After all, there were times when I did have entire conversations in French. At the Carnavalet Museum a woman looking over one of the rooms used to illustrate centuries of French interior design came up to me and, for some reason, asked whether I had been brought up in the Marais -- the area where the museum is located. Shocked that she could mistake me for that much of a local, I said no, that I'd been born in the United States and was just a visitor.
The possibility remains, as a few Francophile buddies have pointed out, that the battalions of English-speaking French people I encountered were either trying to be helpful or were seizing the opportunity to improve their mastery over a foreign tongue.
But I was there and my friends weren't. They didn't see that what was happening around the mouth and eyes of my partners in dialogue was the massing of muscles charged with expressing impatience and not the teamwork of muscles associated with concern, sympathy or cultural pursuit.
I love France. I love the French. I don't know why they continue to do this. Is it part of a need to hang on to national identity now that 1992 and the European Economic Community are that much closer? I don't know. I don't care. I only wish we spoke a common language -- theirs.

HEADLINE: Postings: Rising in Williamsburg;
59 Subsidized Rentals

 BODY:
The Williamsburg section of Brooklyn will see a new six- story subsidized apartment house rise on Union Avenue at Maujer Street this summer under the Turnkey Program of the state's Division of Housing and Community Renewal.
Groundbreaking for the 59-unit rental building, designed by Steven B. Jacobs Associates of Manhattan, took place March 21, and the building is scheduled to be ready for occupancy next spring. 
 Angelo J. Aponte, the State Housing Commissioner, said the $7.4 million project, to be called Williamsburg Court, will be an "economic shot in the arm" for the Williamsburg and Greenpoint neighborhoods. The money came from the State Housing Trust Fund Corporation, which gave the 99-year mortgage to the St. Nicholas Neighborhood Preservation Corporation, a nonprofit community group that will operate the housing project. Rents will be about $350 for a one-bedroom and $650 for a two-bedroom, with local residents getting rental priority.
"Williamsburg Court enables St. Nick's to continue to fulfill the corporation's commitment to providing affordable housing for our community residents," said Salvatore Abramo, chairman of the corporation, "and further our objectives of removing blight and stimulating neighborhood preservation."

HEADLINE: Talking: Refinancing;
Lowering The Costs On a Loan

BYLINE: By ANDREE BROOKS

 BODY:
ALTHOUGH this may be a good time to refinance a mortgage loan -- interest rates are lower than at any time in the last four years -- the upfront costs may come as a shock to many home owners.
A refinancing normally costs about 4 percent of the loan amount and usually requires an updated appraisal of the property, points charges (one point is 1 percent of the loan), a new survey, new title insurance, legal fees and credit checks. 
 Because the costs are high, the conventional wisdom is that a refinancing is economic only if the difference between the old and new rate is 2 percentage points and if the owner plans to stay in the house long enough to recoup the costs. But that 2-point rule of thumb can be bent a bit because a diligent borrower can lower the costs. The key is to deal with each item separately. Thus even if the saving on one is relatively small, a substantial amount can eventually be shaved off the total cost.
 To achieve this, the right kinds of questions must be put to the lender and the other professionals involved. William Selsberg, a real estate lawyer in Stamford, Conn., suggested shopping around and asking each lender for a detailed list of its requirements. For instance, he noted that it is not uncommon for some lenders to require a new survey upon refinancing, which can cost about $500; others do not require this.
 Before making any move a borrower should carefully read the original mortgage document. About two years ago lenders were busy offering "convertible" loans -- adjustable-rate mortgages that could be exchanged for fixed-rate loans at a specified time in the future. Many of these are now eligible for conversion at today's low fixed rates for under $500 in upfront fees, noted Barry Havemann, president of HSH Associates of Butler, N.J., a mortgage rate monitoring service. Thus a complete refinancing in the conventional sense might not even be needed.
 For everyone else the most important fee to address initially is the anticipated points charge, since it has the potential to consume 75 percent of the upfront costs. Lenders now are charging up to three points. Each point represents $1,000 for each $100,000 borrowed.
 The most likely lender to refinance without charging points is the original lender or, alternatively, the one to whom monthly payments are currently being sent. These companies will be the most willing to eliminate other charges, too.
In January, Arbor National Mortgage of Westbury, L.I., decided to begin offering a special deal to existing customers. "We knew most people were being tempted to refinance," said Nancy Boles, Arbor's vice president of marketing, "and we didn't want to lose any of them."
 It started its "portfolio refinance program" for any borrower who initiated a mortgage loan either with Arbor or American Mortgage Banking (a company with which it recently merged) provided monthly payments are still being made to either Arbor or American. Also eligible is anyone whose loan was originated through another lender but whose monthly payments are now being made to Arbor or American.
 Under this program there are no points or legal fees. "You don't even have to come into the office," said Ms. Boles. The only charges, she said, are for an updated credit check, a new property appraisal, title insurance and a processing charge called a "modification fee." Fees total around $950.
 About 100 borrowers have so far taken advantage of the deal, said Ms. Boles. The only ones ineligible are those who originally borrowed as much 80 percent of market value -- the maximum permitted -- and whose outstanding balance now exceeds the diminished value of the property.
 Upfront costs also can be lowered if a lender is willing to fold the points into the loan amount being refinanced, as is currently being done by Globe Mortgage, a lender based in Hackensack, N.J., that finances properties in New York, New Jersey, Connecticut and Pennsylvania.
NANCY GORDON, Globe's vice president of mortgage origination, concedes that Globe is charging borrowers 3 points. Even so, she insisted, many borrowers gain an edge by turning an old 10.5 percent fixed-rate, 30-year loan into a 15-year loan at 8 7/8 percent at very little upfront cost. Monthly payments do not decrease, but the refinancing helps pay the loan off faster. However, the change will increase the loan balance initially by the sum needed to cover the points -- for example, a $100,000 balance will become a $103,000 balance.
 Consider, in addition, the cost of reissuing a title insurance policy. Substantial savings can be achieved by asking the professional handling the new policy for a "substitution" or "reissue" rate. This should be available, say title insurance officials, if the property was bought no more than five years ago. Otherwise a new policy may be required.
 The reissue rate is around 60 percent of the new-policy rate with slight variations depending upon area. For example, Lawyers Title Insurance normally charges $563 to cover a $100,000 loan in lower New York State but only $297 for a "reissue" upon refinancing, said Ted Dacey, Lawyers Title's regional vice president.
 However, a reissue rate is normally available only from the company that issued the original policy. And it must be requested; otherwise a totally new policy may automatically be prepared. Even if another title company is being used, the borrower can still ask that company if it will honor a reissue rate, say industry officials. Many will do so.
 Not everyone automatically needs a lawyer. Whether to use one will depend on the way loans normally are handled in the area, whether the borrower feels comfortable going it alone and the policy of the lender.
 The fee charged by the borrower's lawyer is also flexible, "because refinancing is not nearly as complex as the original transaction," said Ian Hirsch, a real estate lawyer in Hackensack, N.J. He suggested calling a few lawyers to get the regional average. In the New York metropolitan area expect to pay about $500.
Mr. Selsberg, the Stamford lawyer, said another way to estimate a reasonable fee is to halve the original transaction fee. For instance, if the lawyer charged $1,000 when the home was bought, a refinancing should cost no more than $500. But even this amount may be negotiated and normally adjustments are made.
 Probably the best concession can be obtained by returning to the lawyer who handled the original closing. "He already has your details on file," said Mr. Selsberg. Further, the borrower may feel more comfortable doing so. And the lawyer is usually eager to encourage a client to return, added Mr. Selsberg.

LENGTH: 1329 words

HEADLINE: Perspectives: Rent Decontrol;
Unlocking the Tax Gold in Apartments

BYLINE: By ALAN S. OSER

 BODY:
MENTION the word "decontrol" and New York City legislators head for the woods. But the Citizens Budget Commission, a watchdog of city finance, has mentioned it in a Feburary report called "Reforming Residential Rent Regulation."
The report informs a government engrossed in budget-slashing that it is forgoing hundreds of millions of dollars in tax revenue by obliging landlords to charge better-to-do tenants less than their apartments are worth and less than the tenants can afford to pay. 
 The commission proposes two reforms. One is vacancy decontrol, in which an apartment is deregulated after a regulated tenant moves out and a new tenant moves in. At present, rent-controlled apartments become stabilized upon a tenancy turnover and most stabilized apartments become restabilized.
The other reform is income-targeted deregulation for tenants in place: If the household has an income that enables it to afford a "higher" standard of living, as defined by the Bureau of Labor Statistics, the apartment is deregulated and a market rent is phased in.
Vacancy decontrol plus targeted decontrol would produce $100 million a year in new tax revenue by the second year, the commission estimates. Higher rents would lead to higher assessments, which in turn would produce more tax revenue.
"We wanted to focus on the revenue issue, not just the landlord-tenant issue," said Raymond D. Horton, president of the Budget Commission. "We thought there ought to be new ways of raising money without doing grievous harm."
To all this Albany is likely to turn a deaf ear. Gov. Mario M. Cuomo has shown no appetite for rent reform. It is from him that the impetus would have to come. He would face a nasty problem in the Legislature. The politics there are that any "pro-landlord" action must provoke an equal and opposite "pro-tenant" action, and vice versa.
In the Assembly, the chairman of the housing committee, Alexander B. Grannis, an Upper East Side Democrat, has already reacted negatively to the plan, the commission's staff said, on the ground that it would be too difficult to implement.
The city's fiscal situation should nevertheless lead responsible New Yorkers to question the two major hidden subsidies that current law confers on voters. One goes to homeowners through low taxes based on assessment restrictions. Their share of the tax burden over the last 10 years has dropped although values of homes have risen faster than values of any other type of property.
If homeowners paid only $200 a year more in taxes for each housing unit in one- to three-family buildings, the city would pick up $117.5 million in one year, the Finance Department says. But the Legislature has restricted increases and the City Council has shifted more of the load to offices.
The other hidden subsidy goes to renters, especially wealthier ones. It is essentially paid by landlords when they are obliged to charge tenants less than the product -- the apartment -- is worth.
"If there is going to be a subsidy it ought not to be to those at the higher-income level," said W. Gordon Binns Jr., a corporate pension-fund director who is chairman of the Citizens Budget Commission's economics and community development committee, which commissioned the study. It was done by Elizabeth A. Roistacher, professor of economics at Queens College.
The proposal for deregulation based on income is novel. Vacancy decontrol has been tried (from 1971 to 1974) and so has "luxury" decontrol, directed at high-rent apartments. Even used together, they would not generate enough revenue, the commission says.
HIGHER-INCOME tenants as defined by the commission live in 20 percent of the city's regulated units, or 216,000 of the million rent-controlled or -stabilized apartments. The method used was to update the figures of a 1967 Bureau of Labor Statistics study of urban living standards. It established three levels, based on expenditures for food, clothing, entertainment, household goods, education and more. The commission used the highest standard.
It turned out that the threshold income needed currently by a family of four to live by the highest of these standards was $75,500. For three it is $59,800. For two it is $38,000, and the commission decided to lift the figure for one person to $38,000 as well.
With one exception these figures are roughly double what Federal economists believe are the current median incomes in New York City by family size. The seeming anomaly is the $38,000 for a household of two; the current estimated median is $27,650.
The commission's approach in any event produces a finding that higher-income tenants were paying an average of $616 a month in rent in 1987. Their apartments would rent for an average of $745 a month currently if they were decontrolled. The commission suggests that phase-in increases be limited to 15 percent a year.
About 90 percent of the higher-income households have either one or two members. And more than half the higher-income renters -- 57 percent -- live in Manhattan.
It is not hard to grasp why many higher-income tenants would be aghast at this. Long-occupied apartments frequently provide housing bargains because of a favorable location, but not first-rate housing quality. The higher-income tenant is more conscious of the latter than the former.
A tenant earning $100,000 a year may be paying $1,000 a month to live in an East Side apartment that is worth $1,600, yet feeling nothing but wrath toward a landlord who cuts corners on services and amenities. The tenant might well be incensed to see the rent advancing to market levels without housing quality advancing immediately.
On the other hand, because the proposal affects so many units, it can be expected that landlords in the current rental market would have to be willing to provide better maintenance and services to keep their tenants. "Increased competition in the housing market would translate into better housing," Dr. Roistacher said.
Under the commission's plan, tenants with incomes below the higher-income threshold would have to provide income data to the state's Division of Housing and Community Renewal to keep their apartments regulated. Whether this would withstand a legal challenge is not clear.
Bureaucratically there would be difficulties as well, although they are probably surmountable. The state would face a new barrage of filings from tenants and would have to determine systematically who is eligible and who ineligible for a regulated apartment.
Difficulties notwithstanding, it is hard to justify a regulation system that keeps rents low for wealthy people. "We think that starting deregulation on an income basis will eliminate the tax subsidy for those who need it least," Mr. Binns said.
The benefit to the housing market would be far-reaching: fitting household size to apartment size as small families moved from expensive large apartments; lowering over-all rent levels through a more competitive market and ultimately encouraging owners and lenders to stay in the rental business.
For the city, income-targeted decontrol using the commission's suggested threshold incomes has the potential to produce $68 million more in property taxes annually, Dr. Roistacher calculates. The Finance Department says each added dollar of taxes from rentals would generate a dollar from co-ops or condominiums. There are 416,200 co-ops and 73,300 condominiums in the Class 2 property category with rentals.
Other approaches to raising rents for higher-income tenants can be imagined. For example, deregulation could be applied to households earning more than 200 percent of the city's median household income. In that case only 180,000 tenants would be affected instead of 216,000, Dr. Roistacher said.
If nothing else, the Budget Commission's plan should succeed in stimulating thinking about a situation that is a national embarrassment. New Yorkers sometimes seem too willing to sweep it under the rug.

HEADLINE: Commercial Property: Columbus Avenue;
Turnover Stabilizes as a Street Finds Its Own Niche

BYLINE: By DAVID W. DUNLAP

 BODY:
KNOWN since the mid-1980's for high turnover, the tenancy of Columbus Avenue may have stabilized somewhat, to judge from a year-to-year survey that shows a net increase of only three vacancies out of 241 storefronts from 66th to 86th Street.
Along this one-mile stretch, 33 storefronts, or 13.7 percent, are currently empty. 
 The citywide vacancy rate for retail space is about 10 percent, said Charles Aug, president of Garrick-Aug Associates Store Leasing, making an educated guess.
"You probably have the same phenomenon of availabilities on Madison Avenue, with wholly different market conditions," said C. Bradley Mendelson, a managing director of the Edward S. Gordon Company.
Of 211 spaces on Columbus Avenue that were occupied at this time last year, 179 have the same tenants today, 12 have different tenants and 20 are vacant.
Of 30 spaces vacant last year, 17 are now occupied and 13 remain vacant.
"If the numbers are correct, I'd have to say that's a definition of health," said Benjamin Fox, executive vice president of New Spectrum Realty Services, a retail brokerage that is quite active on Columbus Avenue.
"I don't see anybody fleeing," said Robert C. Quinlan, who owns the Walker Malloy management and brokerage concern and also six large buildings on the avenue. "Columbus is still packed on weekends. The volume was probably much higher two years ago, but it's still high."
"The fact that 60 percent of what was vacant last year has been rented is a good sign," Mr. Mendelson. "Fifteen percent turnover in a year is not something that would be questionable. There's normally 15 to 20 percent turnover in any neighborhood."
"The street has lost some of its glitter but it hasn't lost its location," Mr. Aug said. "I think Columbus Avenue is trying to seek its own level. Stores selling $1,000 suits couldn't do business. The Gap and the Limited are reporting pretty good sales."
"Inherently, Columbus Avenue is healthy," Mr. Fox said, "and the fact that it's rebounded as quickly as it did, despite the fact that it's a sluggish economy, is testament to that fact."
For the purpose of the survey, a storefront was counted as vacant if there is no current tenant, even if the space has been rented. By the same token, a storefront is counted was occupied even if the current tenant is about to go out of business.
The true vacancy rate may be lower than the numbers suggest. Five vacant spaces are on a block between 66th and 67th Streets that is being sold by Capital Cities/ABC and would not be available for rent in any case, and three storefronts counted as empty in this survey, at 76th Street, will reopen soon as a Gap clothing store.
Since last year, the avenue gained six restaurants but lost another six (no net change), gained 10 clothing and shoe stores but lost 14 (a net decline of four) and lost three gift stores. It gained a private mail service, two photo shops and a Chase Manhattan Bank, near 74th Street, where Miss Grimble once sold cheese cake.
It is also getting a new 10-story office building for Capital Cities/ABC, which is now under construction at 66th Street and is expected to be completed in 1992. The building will be used primarily to house ABC news shows. It will be connected to the network headquarters immediately to the east and the WABC building to the north. The architects are Kohn Pedersen Fox Associates.
"No decision has been made regarding whether there will be any stores" on the ground floor, said Julie Hoover, the network's vice president for corporate projects.
Among all the arrivals, there were several notable departures recently. One was the Last Wound-Up, near 74th Street, which opened in 1981 and seemed to embody the avenue's fanciful phase. Visitors to the tiny store could find hundreds of wind-up toys to play with or buy.
"There's 320 square feet and they wanted over $5,000 a month rent and the street isn't doing that kind of money," said Nathan Cohen, the founder of the store, which continues to operate at Broadway and 19th Street. "I asked them to come down in rent and they refused. We would have loved to stay. The landlord put us out of business."
MR. COHEN said his rent 10 years ago was $1,500 and business was robust. Through the term of his seven-year lease, the rent rose gradually to $2,000. But at the expiration of that lease the owners raised the rent to $5,000, Mr. Cohen said, just as trade was starting to decline.
Since last year, three local businesses -- the Cherry Restaurant, Grossinger's Home Bakery and the Putumayo clothing store -- were displaced from a building on the southeast corner of 76th Street.
Those spaces are being combined into a 5,000-square-foot Gap store that is to open in May, said Mr. Fox, who brokered the deal.
The seemingly ubiquitous chain, based in San Francisco, already has four stores within a 10-block radius. "With all these units around," Mr. Fox said, "their sales figures still showed them that Columbus was strong enough to warrant another."
"I think you're going to see on Columbus more of the national tenants like the Sharper Image," Mr. Mendelson said.
Mr. Quinlan said he also discerns a greater willingness on the landlords' part to accept food-related tenants, who normally are shunned in favor of dry-goods dealers.
At the same time, Columbus seems to be losing avant-garde clothiers such as Parachute, between 74th and 75th Street, which has been replaced by the Vari Zioni boutique.
"When Parachute went out, the rent was $96 a square foot," Mr. Fox said. "I just rented that store to Vari Zioni at $74. There was a landlord that understood realities."
"IN 1986," he said, "I rented $100-a-square-foot stores and it would take me, if I had a sign in the window, a week to rent. Today, rents are down to $65, $70 a square foot. You're talking about a 30 percent drop. I think it's helped re-establish the avenue."
"It took a number of years for Columbus Avenue to find its niche," he said. "Its niche really is not to compete with Madison Avenue but really to fill the void in the Upper West Side for moderately priced clothing."
"The whole trend went too far upscale," Mr. Fox said. "A lot of retailers saw that the hard way and retrenched."
"It was such a laboratory," Mr. Quinlan said. "Manufacturers who wanted to test new lines on the public tried Columbus first." As an example of an experiment that failed, he cited the short-lived Fizzazz boutique, at 73d Street, which sold only sportswear emblazoned with the Coca-Cola logotype.
"Columbus was an evening and weekend street, a wonderful walking street, because singles went from bar to bar to bar," Mr. Mendelson said. "I think the nature of the Upper West Side resident has changed over to married-with-children."

HEADLINE: Shopping for Co-op or Condo Managers

BYLINE: By IVER PETERSON

 BODY:
RELATIONS between co-op and condominium boards and their managing agents are a little like modern affairs of the heart: The boards want to be sure they are appreciated for their special qualities, management firms seek to prove they are hard and attentive workers, and neither side is afraid of divorce if things don't work out.
Such marriages and break-ups seem to be more and more common these days, as the relations between the two shift with the city's changing economic and social climate. The boards, which once looked pretty much alike to the city's management and brokerage firms, are now more likely to demand custom-tailored services. The management firms, on the other hand, are looking hard at the costs of their services, and trying to make boards realize that champagne services don't come at beer prices. 
 "The big difference today is that the management market for co-ops and condos cuts across a much broader swath of the economic scale, and the buildings therefore have different economic priorities," said Irwin Gumley, a real estate operator for 35 years and a management executive for 15 who recently started his own management and brokerage firm, Gumley-Haft, in Manhattan. "In the 1950's co-ops were only at the top of the economy, there was no inflation, and things just went along from day to day."
Today, Mr. Gumley went on, co-op and condominium ownership has spread from Fifth Avenue to Flatbush, from Park Avenue to Pelham, and the duties of co-op and condominium boards have multiplied just as the economics of apartment ownership have changed.
The city's apartment houses are aging, most having been built before or just after World War II. Numerous city Building Code rules, from those dealing with smoke detectors to those involved with exterior inspections, multiply the paperwork. The economic slump has produced a painful phenomenon almost unheard of a decade ago -- the tenant shareholder who cannot pay maintenance fees and the bankrupt builder who saddles the board with unsold apartments for which he can not, or will not, pay maintenance fees.
Perhaps most important, say managing agents, is that apartment owners themselves have changed the way they view their apartments. Where an apartment was once simply home sweet home, many of today's owners regard their co-op or condominium as an investment portfolio; they express their worries about care and the reputation of their investment with a hawk-like attention to the way it is managed, particularly now that real estate values have been battered by the recession.
Owners argue over board policies, badger the management and demand to be heard.
"Before, we managed housing," said Roger Tuckerman, president of Brown Harris Stevens, one of the city's biggest and oldest brokerage and management firms. "Now we manage investments."
Donald Goodwin, Brown Harris Stevens' director of management, noted how the work of simply attending board meetings had multiplied.
"TWENTY years ago, if a board met more than twice a year, you would say it was a troubled building," he said. "These days, boards in buildings where everything is fine will still meet every other month."
Mr. Goodwin's colleague, Julie Willkie, noted how such a routine trend as the one toward interior renovations has burdened managing agents.
"People used to buy what they liked," she said, "now they renovate, so you have six months of painters and plasterers and carpenters going up and down the elevators and getting on the other shareholders' nerves. And guess who has to handle the complaints."
In this atmosphere, buildings are shopping around as never before, to hear board members tell it, for the managing agent that fits their needs. To meet the challenge, the management agency business is fragmenting and specializing, with a dozen or more new companies formed in the last decade to compete with the big established firms.
Leslie Kaminoff, head of Akam Associates, focuses on management only and eschews the sale of apartments, which traditionally accounted for most of a management firm's income. Stuart Bardin of Quadrangle Realty Services has made a reputation as a manager of small buildings that large companies disdain, and for dealing with co-ops whose sponsor has defaulted on unsold shares.
Perhaps the biggest recent splash in the management and brokerage business was made by the Milstein family of bankers and developers, when they bought Douglas Elliman & Company, an old-line firm, in late 1989.
Howard P. Milstein, the firm's new chairman, noted in an interview that the company built 3,000 New York City apartments in the 1980's. But with construction now all but stalled, he said, integrating management and brokers with his family's banking and development expertise is a logical step.
"Real estate money in the 1990's is going to be made in services," he said. His management firm is seeking contracts with the Resolution Trust Corporation -- the Federal agency that disposes of properties held by failed savings and loan institutions -- to manage the liquidation of properties taken over from failed real estate lenders. Mr. Milstein suggested that ownership of a management and brokerage agency will strengthen his ability to win R.T.C. contracts.
At the same time, by spanning services from banking to building, Mr. Milstein said, his company can offer its co-op and condominium management clients, for a fee, a broad list of customized services, from savings in insurance costs to expertise in conducting capital improvements and repairs.
While Douglas Elliman pursues the managing agent's traditional role of handling everything from supervising roof replacements to putting flowers in the lobby, some agencies are helping cost-conscious co-op boards move in the opposite direction, offering limited services and a degree of self-management as a way to save money. Under this system, boards may order services from a menu of tasks at set prices from management companies, and do other tasks, such as dealing with outside contractors, themselves.
If there is an overall theme to the quest for the right balance of savings and services, it is the search for a way to satisfy the desire for specialized, individualized treatment. What board members seem to want, managing agents and board members themselves agree, are managers who care as much about the apartments under their wing as the owners of the apartments do.
"Anybody can order heating oil and anybody can pay the payroll," said Grace Griffenberg, president of the board of 40 Sutton Place, a condominium of 76 apartments. "It's the other intricate parts of managing a building that make the difference -- is the superintendent efficient or is he wasting everybody's time? Is the staff managed properly, and can you get them to return your phone calls?"
Unreturned telephone messages were apparently one of the gripes she and her fellow board members had about Douglas Elliman shortly after it was acquired by the Milstein family three years ago.
"They were terribly impersonal, and we couldn't get them to return our calls, and there was really no sense of planning ahead for our maintenance, she said. "It was just, 'Wait for it to break and we'll fix it.' " Her condominium is now managed by Akam Associates.
EXECUTIVES at Douglas Elliman insist that the firm has strengthened its responsiveness by drastically reducing the number of buildings each account executive manages from about 15 to 7 or 8, a process Mr. Milstein believes will eliminate the feeling boards get that they must compete for attention.
The rivalry between managing agents is intense, and few of them have a good word to say about the others. But in one area Douglas Elliman is being quietly but fervently cheered on by its competition -- its almost single-handed drive to force management fees upward. After a decade of driving each other's fees downwards, in which management agencies raided the competition's roster by driving fees downward, other agencies hope they will be able to demand larger fees as well.
MR. MILSTEIN said that he has won management fee increases averaging 70 percent while losing only 10 percent of his client list, leaving about 160 buildings. A typical Douglas Elliman client is accordingly being charged $35,000 to $45,000, instead of the $24,000 that is the norm elsewhere, for a year's services in paying the bills, keeping the books, dealing with the city, managing the staff and attending board meetings.
"The previous philosophy at Douglas Elliman was basically a philosophy of depreciating the product," Mr. Milstein said. "We are going to provide a first-class service and make it pay for itself."
This is a departure from past practices at management and brokerage firms that depended on the commissions from the sale of apartments in buildings they managed to cover any shortfall in management costs not covered by the fee.
That formula worked a decade and more ago, when the firms could count on brokering 75 percent of the sales in their buildings. But then, in the early 80's, came the trend toward co-brokering, or sharing, commissions with outside brokers. This was followed by a blossoming of small, independent real estate brokers who cruised the co-op and condominium cocktail circuit for listings.
The share of management-firm sales has now dropped to about 15 percent of the total, while in the last few years the number of sales themselves declined with the slump, leaving many firms with heavy losses.
Both Douglas Elliman and Brown Harris Stevens underwent management changes and personnel cutbacks in this period, and both companies are still looking for solid footing in the market, which is why even Douglas Elliman's competitors hope it can get boards used to paying more.
Part of the problem in squeezing more money out of the board, Mr. Goodwin pointed out, is that more than 85 percent of a co-op or condominium board's budget is devoted to fixed costs like taxes, salaries, insurance, utilities and so on. That leaves 15 percent for the board to exercise some control over, he said, including management fees.
"When I came into this business you almost never asked for fee increases," he said. "And when you did, you went to them with hat in hand and almost begged for it. So over the years, we have sort of trained the boards not to expect an increase, and to regard them as negotiable when we do go to them for one."
The search for higher fees comes at an awkward time this spring, with the contract between most of the city's apartments and the Service Employees International Union, which represents building staff, set to expire on April 20. Most large buildings have their labor contracts negotiated by the Realty Advisory Board on Labor Relations, where contract talks on wage increases and other benefits are under way.
Managing agents are notoriously cagey about the exact number of apartments on their rosters, and prefer to give approximate numbers. An even more tender subject is the loss of buildings to others, and the talk among managing agents today is about how many accounts the firm really lost when it began forcing prices up. Competition is naturally keenest for the trophy buildings, co-ops of such refined reputation and location as to serve as subtle advertisements for a managing agent's abilities to please.
Mr. Kaminoff won such a prize when he took over management of the Beresford, on Central Park West between 81st and 82d Streets, from Douglas Elliman in 1989. Mr. Kaminoff, who started out with one building on Vermilyea Street in Upper Manhattan nine years ago, said he won the Beresford and "about 50" other buildings from bigger and better-known firms by tailoring his services to each building.
"The key thing with boards are finances and reporting flexibility," Mr. Kaminoff said. "Ninety-nine percent of the management companies have a boilerplate financial report which they send to every building they manage on a monthly basis. That was fine when every building was a co-op, but now we have co-ops and condos and cond-ops, and every board has its own financial strategy and needs to see different kinds of reports. We advise them on those issues."
The other approach Mr. Kaminoff favors is the monthly building walk-through, where the managing agent makes a white-glove inspection with the resident staff and then suggests maintenance initiatives. The sooner a manager can warn the board of an impending problem, Mr. Kaminoff said, the easier it will be to solve the problem.
And, Mr. Kaminoff said, echoing other managers, management has to pay for itself. No more, he said, should managing agents entertain the fiction that real estate sales will help cover management costs.
ALTHOUGH rates vary widely, it appears that they typically run between $300 and $400 an apartment annually for a normal elevator co-op of under 100 apartments. Some managing agents use a formula that begins with a standard, flat fee of $20,000 to $30,000, and add $300 to $400 for each apartment over a certain number. Exact fees for a building are a closely held secret, however, since no manager wants to have to explain to Client A why it is paying more than Client B across the street.
"What happened is that boards are now more willing to pay for services," Mr. Kaminoff said. "They got burnt by the old promises the brokerage companies made that sales would cover management costs. Today, if you can show a building that you can help them, they are willing to pay for it."
The itemized menu of limited services works a little differently. Many co-op boards have been receiving contract proposals from management firms that spell out exactly what a building will get for a limited fee -- perhaps $16,000 instead of the standard $25,000 or $30,000, for handling routine tasks such as collecting maintenance fees, paying staff, notifying owners of pending board meetings, calculating income tax deductions for building mortgage interests costs for shareholders each January and attending two board meetings a year.
Any additional services -- handling flip taxes on sold co-ops, for example, or gathering paperwork to appeal a tax increase and attending more than one or two board meetings -- are billed on an hourly rate, just as law firms are able to bill down to the minute for all services rendered.
Fine-tuning management services like this is requiring firms to invest heavily in computerization, Mr. Tuckerman of Brown Harris Stevens said, but once done, it would give co-op boards far more flexibility in buying services than they now have.
"If your lawyer can bill you according to each phone call he makes or letter he writes," Mr. Tuckerman said, "there is no reason we shouldn't be able to do the same thing, if that's what the customer prefers."

HEADLINE: Q and A

BYLINE: By BRUCE LAMBERT

 BODY:

Teaching at Home
 
Question: I am a full-time school teacher and want to offer computer tutoring in my rent-stabilized apartment nights and weekends. Do rent guidelines permit that? . . . Houda Chawki, Corona, Queens
 
Answer: Yes. Thomas R. Viola, a spokesman for the State Division of Housing and Community Renewal, said that no rent regulation bars you. There are, however, two other potential obstacles.
First, zoning laws. Vahe Tiryakian, spokesman for the city's Buildings Department, says the occupational use must be secondary to the residential use and limited to 500 square feet or a fourth of the space, whichever is more. The person offering the product or service, who must live there, is permitted only one nonresident employee. The following enterprises are banned: commercial stable or kennel, public relations or ad agency, beauty parlor, barbering, depilatory office, interior decorating office, ophthalmic dispensing, pharmacy, real estate or insurance office, stock brokerage and veterinary medicine. Classes are limited to four students.
On the off-chance that your tutoring involves computer music, be aware the rules limit music classes to one student at a time.
A second barrier may be your lease. The state's Court of Appeals has ruled that a psychologist who partitioned an apartment office and saw up to 22 clients weekly significantly violated a standard clause in rent-stabilized leases specifying residential use. No precedent exists on a case exactly like yours, according to Michael R. Finder, a Manhattan housing lawyer. If you have such a lease, said Mr. Finder, theoretically the landlord could challenge it. You would better withstand a challenge by limiting your classes, students and hours and by keeping the tutoring incidental to your residential use and secondary to your main job outside.
 
Razor-Wire Usage
 
Question: An apartment building next door has a 10-foot-high fence topped with coils of razor wire along my property line. Our community board office told me that razor wire, which seems dangerous and unsightly, is against the law. But the building's management told me there are no restrictions on the wire or height. Are they right? If not, what can I do to make them take it down? . . .Susan Lowes, Brooklyn.
 
Answer: The management is right about the wire but wrong about the height restriction. Mr. Tiryakian said fences are limited to a height of 6 feet in areas zoned residential and 10 feet elsewhere, not including the height of the wire. Call the New York City Buildings Department's borough complaint office at (718) 802-3681 for inspection and enforcement.
 
Co-op Rental Liability
 
Question: If a co-op owner loses his apartment through legal action because of a proprietary lease violation, what happens to his equity? Who becomes the owner? How is the property disposed of? Who receives the proceeds? . . . Fay Cea, Manhattan
 
Answer: The co-op board would take control of and sell the shares of stock for the apartment. Any money left over after satisfying liens would revert to the evicted former owner, according to Marc J. Luxemburg, president of the Council of New York Cooperatives. Co-op evictions and forced sales are unusual, but not unheard of.
The most common cause is unpaid maintenance and assessments. The sales proceeds would be used to cover those delinquencies, as well as selling costs like legal fees, advertising and commissions. Other possible liens could include unpaid bank loans, tax bills or court judgments.

HEADLINE: ART;
A Look at the Last Century Through American Indian Eyes

BYLINE: By VIVIEN RAYNOR

DATELINE: RYE

 BODY:
AT the Rye Arts Center is "Visions of the Past Century: Paintings by Native Americans (1897-1987)." This is a group of some 30 works culled by Evelyn Bernstein from the collection of the Smithsonian Intitution's National Museum of the American Indian.
Soon after its opening, the show received a boost from Mrs. Bernstein's husband, William, executive vice president of Orion Pictures, who gave a lecture on the making of the Oscar-winning Orion movie "Dances With Wolves." 
 But after the fanfare all that remains is the exhibited art, a sound track of Indian music and, to this observer, an atmosphere of great sadness.
This is established at the beginning by "An Omaha Indian Kills a Horse Under Long Cat." The horse is off the ground, its back arched, blood pours from the bullet wound in its side and arrows hurtle through the air. But Long Cat, an Oglala Sioux, survived to tell the tale himself in this sepia ink and red crayon drawing of circa 1880.
Except for the rifle, the horse and the medium, the image could have been done on a cave wall in pre-Conquest or even prehistoric times. After all, the technique -- figures that are almost ideograms, the absence of perspective -- exists in the past of all cultures and it appears childlike. Yet, as with the paleolithic art at Lascaux and Altamira, no child could have done this, for everything in it is knowledgeably defined, down to the bump of the pelvis in the horse's back.
Although it is one of the smallest works in the show, Long Cat's self-portrait is the most memorable, for notwithstanding the symptoms of progress, it represents all too clearly the moment when man stopped being part of nature and became its boss.
The Europeans' introduction of new material like beads and, some say, the color blue, changed the character of Indian art and in some ways for the better. But it took the 20th-century notion of art as an end in itself to change the quality (for the worse) and, perversely, the same notion makes viewers generally prefer what they deem "unspoiled" Indian art.
Still, if visitors put on their 20th-century lenses, the show has quite a bit to offer. Near Long Cat is an undated ink line drawing on hide of Eskimos chivying elk, by George Aden Ahgupuk. Most of the parka-ed figures are waving their arms at the herd, but one has wrestled an animal to the ground. The scene is European in style, and something -- smudges modeling the mountains in the distance, the presence of a lone elk running and bellowing in the foreground -- hints that it was based on a photograph.
Also noteworthy are the pencil and crayon drawings of totem pole images that are attributed to the Nootka of Vancouver Island. Until the eye identifies the elements -- birds, killer whales, faces and the like -- these compact designs have the look of a William Blake drawing.
Earnest Louis Spybuck, an "absentee" Shawnee working in the second decade of the century, is more a folk artist diligently recording folk customs in "Woman's Dance at Night -- Pigeon Dance." Done in crayon and water color, this is the equivalent of a village hop, with the women, dressed in blouses, skirts, scarves, flowered hats and high-heeled boots standing in the center. At the right two of the men are dancing together, but most are minding their own business sitting around the clearing, which is surrounded by tents and shelters made of poles and straw roofs.
"Sauk and Fox Buffalo Dance," by the same artist, is a similar picture except that it includes a man eviscerating a dog in the foreground, presumably toward a banquet of stew.
Much more sophisticated is the encounter between two braves, one on a bucking white horse, the other on the ground with a leg trapped by his fallen skewbald mount. A gouache on paper board, it was painted by Velino Shije, who in the 1920's designed the sun symbol for New Mexico's state flag. A statement accompanying the show explains that for this act he was considered "outrageously individualistic" and was expelled from his Zia tribe.
By the 1930's, schools for Indian artists were emerging -- notably in New Mexico and Oklahoma -- and although there is no matching artists to teachers, the effect is obvious. An example is "Gan Dancer," a 1962 work by the Mescalero Apache artist Ignatius Palmer, one of several small figures flatly painted in gouache that evoke both Oriental art and the late Kandinsky.
Again, there is a non-Western strangeness about the Navajo Jimmy Toddy's watercolors of the 1940's. One, untitled, is of a mockingbird perched on a wire in the foreground while more birds fly over a hogan in the background. The other is a gouache of a deformed tree.
The trouble starts in the 1960's with the prints of the enormously successful Navajo artist Rudolph Carl Gorman, particularly with the 1964 lithograph of a woman posed, Vogue model-style, with a tulip pot, titled "Navajo Mother." After that comes a 1982 work by the Cherokee artist Connie Seabourn: "Mother Shades," a dappled watercolor of a similarly glamorous woman in front of an adobe structure. This virtuoso performance has the same depressing effect as Veloy Vigil's "Owl's Hoot," an acrylic of two Indian-like figures executed in slashes of furious red, blue and green -- a commerical kind of Expressionism.
It is hard not to rejoice that economically, Native American artists are drawing abreast of the imported kind, but it is just as hard not to be dismayed at the way the artists are doing it. Apropos, one of the most telling pictures in the display is the Hopi painter Arlo Nuvayouma's 1965 gouache of an Indian family picking out a television with the aid of a white salesman.
The show remains through April 14 at the center, 51 Milton Road, in Rye.
The distance between the Indians at Rye and Carl Rattner at the Edward Hopper House, in Nyack, cannot be measured in miles alone. Mr. Rattner's show, like that of David Weinrib last year, fills the house with an erotic atmosphere.
Visceral forms, usually cast in a pink or tan ceramic, undulate horizontally or else twist in and out of each other with no end. Endlessness, in fact, is one of the principal themes here. The drawings resemble the configuration of an ear, except that it is often impossible to distinguish the forms from the spaces between them.
But there is no formal ambiguity in the sculpture. "Spiral," for instance, looks like a serpent extruding smaller serpents, one at each end. Then there are the more bone-like forms that cluster together, invoking barnacles, bean pods and other natural curiosities. It is an organic world that Mr. Rattner inhabits, and things are often splitting, as if in the act of giving birth. Another motif is the half-egg form that reposes on its curved back and is modeled into curved ridges on the top.
Finally, there are the rippling shapes confined in vertical wooden boxes, lest they wriggle free. The boxes, however, do a good job, and their zigzagging outlines add a stimulating touch of discipline to the proceedings. Geometry appears in a few modeled works, too, such as "Angle," a spiral that is a triangle before it softens into a ribbon.
Mr. Rattner is a sculptor in craftsman's clothing, and he achieves his shapes in a way that suits his purpose but seems convoluted to this observer. Each element is modeled in longitudinal sections, and cast so that the seams and slits are integrel to the design. A more grandiose sensibility would do them in bronze and, perhaps, would lose the fleshy impact that comes with the pink and beige ceramic. The work stays at 82 North Broadway through April 7.

GRAPHIC: Photos: In Rye are "An Omaha Indian Kills a Horse Under Long Cat," above, and "Cold Journey Home." A sculpture show in Nyack includes "Interlock" by Carl Rattner, right. (Top and left, the Rye Arts Center; above, the Edward Hopper House)

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Review

TICKER: OPC (NYSE) (55%); 

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (95%); EUROPE (69%); 

STATE: NEW YORK, USA (84%); 

COMPANY: ORION PICTURES CORP (55%); NATIONAL MUSEUM OF THE AMERICAN INDIAN (58%);

HEADLINE: Campus Life: Southwestern;
From This Class, Many Signs Point To the Windy City

DATELINE: CHICAGO

 BODY:
Once the social-science laboratory for the University of Chicago, the city of Chicago has become the site of an urban studies experiment for an interdisciplinary humanities course taught about a thousand miles away.
Some 40 students from Southwestern University in Georgetown, Tex., visited the city last week to see firsthand what they had been studying since January in University Studies Class 216, popularly called the Chicago course. 
 "Texans, in general, don't automatically see Chicago as a preferred destination," said Martha Allen, a history professor who teaches the course with Daniel Hilliard, a sociology professor, and Farley Snell, a religion professor.
But Professor Allen said a reading of literature from different academic disciplines pointed to a distinct trend: Chicago as a center of modern American thought. "Every area we looked at -- architecture, higher education, economics, art, literature, urban planning, social work -- they all pointed toward Chicago," she said.
Last week, her students were pointing at Chicago, specifically at the Art Institute; Clarke House, the oldest house in Chicago; the Pullman community, the company town that became a site of the infamous 1894 Pullman strike; the city's historical society to view the exhibit "Chicago in the 1890's," the very subject of their classroom work, and both the Frank Lloyd Wright home and studio and the Wright-designed Unity Temple in the suburb of Oak Park.
"I wanted to learn more about the history of the city," said June Chen, a senior biology major from Austin, Tex., who had visited Chicago on her own last summer. "Like the Frank Lloyd Wright tour -- His work is more interesting in person because we learned about it in class."
Developed under a National Endowment for the Humanities grant, the Chicago studies course was structured to teach students the interrelationships between humanities disciplines. Of the 18 credit hours that students receive, six are based on the study of Chicago.
With an enrollment of 42 students, Chicago studies is one of the most popular courses on campus. Indeed, Chicago itself seems to have captured Southwestern University's fancy. The college library has built a collection of more than 400 books about the city, while students, faculty and staff members have all worked to finance the class's Windy City trip.
Fund raising included helping to take inventory at the Austin Montgomery Ward; auctioning off student labor to do odd jobs for faculty and staff members, and holding a garage sale.
Then there was "the Jug," an empty bottled-water container for any spare change that students wanted to cast its way. "Even students who weren't signed up to go on the trip contributed," said Professor Allen. The jug total: $90.
"We studied it to be such a big city," said Harland DeWitt, a senior in American-studies from Houston. "But now that I'm here, it has a kind of weird, small-town feeling to it."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Students from Southwestern University in Georgetown, Tex., on a tour of the Historic Pullman Center, a community built for industrial workers in 1880-1884, during a trip to Chicago last week. The trip is part of a course popularly called the Chicago course, which is structured to teach students the interrelationships among the humanities disciplines. (Steve Kagan for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Suzanne Guthrie To Marry in Fall

 BODY:
October wedding plans for Suzanne Briggs Guthrie and David Perry Harmon 3d, a son of Melissa Pederson of Glenview, Ill., and Mr. Harmon Jr. of Annandale, Va., have been announced by her parents, Mr. and Mrs. William Vanderbilt Guthrie of Chappaqua, N.Y.
 Miss Guthrie, 24 years old, is a sales representative at Richard Bauer & Company, paper brokers in New York. She and her fiance, 25, graduated in 1988 from Denison University. Her father is a senior vice president for sales and marketing at the Brown Printing Company in New York. Her mother, Gwendolyn Briggs Guthrie, is a librarian at the Chappaqua Public Library and a teacher at the Country Day Nursery School in Chappaqua.
 The prospective bridegroom is a sales representative at DHL Airways in New York. His father is an agricultural and economic consultant in Annandale.

HEADLINE: Business Diary: March 24-29

 BODY:

COMPANIES
 
What Sort of Price for A.T.&T.'s Reluctant NCR Prize?
 

It has not exactly been a perfume and flowers courtship. From the moment the American Telephone and Telegraph Company announced its intention to buy the NCR Corporation it has been waltzing on its presumed partner's toes. There were threats: A.T.& T. said it could muster enough shareholder votes to remove NCR's board of directors and replace it with a pro-A.T.& T slate at last Thursday's annual meeting in Dayton, Ohio. The tallies will not be known for 10 days, but it is believed that A.T.& T. fell short. It could only get enough votes to replace four directors -- not enough to soften NCR's stance. There was haggling: Just prior to the annual meeting, Charles E. Exely Jr., NCR's chairman, authorized a price of $110 a share, or $7.45 billion for the company, a figure A.T.& T rejected. But A.T.& T. is said to be willing to pay $105, if the terms are friendly. On the strength of last week's proceedings, NCR's stock closed at $99.625, up $2.
 
Sony Eyes Orion's Oscars
 
If Orion Pictures was the big winner Oscar night with "Dances with Wolves," capturing best picture and six other awards, Japan was a loser. Neither MCA, which Matsushita Electric Industrial Company bought in November for $6.1 bilion, nor Sony, which spent $3.4 billion to buy Columbia Pictures Entertainment in 1988, won a single Oscar. But Sony may try to buy its way into this year's pack yet. Sony's affiliate, Castle Rock Entertainment, is talking about buying Orion. At the moment, Orion needs money more than Oscars. It lost $15.6 million in last year's third quarter on revenue of $134.9 million, and it has $509 million in long-term debt. Orion's biggest investor, billionaire John W. Kluge, who owns 68 percent, has hired Salomon Brothers to sell his stock. Just the invitation Sony needs.
 
One More Airline Down
 
Will Midway Airlines be able to ride out the turbulence of Chapter 11? The Chicago-based carrier became the third major airline to file for bankruptcy protection since December. It filed to get a $40 million line of credit from Continental Illinois that it needs to keep flying. Midway vows to stay in the air and its chairman, David R. Hinson, has promised to maintain its schedules. But analysts are not optimistic about Midway's re-emergence. Last year it borrowed heavily to buy landing slots, planes and routes for $200 million from bankrupt Eastern Airlines. Higher fuel prices, a traffic downturn during the gulf war and intense competition have since stretched the company thin. "Unless there is a traffic recovery by June, the likelihood of a successful reorganization is dim," said Julius Maldutis of Salomon Brothers.
 
Maxwell in Perpetual Motion
 
Robert Maxwell's passion is the deal. Two weeks ago, the hyperactive Mr. Maxwell bought The New York Daily News. The Tribune Company paid him $60 million to take over its liabilities which could easily amount to twice that much. Last week, while in Israel attempting to start a Russian language weekly newspaper, Mr. Maxwell agreed to sell Pergamon Press, a publisher of scientific books and journals, to the big Dutch publisher Elsevier for about $770 million. One deal pays for the other. The proceeds from Pergamon's sale will be used to pay off some of the $1.7 billion in debt the Maxwell Communication Corporation ran up from its frenzied acquistion of Macmillan Inc., and the Official Airline Guide. Pergamon, one of Mr. Maxwell's original holdings, was purchased in 1951. In 1971, he temporarily lost control of the company. When he regained it later that year, he moved its headquarters onto the grounds of his lavish Oxford estate -- he possesses one of the best wine cellars in Europe -- as a dramatic symbol that Pergamon would always be his.
 
I.B.M. Trims Its Ranks
 
What sounded like a distress call may end up being a victory shout. The International Business Machines Corporation announced it was paring its ranks. Big Blue said 14,000 employees would go -- 10,000 through attrition and various incentives, another 4,000 with the sale of the company's typewriter, printer and office products division. A sign of trouble? The giant says no, just a new, trim fit. It was "not just the conservative, but the prudent thing to do," said Daniel Mandresh, who follows I.B.M. for Merrill Lynch. The cuts could mean a saving of $400 million this year in pretax expenses. The analysts were overjoyed.
 
Abboud Dismissal Redux
 
In his stormy career, A. Robert Abboud has been fired from some pretty fancy jobs. Last week it happened again. Mr. Abboud was asked to leave as chairman of First City Bankcorporation of Texas, which he and others took over in 1988. The bank lost $268.2 million in the last half of 1990. In 1980 it was the First Chicago Corporation from which Mr. Abboud was dismissed. In 1984, Armand Hammer ousted him from Occidental Petroleum.
 
 
 
INTERNATIONAL

 
South Korea Chooses a Plane
 
The fighting may be over but the casualties continue. After negotiating for two years with the McDonnell Douglas Corporation to jointly develop a front-line fighter based on the twin-engine F-18A, South Korea suddenly turned frugal. It canceled the project in favor of a cheaper plane. The General Dynamics Corporation will join with Korea's Samsung Group to develop a plane based on the single-engine F-16. The F-16, used widely in combat in the gulf war, costs just $18.4 million versus $30.8 million for an F-18A. The F-16 flew 30,000 sorties, easily defeating the best Iraqi arms -- the same ones the North Koreans use.
 
The German Export Giant
 
Since the gulf war ended, the dollar has been rising. But not enough to offset Germany's new lead: it is now the No. 1 exporting nation, leaving the United States in an unfamiliar No. 2 position. Germany exported $421 billion worth of goods in 1990 versus $394.5 billion for the United States. The reason for Germany's ascendency is its currency. The mark is still at historic highs. "The mark may be wiggling around the trend line these last two weeks, but that doesn't offset the last six years," said Carl Weinberg, chief economist at High Frequency Economics in New York. "The dollar is still depreciated." The strength of the mark raised the value of Germany's exports and broadened its financial position. But in one area, at least, the United States is still No. 1. "The U.S. imported $494.9 billion worth of goods in 1990, about $100 billion more than it exports," No other country comes even close.
 
In Brief
 
The Commerce Department is borrowing a strategy from Japan's Ministry of International Trade and Industry. It has selected 20 American companies to promote in Japan. Timkin, Square D and Dana are among the few. Commerce Secretary Robert A. Mosbacher will take the heads of these companies to Japan and personally escort them around.
 
 
 
THE ECONOMY

 
Congress Versus Brady
 
United States Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady was upbeat last week. He very nearly promised that no taxpayer-funded bailout of the banks would be needed, unless the nation's economy performed much worse than expected. There was one catch: Congress had to approve his proposed overhaul of the banking system for the nation's banking woes to really wither. The proposals would allow banks to open branches nationwide and let them merge with investment banks and brokerage houses. The intended changes would also give industrial companies the right to buy banks -- the most controversial issue. Mr. Brady said he did not think his proposals would sail through Congress unchanged, but he felt all of them would be approved. Not so, argue many in Congress, who are rolling up their sleeves for a fight.
 
Broke Consumers Want to Spend
 
February was a better month for the mechanic than it was for the manufacturer, but just barely so. Personal income was up by a slim 0.3 percent, but sales of durable, big-ticket items like washing machines were down by 0.3 percent, following an even bigger drop of 1.5 percent in January, according to the Commerce Department. "The fall was really 0.4 percent, if you exclude defense spending," said Robert Ortner, former chief economist at the Commerce Department. "Consumers would love to spend. All the indicators say that." They just don't have the money.
 
Job Security, the Big Worry
 
The economy may still be in a recession, but no one seems too gloomy about it. The Conference Board's overall index of consumer confidence for March registered its biggest jump ever. The index, derived from a survey of 5,000 heads of American households about their views of the present and the future, has been a reliable indicator of recessions and recoveries. But the silver lining in the index's findings comes with a cloud. The one area of negative sentiment revealed in the survey is job prospects. "People still have enormous concern about job security," said Fabian Linden, the survey's director. The index may be picking up end-of-the-war euphoria, rather than actual consumer confidence.
 
Is Recovery a Sure Thing?
 
Was it the beginning of an upward trend, or a dead cat's bounce? That's what economists wondered when the leading indicators index registered a 1.1 percent increase for February, the first increase since June. The index includes stock prices, consumer confidence, building permits, and money supply. "It was a sizable increase. It suggests a recovery in 1991," said Allen Sinai, chief economist of the Boston Company. With sharp increases in new and existing homes sales, some ask whether the recession is ending. "It will probably happen sometime between the end of spring and Labor Day. The index leads the economy by three to seven months." Mr. Sinai said. What still eludes economists is how strong the recovery will be. "That, the numbers do not show."

HEADLINE: Wall Street;
A Better Way to Track Your Assets

BYLINE: By Diana B. Henriques

 BODY:
A leading Japanese insurance company and one of America's foremost pension consulting firms last week unveiled a new computerized asset-allocation model that is many times more powerful and responsive than the ones currently in use on Wall Street. The development could reshape the way in which trillions of dollars of financial assets are managed.
The new financial software system was designed for the Yasuda Fire and Marine Company, the second-largest property and casualty insurer in Japan, by the Frank Russell Company in Tacoma, Wash. 
 Instead of the opaque jargon that usually attends such feats, this occasion was marked by unabashed superlatives. The Yasuda-Russell model "is the largest known asset-allocation model in existence for a major corporation," said William T. Ziemba, a finance professor at the University of British Columbia and a consultant to the project. "It uses the latest academic theory and fully considers the constraints and uncertainties of the company to achieve their long-run goals."
The reason that asset allocation is so important can be found in the oldest, and still the soundest, rule on Wall Street: "Don't put all your eggs in one basket." Simple as that sounds, figuring out the optimal number of eggs to put in each of the available baskets -- stocks, bonds, cash, real estate, commodities, baseball cards -- is extremely difficult.
The first breakthrough came with the pioneering work done three decades ago by Harry M. Markowitz, who was awarded the Nobel Prize for his efforts last fall. His theories laid the groundwork for the subsequent development of various computer models that money-managers use today to calculate the best mixture of assets for the unfolding economic environment.
Anything that could dramatically alter the way giant investors make their egg-sorting decisions is big news for money managers, for investors and for the markets. And that news broke quietly in Japan as Yasuda introduced the remarkable results of its ambitious three-year campaign to find a better way to manage its assets.
Current allocation models can test for about a dozen different economic variables, at most. The new Yasuda-Russell model can test for thousands of business, economic and regulatory variables, so that each user can create a computer microcosm that closely reflects its own unique environment and goals. Yasuda is betting this will make its investment decisions more accurate and, thus, more profitable.
Andrew Turner, senior vice president and director of research at Russell, sees the new model as a vast improvement over today's tools. "The standard framework, while simple to use, allows for only a crude approximation of the real economic problems," Mr. Turner said. The Yasuda-Russell version, by contrast, creates a world within the computer that is much more finely textured.
In addition, while current models can test the performance of a particular mix of assets through a single period of time -- one quarter or one year into the future, for example -- the new one is a "multiperiod" model. That means it can calculate performance over the next year and over the next five years, seeking a blend that is ideal for both time spans. And all this is accomplished in a few hours on the RS-6000 work station made by International Business Machines.
Yasuro Ohtani, the deputy manager of financial planning for Yasuda Kasai in Tokyo, said in a telephone interview that his company commissioned the new model nearly three years ago because it was dissatisfied with the limitations of what he called "Harry Markowitz-type models." Those models, for example, cannot allow for complicated regulatory constraints imposed by the Ministry of Finance. Nor can they cope with illiquid assets, like loans and real estate, in the company's $24 billion portfolio.
"Our investment activities are very complicated," Mr. Ohtani said, "so we felt we should make a more advanced model."
Mr. Turner, who headed the design team, stopped short of declaring current asset-allocation models obsolete. "Harry Markowitz is a tremendously brilliant man, and his work was a wonderful insight at the time," he said. "But we've reached a point, both in optimization theory and in our ability to compute solutions, where we no longer need to restrict ourselves to those simple and elegant solutions. We can say, 'I'm going to make this really messy,' and throw in all the real-world dirt that really exists and then slog through it to a solution."
The advent of this new tool comes as asset allocation strategies in general are in poor repute on Wall Street. The "tactical asset allocators," those money-managers who rely exclusively on computer modeling to guide their investments, have not performed well in the past year and a half. Some allocators have become frustrated and ignored their model's advice in favor of more intuitive decisions; others have stood firm and lost clients.
Mr. Turner suggested that the blame may lie more with the tools than with the theory. While many money-managers may not need a model as richly detailed as the Yasuda-Russell version, he predicted that some features of the new model could probably be applied to make less sophisticated models more accurate and effective.
George F. Russell Jr., chairman of the Frank Russell Company, has observed the investment scene for decades. He believes widespread use of the new model, which can be tailored to reflect the goals of each institution that uses it, could have a sweeping effect on the way the market behaves.
"Now we're using yesterday's technology; this is tomorrow's technology," Mr. Russell said. "Organizations today have to use a very limited scale of choices; this allows them a very broad choice scale. So we'll have a lot more diversity than we have now -- and that means the market becomes more free, which ought to excite all of us."
