HEADLINE: World Markets;
Dollar's Gains Upset the Exchanges

BYLINE: By Jonathan Fuerbringer

 BODY:
Oh, how the dollar has hurt Americans with investments abroad this year. And with so little warning.
The gains they realized in Europe, as markets rallied after the Persian Gulf victory, were cut in half or even more by the end of the first quarter, according to the Financial Times index of world markets. But until seven weeks ago they hadn't a worry about losing money when converting profits into dollars. 
 The dollar hit its post-World War II lows against the German mark and Swiss franc and its 1991 lows against other major currencies in mid-February. But it has since jumped 17.3 percent against the mark, 15.9 percent again the lira, 16.6 percent against the French franc and 14.6 percent against the British pound.
The gains of American investors are now being gobbled up by unfavorable exchange rates. The 28.5 percent gain in the Swedish market is cut to 17.2 percent when profits are converted into dollars. In Spain, the 26.5 percent rise is reduced to 14.2 percent. In Italy, the 12.6 percent rise is reduced to 0.66 percent. In Britain, the 15.4 percent jump is slashed to 4.6 percent, while in Germany, the 7.7 percent spurt falls to a 5.2 percent loss.
Despite the dollar's move, some investment managers are not making major changes in their portfolios. Some of them had already expected the dollar to rise moderately this year -- even to its current levels -- but just not this quickly.
And while there will be a loss on exchange when profits are brought back home, the higher dollar will be a benefit for many European companies that either export to the United states or have large operations here. The stronger dollar will make European exports more competitive here and the dollar earnings in the United States will grow when translated back into marks, pounds, francs, lire and guilders.
"What we have been doing is placing emphasis on companies that are more export- oriented," said Sander Cseh of Cseh International in Philadelphia. For example, he likes Hunter Douglas of the Netherlands, a manufacturer of blinds and other window coverings with major business in the United States. But he has not "bet the ranch" on these prospects because he is not sure of how high and how long the dollar will rally.
Some forecasters predict that it will rise to 1.80 against the mark by the end of the year, 6 percent above where it is now. Some say it will drift even higher.
Nicholas Sargen, a director of international bond trading at Salomon Brothers in New York, thinks the dollar might peak at 1.80 marks, but is likely to fall below that level before the end of the year. He is not expecting a higher rise in part because of the impact of capital flows. For the dollar to continue to rise this year, he says, a significant amount of foreign capital would have to flow into the United States. He does not anticipate that happening.
Traditionally, interest rates have been a key determinant of exchange rates. A country with higher interest rates than another usually has a stronger currency, other factors being equal, because the higher return attracts investors.
But the movement of capital from country to country has become a much bigger factor,and can often overwhelm the interest rate factor.
New data for the fourth quarter of 1990, Mr. Sargen said, showed that there was a net inflow of foreign capital into Germany of $18.7 billion. This surge, he said, helps explain the jump in the value of the mark near the end of the year, when it reached new highs against the dollar. The $18.7 billion inflow was even greater than the $16.7 billion in the fourth quarter of 1989, when money was flowing to Germany on the euphoric wave from the fall of the Berlin Wall and the opening of Eastern Europe.
Although he does not yet have the data, Mr. Sargen now suspects that the flow of capital, mostly into German bonds, continued in the first part of this year, which helped bring the mark to its post-World War II record high against the dollar in mid-February.
But he said those investors have been sent scurrying by the renewed focus on German political and economic problems since the end of the Persian Gulf war and the diminished fears that there would be a devaluation in the European Monetary System. That capital drain helped bring down the mark -- and push the dollar up -- quicky, adding to the momentum from the interest rate changes also now expected to favor the United States.
The impact of capital flows to the United States can also explain the fall of the dollar in 1990. The total flow of capital shifted to a net outflow of $27 billion, compared with a net inflow of $88 billion in 1989.
Mr. Sargen does expect there to be a reversal in 1991. But he does not think the United States bond market will be attractive enough to bring back the flows of capital that marked 1988 and 1989. "My story," he said, "is that the pendulum of captial flows that hurt the dollar last year will start to shift back. But there is no way we will get back to the good old days of the late 1980's."

LENGTH: 2923 words

HEADLINE: Advertising's Antic Upstarts

BYLINE: By RANDALL ROTHENBERG

 BODY:
A lunchtime crowd of some 40 Italian silk ties and 40 short black skirts was munching on tortellini at the Art Directors Club in New York one recent afternoon when the least fashionable of their number, a lanky Texan in a blue blazer, gray slacks and white shirt, shuffled up to the microphone and held them rapt by condemning their chosen profession, advertising.
The problem with advertising, said Gary Goldsmith, is not the deep recession that is reducing ad spending and forcing copywriters into the street. The real problem, said the 36-year-old chairman and creative director of the Goldsmith/Jeffrey agency, is the 13,000 commercials forced on the average American television viewer each year -- most of them stultifyingly boring. 
 He played a videotape reel of his favorite spots. Perhaps predictably, several were from Goldsmith/Jeffrey, including an emotional advertisement for El Al Airlines that featured old newsreel footage of Israel's pioneer settlers and a commercial for Everlast sportswear that showed only a ticking stopwatch.
A group of young advertising students looked on adoringly. "Not to lose that spark -- these kinds of agencies will give you that opportunity," said Joanna L. Davanzo, a 20-year-old junior at the Fashion Institute of Technology.
Which agencies? she was asked. She and her friends thought a moment, and then spewed forth the names: Goldsmith/Jeffrey Inc.; Deutsch Inc.; Omon, New York; Kirshenbaum & Bond, and Buckley DeCerchio Cavalier Inc.
To the general public, their names are unknown. But to young copywriters and art directors, and to a small but growing list of marketers who believe only risky advertising will pierce consumers' torpor, these off-off Madison Avenue agencies are contemporary advertising's hottest shops.
 
Bred on Television
 Their leaders share many traits, notably an antic youthfulness. They are the first generation of agency founders bred entirely on television. But in their commitment to their craft and their fervent belief in themselves and their work, they represent the advertising industry's longstanding ability to renew itself even in the darkest times.
And gloom indeed pervades Madison Avenue. With the advertising slump in its third year, some believe the financial pressure on agencies and clients has led to a plethora of safe but lackluster ads. Last week, for the first time, Advertising Age magazine even declined to name an agency of the year, citing a lack of enthusiasm for any one shop.
But out on the street, the hot shops are generating enthusiasm, certainly among young "creatives" -- the industry's copywriters and art directors -- who consider them colorful intruders in the land of the bland. While their work has been criticized as over indulgent, even sophomoric, by advertising traditionalists, their insouciance and innate savviness about the media are already being echoed in the ads produced by some larger and older agencies. Wacky humor is in, say the upstarts, and boring product descriptions are out. Dare to be offensive, they declare, or risk losing your viewers and readers.
The five agencies, all in New York, repeatedly turn up as the hippest shops in informal surveys of client consultants, executive recruiters and industry creatives. They are to the early 1990's what agencies like Chiat/Day/ Mojo and Hal Riney & Partners were to the 80's -- ingenues on the verge of stardom.
For decades, upstart agencies have tried to establish themselves by their creative product, hoping against hope that one tiny client might turn into a national marketing behemoth and take the agency up the mountain with it. Leo Burnett, after all, was little more than a copywriter who favored big black pencils and owned a small Chicago agency until he created the Marlboro Man in 1954. Today, the Leo Burnett Company is the world's 14th-largest agency.
"If you look at advertising history, most of the shops that are the big guys today started up as small shops in the 40's and 50's, where the owners of the agency were also the business strategists and the copywriters," said David E. R. Dangoor, the head of marketing at Philip Morris U.S.A., makers of Marlboro. "That gave birth to new ideas and a lot of gutsy marketing moves."
There is no reason that history should not repeat itself, added Mr. Dangoor, who has retained Omon to do advertising for his company. "Everybody is so fed up with advertising," he said. "Is there a new way to advertise? These small agencies can be a source of stimulation for us to find out."
 
Some Distinctions
 In the style of their lives and work, today's hot shops differ in ways large and subtle.
The Deutsch agency, at 105 employees and $100 million in annual billings the largest of the group, is not a new shop at all, but a 22-year-old agency consciously reformulated by its second-generation management as an avatar of hip advertising. Its television commercials for the Ikea furniture stores, with their candid interviews with conventional salespeople, reek of David Letterman's sharp sarcasm. But the agency also has by far the largest media-buying and research departments of any shop on the hot list, and unlike the other agencies, most of whose work is in print advertising, some three-quarters of Deutsch's billings derive from television advertising.
Omon, by contrast, the New York branch of a small Australian agency, is the smallest and newest shop among the group. Its 14 employees toil in a Soho loft so tiny there's barely room for serifs on the type in its print ads. But Omon, which was established here in 1989, shook the ad industry quickly, first by bringing in as its president Joanne M. Davis, a respected rainmaker from the large Wells, Rich, Greene agency, and then by landing business from two enormous and prestigious clients: Philip Morris and Toyota, the latter of which spurned its giant agency of record, Saatchi & Saatchi, for the new kid.
Kirshenbaum & Bond, like the old Mad magazine, offers humor in a jugular vein, having exploited Dan Quayle and Imelda Marcos in its ads for Kenneth Cole shoes. At times, the advertising done by the three-and-a-half-year-old agency has seemed calculated to offend, as in its print campaign for Saint Laurie, a discount clothing store in New York, that featured a handsome male model staring off into space, over the headline, "Dress British. Think Yiddish."
Jonathan Bond, the boyish, 33-year-old co-founder, says such controversial advertising employs a "multiplier effect" on behalf of clients, generating talk and publicity that can be more valuable and vastly less expensive than conventional advertising. Serious clients have bought the premise; in the past few months, Schieffelin & Somerset has given Kirshenbaum responsibility for its Hennessy cognac and Moet & Chandon champagne advertising, the Savin Corporation has brought over its office copier account and Chase Manhattan Bank has turned to the agency to help it market credit cards to college students, bringing the agency's billings to $45 million.
Goldsmith/Jeffrey is capable of work so spare one is tempted to call it anti-advertising. Its television ads for the Nynex Business-to-Business Yellow Pages rarely show anything more than the telephone book against a stark white background. Such work has given the agency a reputation as the most stylish and marketing-savvy of the new shops, and has contributed to its growth to $30 million in billings after only four years.
 
Pizza and Bon Jovi
 Finally, there is Buckley DeCerchio Cavalier, which, in attitude as well as advertising, seems more freewheeling than its hot-list competitors. It goes for the blunt, comic headline. "Take a job where the only person you have to listen to is Bon Jovi," it says in a delivery-driver recruitment ad for the Godfather's Pizza chain.
Indeed, in virtually everything they do, the agency's leaders go for blunt comedy. "Look," said Thomas L. DeCerchio, the copy writing partner, as he dragged a visitor to the large windows of the Soho loft his agency shares with an architecture firm and pointed west. "We're just a Lee Harvey Oswald shot from Kirshenbaum & Bond."
But Buckley, which was founded in 1988, is no joke to the several prestigious clients it has dragged in, among them Yugo automobiles, Discover magazine and Snapple soft drinks. Winning Snapple, which has grown from a $1 million to a $5 million account in only one year, convinced the agency's principals to move into their current office space, said Jane Cavalier, the agency's president. Until then, they had worked out of Mr. DeCerchio's studio apartment.
Still, for all their differences, these agencies are fundamentally the same. First and foremost, they are bucking the worst advertising recession in 20 years.
Equally important, they are young, in spirit as well as in years. Asked why they went into advertising, both Ms. Cavalier, who is 30 years old, and Paul Bernasconi, Omon's 25-year-old co-creative director, gave the same immediate answer: "Bewitched," the 1960's television show whose co-protagonist, Darrin Stevens, was a befuddled ad executive married to a sorceress.
Perhaps most significantly, in an industry that is depressed both economically and emotionally, these are the agencies at which young people want to work. At most ad agencies today, job applications are as scarce as job openings, so fearful are people of leaving their nests in tough times. One mid-level executive at the large Ayer agency said that he used to receive 200 resumes and portfolios a year. In recent months, only three have crossed his desk.
But the new hot shops are awash with portfolios, sleek and black and filled with ads done for more established agencies, from young job seekers. Frank Todaro, a 27-year-old art director, left a secure position at Scali, McCabe, Sloves, where he was one of the youngest vice presidents in its history, to become creative director at Buckley DeCerchio Cavalier. "I saw what everybody else did -- that the places that had once been terrific were flattening out," he said. "And I also saw that places like this and Goldsmith were the new Scalis."
 
No Guarantees
 Of course, there are no guarantees that any one of the five will survive, let alone prosper. Advertising history is filled with cautionary tales. Just two years ago, Doyle Graf Raj, a highly regarded agency that did memorable print ads for Steuben glass and Haagen-Dazs ice cream, died, overburdened with expenses.
So too, veterans of advertising's "creative revolution" of the 60's find much of the work from contemporary agencies to be forced.
"Today, the fun seems to be manufactured somewhat," said Frank DiGiacomo, who worked as a copywriter at one of the more manic 60's agencies, Della Femina, Travisano & Partners, and who now produces commercials. "It's, 'Oh, look at us, aren't we wild and crazy,' or 'Our work is irreverent, bordering on distasteful.' It's shock advertising, and that's not how I was brought up in this business."
Donny Deutsch, the 33-year-old creative director and current guiding spirit of the Deutsch agency, was criticized for Pontiac car advertising that some deemed racist because it invoked fears about Japanese purchases of American companies. Mr. Deutsch, whose father, David, founded the agency, shrugged off the complaints: "Anytime you do work on the edge, that's going to happen."
The trade-off for the risk of criticism, he and others said, is the ability to keep creative staffs happy by allowing them the opportunity to do "great work," an industry euphemism for ads that get noticed.
"What drives any creative talent is someone recognizing the work they're doing," said Malcolm D. MacDougall, who wrote "Come to think of it, I'll have a Heineken" and other legendary slogans while at larger agencies, and who now runs a small creative consultancy. "Nobody is satisfied doing a talking head about aspirin. That's the way it has always been and that's the way it's always going to be."
Hank Perlman, a 24-year-old writer who left Deutsch for Buckley, underscored the point when he was asked why he became a copywriter. "It's a selfish thing," he said. "I wanted to say, 'I did this. Isn't it funny?' "
Recognizable advertising has other benefits, of course. It can brand an agency's identity into the minds of potential clients, peers in the business and advertising trade publications, generating a cycle of talk that often translates into new business. "So much American advertising is a wasteland," said Mr. Bernasconi of Omon. "But the ads are our product. As in any manufacturing business, if your product is substandard, you won't get the business."
 
Can They Grow?
 Even if they succeed at reeling in and satisfying larger marketers, one threatening question still hangs over advertising's hot shops: Can the small brands they advertise turn into multimillion-dollar advertisers that will stoke their prosperity, the way Marlboro did for Leo Burnett?
Mr. Dangoor of Philip Morris conceded the prospects are a bit dimmer than in the past, but maintained that in any industry, "you never know when the dynamics will change again" and lead to explosive growth in unexpected product categories.
Indeed, all five hot shops have had surprises that indicate that growth is still possible. Goldsmith/Jeffrey's Everlast campaign, for example, attracted the attention of Riddell, the nation's major manufacturer of football helmets. Riddell then did a deal with USA Classic, Everlast's licensor, to market branded active wear to the general public. Goldsmith, of course, got the account.
Like their professional forebears, the David Ogilvys and Bill Bernbachs who believed as fervently in their own destiny as they did in the power of advertising, the young executives running the new hot agencies refuse even to entertain the notion that they might not, someday, be giants.
"People say to me all the time, 'What a chance you guys are taking, it's so risky,' " said Gary Goldsmith. "I always say the alternative is riskier -- sitting in a big agency for 20 years, producing work you don't like, and turning around and finding out it didn't count for anything."
 
 
HAVENS FOR 'EDGY' CLIENTS
 To do good work -- Donnie Deutsch of the Deutsch agency calls it "edgy advertising" -- means finding clients who will take risks. For small agencies trying to build a reputation, this usually means retailers and other small companies that are not overburdened with bureaucracies, but run tightly by their founders or chief executives. Other types of clients are often not welcome.
"When you speak to the DeCerchios and Goldsmiths, people come to them and they will turn business away," said Sandy Berger, a top agency recruiter.
They will even fire clients. Gary Goldsmith of Goldsmith/Jeffrey, for example, proudly recalls that the Mettler Instrument Corporation, a Swiss manufacturer of weighing and balancing equipment, was "the first one we blew out the door." The problem began when Mettler hired a design consultant who decided to standardize the look of the company's communications, and demanded, among other things, that all type be in alternating blue and green letters.
"Gary and I felt there was no way we could keep the business and stay within our mission," said Bob Jeffrey, 37, the agency's president.
Last year, Hans Gut, Mettler's president, said in an interview that the company had to follow specific design guidelines. But he said of Goldsmith/ Jeffrey, "We admire them. They didn't want to compromise."
Because small, entrepreneurial clients are often equally quick to fire their agencies when business turns sour, all five of the hot shops have made conscious attempts to find larger, and more stable, national marketers willing to take a chance on "edgy" campaigns.
Even with right-thinking clients, agency life is not easy.
"There's a myth that when you have a small agency and small clients you go into work every day, put a great idea down on paper, show it to the client, they jump up and down and say, 'That's it!' and write a check," Mr. Goldsmith said recently in a talk at the Art Directors Club in New York. "Any time you present a great idea, there's initial resistance. You have to fight and back up your work with logic to sell it."

 
BAD TIMES? NOT FOR BABIES
 After decades of double-digit growth, much of it fueled by wild increases in the cost of advertising time and space, ad spending is expected to grow only 4.5 percent this year, less than the rate of inflation, according to Robert Coen, director of forecasting at McCann-Erickson.
Some of the largest national advertisers, like Eastern Airlines, have gone out of business; others, like Chrysler and General Motors, are reportedly slashing spending, and still others, like Pepsi-Cola, are expected only to maintain spending at 1990 levels.
As a result, America's largest ad agencies, among them Backer Spielvogel Bates, D'Arcy Masius Benton & Bowles and Young & Rubicam, have let go dozens of employees each.
Advertising's new hot shops "are almost the only ones that are growing right now," says Leslie Winthrop, president of the Advertising Agency Register, a consulting firm that helps marketers find ad agencies.
"Omon got up to $19 million in billings in only a few months," Ms. Winthrop added. "Kirshenbaum & Bond added $15 million in billings in December and January. At Buckley DeCerchio Cavalier, they're babies, but they're still hovering at $20 million."

HEADLINE: MARKET WATCH;
Unease About Rate Parity and the New Dollar

BYLINE: By Diana B. Henriques

 BODY:
You can listen to what Wall Street says -- or you can watch what it does.
The talk on the Street these days is that investors are going to keep buying stocks because they are focusing on the prosperous post-recession paradise that will eventually arrive, courtesy of the Federal Reserve Board's stimulative efforts. Thus, the Dow has continued its climb, closing the pre-holiday week on Thursday at 2,913.86, about 55 points higher than the prior week's close. 
 That's what Wall Street is saying. But how does the market behave when International Business Machines or Reynolds Metals or Halliburton reports disappointing earnings in the here and now? Panic stations, everyone.
Well, brace yourself. The surprises may have just begun.
As companies are reporting their profits for 1990, they also are closing the books on the first quarter of fiscal 1991 -- a period whose assorted plagues approached Biblical severity. War, swift and astonishingly destructive. Oily smoke polluting the desert skies. A stubborn drought in the Western United States. Upheaval in the Soviet Union. Business failures, here and abroad.
But the most recent affliction is easy to overlook because it is such a boon for tourists planning their summer getaway: the revivified dollar. When the dollar soars, companies that do a lot of business abroad feel the pinch. Widgets sold for marks, francs or yen translate into fewer dollars on the bottom line when the sales slips are sent home. Dollar-based investments look more dear to overseas investors. And exports are more expensive for foreign buyers.
Do earnings really matter? Consider the issue of interest rate parity, a classic measurement of the stock market's relative value.
It's an arcane little formula: You divide the price-earnings ratio of the Standard & Poor's 500 by the inverse of the yield on triple-A corporate bonds, an alternative investment.
In the past, when the resulting percentage has exceeded 15, the market has later taken a sharp tumble, according to Richard Pucci, of the Institutional Brokers Estimate System, published by Lynch, Jones & Ryan Inc., a New York-based brokerage firm. (It exceeded 40 percent in June 1987, before the October crash.)
These days? It's more than 20 percent, as of mid-March. "This is dangerous territory," said Mr. Pucci.
Of course, the equilibrium could be restored if bond yields fell further, which might happen if inflation stays low enough and the Fed is so inclined. Or earnings could climb, which is what Wall Street is predicting. But many analysts expect future gains to be led by certain sectors, including consumer durable goods and basic industries, where credit quality is sharply deteriorating, as measured by Lynch, Jones' bond-market monitoring service. If those sectors disappoint the Street in the months ahead, investors might remember that the third way equilibrium could be restored in the interest rate parity equation is for stock prices to decline.

HEADLINE: Mutual Funds;
Rebound in Financial Services

BYLINE: By Carole Gould

 BODY:
The stock market has begun the year with a bang, with the average stock fund gaining 12.8 percent. Overall, the market has been driven by the small-company group, which had risen 21.2 percent through March 21, according to Lipper Analytical Services of Summit, N.J.
Among specialty funds, those in health and biotechnology (up 25.9 percent) and in science and technology (up 22 percent) have kept their place as leaders. But the quarter has held a real surprise: the financial services funds-- which invest mainly in banks, savings and loans, brokerages, money managers and insurance companies -- have gained 19.4 percent, trailing the leaders by only a small margin. 
 This contrasts sharply with last year's performance, when the nine funds in the financial services group dropped an average of 14.9 percent, trailing the average stock fund by more than seven percentage points. "It's a terrific turnaround," said Stephen A. Schoepke, a Lipper analyst.
The group is largely driven by the bank stocks, which were pounded unmercifully at the end of last year. Then the Federal Reserve began easing interest rates, and market analysts' views of the stocks shifted radically. "The bank stocks went from certain death to possible survival," causing share prices to surge, said David Ellison, manager of the $9.6 million Fidelity Select Savings and Loan, the quarter's leader, which rose 26 percent.
The managers say changes in the financial services industry are beginning to pay off. But they caution that the road to health will be long and bumpy. Much of the first-quarter jump came from a "shift in psychology," said Karen L. Finkel, who manages the $42 million Paine Webber Classic Regional Financial Fund. "But you can't improve asset quality and work out bad loans overnight."
 The group's current bad reputation obscures some good stocks, the managers say. And share prices have dropped so low that investors can pick up some bargains.
Mr. Ellison, who also manages the $30.6 million Fidelity Select Financial Services Fund, says the banking industry offers some "rare opportunities." He points to NS Bancorp of Chicago, a recent public offering at $8 that he expects to earn $2 a share this year. He says the bank has a solid loan portfolio and is well capitalized. Another of his picks is Standard Federal Bank in Troy, Mich.
On the other hand, Philip Dubuque, manager of the $6 million Financial Strategic Financial Services Fund in Denver, has taken profits from his bank stocks in the last few weeks and winnowed his bank holdings from more than half of the fund to roughly 20 percent.
In the process, he has built the brokerage portion from 2 percent in December to about 25 percent today. "Volume on the New York Stock Exchange is running 20 percent higher than last year, and when business expenses are down, heavy trading translates into nearly pure profit," he said. Mr. Dubuque notes that some brokerages have reduced their payrolls by as much as 30 percent since the precrash peak in 1987, and overhead has fallen dramatically. Among brokers with strong retail businesses, he likes Paine Webber, Merrill Lynch and A. G. Edwards, the St. Louis-based regional brokerage.
Mr. Dubuque has also bought shares of Countrywide Credit Industries, a mortgage broker based in Pasadena, Calif. The company is positioned to benefit as consumer confidence rises and the mortgage market begins to recover, he says.
Ms. Finkel at Paine Webber has about three-quarters of her fund in regional banks and thrift institutions. She says these concerns are helped by the consolidation among banks and regulatory measures that let banks expand geographically. Her favorite is Keycorp of Albany, which does business in New York, in Maine and in six states in the Northwest. She points out that the company works mainly with small borrowers and depositors, lessening the risk of a large default.
Scott Offen, who manages the $6.8 million Fidelity Select Brokerage Fund, says many brokerage firms have upgraded their internal financial controls and brought fixed expenses under control in recent years, positioning them perfectly for the current stock market rally. The firms' restructuring means the stocks "won't lose money in bad markets and can make tremendous amounts in good markets," he said. His largest holding is Merrill Lynch. He also likes Primerica, the New York City-based financial services company that owns Smith Barney; Commercial Credit, a consumer finance company, and A. L. Williams, a life insurance company.
James K. Schmidt, who manages the $47 million Freedom Regional Bank Fund in Boston, invests mainly in small to mid-size regional banks, mostly in the Midwest. He says that stock prices are cheap there and that the banks are largely untouched by problem real estate loans like those afflicting Northeast banks. Two of his favorites are the Mercantile Bancorporation in St. Louis and the First of America Bank Corporation, based in Kalamazoo, Mich.
Ten percent of the fund is invested in savings and loan associations. One favorite is Pioneer Federal Bancorp in Honolulu, whose problem loans Mr. Schmidt says represent less than 1 percent of its total.

HEADLINE: All About/Making Recycling Pay;
In Solid Waste, It's the Breakdown That Counts

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA

DATELINE: KING OF PRUSSIA, Penn.

 BODY:
The first thing everyone needs to know about recycling, said Charles F. Oyler, is the difference between trash and recyclable materials. Mr. Oyler is the recyling manager at the big materials recovery center in suburban Philadelphia. The center is operated by Browning-Ferris Inc.
A material recovery facility is a key element in the recycling chain. Materials that have been discarded are brought in to be separated, decontaminated and compressed into bales for shipment to mills that can make them into new products. 
 The factory processes about 125 tons a day of paper and cardboard, steel and aluminum cans, glass and plastic bottles. All have been separated from outgoing solid waste to preserve their value. "There is no practical way to process completely mixed materials," Mr. Oyler said. "Once something is mixed with trash, it becomes trash."
Homeowners are asked to place their newspapers and glass, aluminum, steel and plastic in a container separate from other trash. Similarly, offices participating in the state-mandated recycling program are asked to collect high-grade papers and other recyclables away from other wastes. On collection day, the trash goes to a landfill; the recyclables go to Browning-Ferris.
According to William D. Ruckelshaus, the chairman of Browning-Ferris, one of the largest trash haulers in the country, solid-waste recycling is now at a crucial point in terms of its long-term viability. The public enthusiastically supports the concept at the moment, and that support is reflected in laws intended to prod industries to use more recycled materials.
That increase in demand is prompting an increase in collections and the development of processing plants like the one in King of Prussia. "If the infrastructure gets put into place, with collection systems, processing centers and end-user markets, then it will not matter if the current 'feel good' attitude subsides," Mr. Ruckelshaus said. "Economics will take over and the system will be self-sustaining."

 
 
The Metal Sideshow
Efficient Aluminum, Tin-Tainted Steel
 One self-sustaining part of the recycling business is aluminum. Since remelting aluminum takes only 5 percent of the electric power to extract metal from bauxite ore, aluminum companies have been recycling for more than 20 years.
Aluminum is the most valuable material in the solid waste stream. A ton of crushed, baled cans commands $1,000 to $1,100 from mills. In contrast, similar loads of old cardboard boxes are worth $25 to $90, according to figures compiled by Recycling Times, a publication of the National Solid Waste Management Association. Glass, separated by color, is valued at from $40 to $60 a ton.
According to the Aluminum Association, a trade group, 54.9 billion aluminum cans totalling 9.65 million tons were recycled in this country last year. That represented a recycling rate of 63.6 percent, the group said, up from 60.8 percent in 1989.
Steel has been recycled for generations. Indeed, the entire steel mini-mill industry is based on scrap, largely recovered from old cars and appliances.
But steel cans used to package food products have a thin coating of tin on the inside to prevent the formation of rust, and the tin was viewed as a contaminant because it caused blemishes on the surface of sheet steel. The drive to reduce solid waste in landfills has increased steel-can recycling.
The amount of tin on cans has declined from about 20 pounds per ton of steel 20 years ago to 4 1/2 to 5 pounds today, said William Heenan of the Steel Can Recycling Institute, another trade group. As a result, it can be added directly to steel furnaces in limited amounts without causing problems. He said steel-can recycling has risen from 5 billion cans in 1988 to an estimated 9 billion last year. It is now priced at $40 to $70 a ton, depending on location.

 
 
The Main Attraction
Paper's Many Manifestations
 All of these materials are sideshows, though, to paper and paperboard products. According to the Environmental Protection Agency, paper-based products, which include cardboard boxes, newspapers, computer printouts and tissue products, constitute the largest single element of solid waste -- about 40 percent.
Clean paper products can be recycled, within limits. Cardboard boxes can become new boxes, while some mixed grades of paper, including brown shopping bags, can be reformed as the fluted middle layer inside cardboard. Old newspapers can be made into new newsprint and the paperboard used for boxes of dry cereal.
One of the problems with the early recycling programs of the late 1980's was a glut of old newspapers that depressed the price to the point where communities had to pay to have them hauled away. To an extent, the glut continues; old newspapers, magazines and brown bags can cost $25 a ton to dispose of on the East Coast.
But those economics can be changed by adding value. "We upgraded our news by pulling out the bags and the slick magazines," Mr. Oyler said. "We never had to pay to move it."
Nevertheless, Browning-Ferris officials say that, with the exception of aluminum, the prices now being paid for recycled materials would not pay the costs of collection and processing. But when the savings of $70 a ton in landfilling fees that do not have to be paid are added to the equation, it becomes a viable business. Mr. Ruckelshaus observed that recycling may not make economic sense in states like Texas, where disposal fees are as low as $4 a ton.

 
 
A Picky Process
The Importance of Quality Garbage
 Upgrading the quality of materials coming into the garbage recycling plant involves a combination of machine separation and handpicking. Most of the people doing the handpicking are paid slightly above the minimum wage. Incoming containers are dumped on a conveyor which passes under a rotating magnet that pulls out all the steel cans. The rest of the material drops onto a vibrating screen that has slots three-quarters of an inch wide, to remove small pieces of broken glass.
The material is then fed into an air blower that separates the lighter aluminum and plastic from the remaining glass. The aluminum cans are flattened and blown by air into a truck while the plastic bottles are collected for later baling.
The glass containers drop onto another conveyor where workers separate them by color. Clear glass is generally the most valuable while green, which is used mostly in beer bottles, varies the most widely in price. It is now worth as little as $10 a ton in the northeastern states but fetches as much as $60 on the West Coast.
The quality of incoming material is important, and Browning-Ferris officials say they try to conduct an education campaign before starting curbside or commercial collection programs. Things like telephone books, gummed labels and air freight envelopes have no place in containers intended to collect office papers.
"We take two pictures of loads as they come in and we give one to the driver," Mr. Oyler said. If a shipment is contaminated, it is accepted the first time, but someone from the company warns the shipper. "The second time we charge them for disposal."

 
 
AN INDUSTRY TRIES TO IMPROVE ITS RECORD ON PLASTIC
 Plastic can be recycled as easily as most materials. It can be collected, washed, chopped and reformed under heat and pressure into new products.
But unlike aluminum cans, plastic containers are made from a variety of different resins which often do not perform well when mixed together. And because they are light and take up a lot of space, they are expensive to collect and process for recycling.
A bale of steel cans, for example, weighs about 3,000 pounds. A similar bale of paper weights about 1,800 pounds, but a bale of plastic bottles, which take up the same amount of room on a truck, weighs just 800 pounds, according to officials at Browning-Ferris Inc.
Until recently, very little plastic was recycled. According to the Environmental Protection Agency only about one-tenth of 1 percent of all plastics produced -- mostly large soda bottles -- was recycled in 1986.
But as landfill space became scarce and expensive and legislation to ban or tax materials not being recycled appeared at the state and local level, the plastics industry tried to jump start a recycling system. The recycling rate improved to 1.1 percent in 1988, according to E.P.A. figures.
Last week in Washington, at a meeting of Council for Solid Waste Solutions, an industry group, leaders of the platic-producing and -using companies said they would attempt to force the pace of plastics recycling by providing $20 million a year in assistance to communities interested in recycling.
They said the action was in response to consumer attitudes toward plastic packaging. "Even though consumers like to use plastic packaging, they hate to throw it away," said John E. Pepper, the president of the Procter & Gamble Company. "They feel guilty because they know that plastics are a contributor to our solid waste problem."
The group said the program's goal was to have 25 percent of the plastic bottles and containers sold each year -- or about 18 billion -- in recycling programs by 1995. The plastics industry will invest in the 15 to 20 reprocessing plants needed to reach this level.
Currently, according to the industry group, about 1,500 communities, including about 10 percent of the nation's population, have recycling programs in which homeowners separate recyclable materials from ordinary trash. Only about 500 now include plastic.
The industry leaders said plastic collection programs would be needed in 4,000 communities to reach the 25 percent level. They said the number of recycling programs would have to double each year from now until 1995 to meet the goal.
Although there are hundreds of different types of plastic, two of them, high density polyethylene used in milk bottles) and polyethylene terephthalate (used in large soda bottles), account for more than 80 percent of the plastic used in consumer products. Most existing recycling programs have focused on these materials.

HEADLINE: FORUM;
The Failures of A.T.&T. Strategies

BYLINE: By A. MICHAEL NOLL; A. Michael Noll, formerly a marketing executive at A.T.&T., is on sabbatical from the Annenberg School for Communication, University of Southern California, where he is a professor.

 BODY:
The stable and conservative American Telephone and Telegraph Company of old has become today's newest corporate raider. Its attempt to acquire the NCR Corporation raises questions about where it is going. The company believes that the NCR acquisition is consistent with its strategy in computer networking. Is it? Should we trust A.T.& T.'s management when it comes to computers, strategy and vision?
The biggest event in A.T.& T.'s history was the divestiture of the local Bell telephone companies. Its thinking in accepting divestiture, rather than deciding to continue legal battles against it, was that the company would then be free to enter information-age businesses, such as computers, and capitalize on the research and development strengths of Bell Labs. The local phone companies were viewed then as financial drains. 
 How has this strategy worked? Not well. A.T.& T.'s computer business has failed to achieve significant market penetrations, perhaps indicating that management does not understand the computer business. The promise of many new high-tech businesses being developed from the research of Bell Labs was an illusion. The research cupboard was mostly bare in terms of new businesses and ventures, and business-development knowledge was lacking.
Further, the divestiture was a financial mistake. Last year, the seven regional holding companies had a combined net income of $8.5 billion; A.T.& T's net income was $2.7 billion. A.T.& T. would be more profitable today if it had kept the Bell companies and instead divested its manufacturing unit, Western Electric, and its long-distance division, Long Lines.
The profits from A.T.& T.'s successful network business are being milked to keep the unprofitable business afloat. Based on information from A.T.& T. annual reports and data submitted by the company to the Federal Communications Commission, I estimate that current yearly pretax losses from the sale of products could be as little as $1.5 billion or as much as $5 billion, depending on how research and development and administrative costs are allocated. A.T.& T.'s total revenue from the sale of products is about $12 billion. The product business seems to suffer from huge costs and keen competition.
Shareholders must ask whether management's vision of A.T.& T. as a force in the computer business is realistic, and whether buying NCR makes any sense. Service -- not products -- is where most companies want to be now. Even the International Business Machines Corporation is searching for a service business to offset problems in computers.
When A.T.& T. should probably be unloading its losing product business, it wants to take on NCR. Will NCR simply add to its losses? Will A.T.& T. be able to afford the debt for the acquisition? It says it must be in the computer business to pursue its strategy in computer networking. But this is as logical as claiming that one must manufacture ovens to be in the restaurant business.
These problems make me wonder whether A.T.& T. has lost its way, whether it has any overall realistic strategy. Is the ship rudderless in the high seas of competition, while the captain searches for the New World of computers?
A proper strategy for A.T.& T. would involve developing and articulating a corporate vision to make the company the leading force in telecommunication services. New service-based businesses in telecommunications should be developed building on A.T.& T.'s premier network. Services could be a public data network that builds on the company's common-channel signaling network, a specialized network using satellite transmission for international facsimile, and customized billing services for residential and small-business customers. Given its apparent losses, the product business should be sold or divested. A.T.& T.'s entry into financial and other nontelecommunication service areas seems to make sense, but with care.
A.T.& T. could be a candidate for acquisition. If control of it were acquired, management could be changed and the product business divested. The remaining A.T.& T. would double in value.
A.T.& T. operates the premier long-distance network in the nation. Its telecommunications service is a quality service with a bargain price. Clearly, providing telecommunication service is its strength. Its future should build on that. Theodore N. Vail, the founder of the Bell System, had vision when he saw long-distance service as the key ingredient in A.T.& T's future. His vision is still, if not even more, valid in shaping A.T.& T.'s future. If there were ever a need for another Vail to show A.T.& T. the way, it is today.

HEADLINE: Until Working Father Joins Superwoman

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In "Sexism, and Hard Choices" (Op-Ed, March 15), Linda Ellerbee says we have to be realistic in understanding the hard choices made by both Meredith Vieira and Don Hewitt of CBS's "60 Minutes." But there's an important reality that Ms. Ellerbee overlooks: most women don't have the economic luxury (or the security of having the public eye focused on their employer's response) that Ms. Vieira has to decide to work part time and stick to that decision when the employer says, "No dice." 
 As a student at Barnard College in the early 1980's, I was told repeatedly that the women of the 80's could have it all -- career and family. Now those who urged us on are telling us that it's too exhausting, we can't be Superwomen. "Superwoman is a dirty lie," says Ms. Ellerbee. "You can't have it all -- not all at once, anyway."
So she suggests we alternate family and career over the years, and make "painful choices again and again." This not only puts the burden, once again, on women to find ways to juggle family and career; it also ignores the reality that most families need two steady incomes to survive.
It seems to me there is another possibility: demand that men share the pain of those difficult choices.
Ms. Ellerbee points out that the term "working father" isn't in our vocabulary. And I have yet to hear of a man on the corporate "daddy track." We need to make parenthood an equal priority for mothers and fathers. Until American employers see a man in his "childbearing years" as someone who may take parental leave or who will be out the door at 5 P.M. sharp to pick up his daughter from day care, women will continue to be discriminated against in the workplace.
Most families need two incomes, yet employers still act as if the man is the breadwinner, and the woman is just doing the career thing to boost her self-esteem or to do something until she has babies. Until "working father" is part of our vocabulary, Superwoman will remain. She may be haggard, but she'll still be struggling to do it all -- because somebody has to.
 
MARIN GAZZANIGA
New York, March 18, 1991

HEADLINE: Sludge Composting And Its Benefits

 BODY:
The recent article "Sludge: New Focus on Recycling" discussed Camden County's plans to construct a sewage sludge composting plant, but overlooked the existence of the first in-vessel sludge composting facility in New Jersey. It is in Middle Township and is operated by the Cape May County Municipal Utilities Authority.
As chairman of the authority, I am writing not because we feel slighted or seek further recognition, but because our experience with composting allows us to answer a few of the most important questions raised in the article. Our concern about the environment, of course, extends beyond the borders of our own county.
The mere fact that a successful sludge composting plant does exist in New Jersey is itself crucial to the central issue that prompted the article: whether authorities in the northern counties can expect to research composting technology, launch their projects and obtain state approvals within a reasonable time frame.
For authorities studying the timetable element, it is essential to know that the state regulatory agencies would not be starting from ground zero on composting projects. Permitting standards, performance standards and end-product quality standards were developed in the early to middle 1980's. Our own facility has been operating successfully for more than five years.
Indeed, operations have been so successful that the Federal Environmental Protection Agency last fall awarded our composting facility second place nationwide for "beneficial use of sewage sludge," a major category of the E.P.A.'s annual National Wastewater Management Excellence Awards.
Secondly, the author of the article, unaware of the track record, could only be speculative about the marketability of the compost product.
To say that marketing "should not be a problem" is an understatement. In Cape May County, the product is so marketable that the demand far exceeds the supply. The product, a soil supplement sold as "Capeorganic," is quite literally coveted by landscapers, contractors, golf-course greenskeepers, plant-nursery operators and local government public works departments.
Perhaps even Donald Trump could give the product an endorsement: it was a component of the planting soil mixtures used for landscaping at his Taj Mahal hotel casino.
In 1990, our facility processed 9,871 wet tons of sewage sludge and produced 19,780 cubic yards of nitrogen-rich, pathogen-free Capeorganic. Every bit of the product was sold, and our supplies are spoken for even during the winter.
Yet a third concern discussed in the article was whether feasible methods exist for preventing contamination of the sludge by heavy metals. Given that Cape May County has a tourist economy and very little industry, heavy metals are minimal in wastewater here, so this is perhaps the only aspect of sludge composting in which our experience is not instructive.
State law, however, already requires that pretreatment to remove heavy metals be accomplished by the industries themselves. The lag time for implementation of composting (or any sludge-management system) provides a ready-made grace period and eventual deadline for moving to full enforcement.
For all these reasons, we feel that all authorities should look more closely at composting as a technology for sludge management.
Direct land application is a viable and environmentally beneficial method of handling sludge, but there are numerous administrative problems involved. As for incineration and out-of-state landfills, both are expensive and otherwise problematic, and neither is positive from an environmental standpoint. The Cape May County Authority's experience has proven that sludge is a resource to be recovered rather than disposed of, that it can become an environmental asset rather than a nuisance.
To be thorough about the lessons to be learned from our experience, we must concede that our composting plant, within a quarter-mile of a residential area, did require post-construction modifications to control odors. Advanced air-cleaning technology has been highly effective in dealing with this problem, but other authorities could avoid the situation by choosing locations just slightly more remote than the site that was available to us in Middle Township.
The authority would be happy to conduct tours of the composting facility for visiting authority officials who are facing these decisions on sludge management.
 
DANIEL RILEY
Chairman
Cape May County Municipal Utilities Authority
Cape May Court House

HEADLINE: Black Britons Speak of a Motherland That Looks Upon Them as Outcasts

BYLINE: By SHEILA RULE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LONDON

 BODY:
World War II was raging when Allan Wilmot set sail for England from his native Jamaica to serve, in his words, "the mother country."
Like blacks in Britain's other West Indian colonies, Mr. Wilmot was taught in school that his island had been colonized by the world's greatest civilization. He patriotically sang "Rule Britannia" and waved the Union Jack. When recruitment posters for the Royal Air Force flooded Mr. Wilmot's hometown, Kingston, there was no question that he would sign up. 
 But Mr. Wilmot said he and thousands of other West Indian volunteers arrived in England to find that Britons "weren't expecting us."
 
'They Knew Nothing About Us'
 "We had learned so much about the people, geography, the economy of England," said Mr. Wilmot, who was 18 years old when he came here. "But they knew nothing about us. They believed that colored people had tails but that we cut them off to come to Europe. When we danced with English girls at dance halls, we could feel their hands going down and down and down, feeling for the stumps of the tails."
After the war, Mr. Wilmot settled in Britain, married and raised a family. Now 65 years old and a retired telephone-company employee, he said he had "never felt 100 percent" in Britain.
"Sometimes it feels like they still aren't expecting us," said Mr. Wilmot, who is vice president of the West Indian Ex-Servicemen Association, based in London. "It feels like they keep black people waiting, waiting, waiting in the wings."
It has been more than 40 years since West Indians began the modern migration of ethnic minority groups to Britain. About 495,000 people of West Indian origin now live here and they represent the overwhelming majority of blacks in the nation. Yet they make up about 1 percent of Britain's 55 million people, a tiny minority by United States standards, and they seem to be a people visible and invisible.
 
Idea of a Black Briton
 Sixty percent of all West Indians in Britain live in London. Their concentration here has produced disproportionate visibility in several areas. Some neighborhoods, although predominantly white, are shot through with a distinctly West Indian rhythm. Melodic Caribbean accents waft through streets punctuated with grocers' displays of plantains, goat meat and other staples of Caribbean cuisine. West Indian social and business clubs and restaurants stand alongside the ethnic group's fast-growing Pentecostal churches.
Reggae is a major influence in Britain's cultural scene. The annual Notting Hill Carnival, inspired by Trinidadians who migrated to the Notting Hill area in western London after World War II, has grown from a small outdoor party into what is widely regarded as the largest street festival in Europe.
But along other avenues, West Indians, Africans and other blacks are rarely seen. Race relations experts, social scientists and others say that there are virtually no blacks in the top echelons of the British Government, judiciary, police, press or civil service. Three blacks and one Asian are in the 650-member House of Commons, all members of the opposition Labor Party.
Several studies show that West Indian and other blacks are more likely than whites to be unemployed, jailed, homeless and poorly educated. And although 53 percent of people of West Indian descent living in Britain were born here, many among them say that the society has yet to feel comfortable with the idea of the black Briton in the way that the United States accepts the concept of the black American.
 
Patterns of Prejudice
 Blacks and others say the situation is linked to deep patterns of prejudice. For more than 25 years, Britain has had laws aimed at combating racial discrimination, but it has never tackled "the fact that the culture regards black people as inferior and not deserving of equal treatment," said Colin Brown, a former senior research fellow at the Policy Studies Institute based in London.
Indeed, even as Britain was passing laws intended to tackle racial discrimination, it was enacting statutes to stem the flow of blacks and other racial minorities into the country. A law passed in 1962 limited entry of Commonwealth immigrants to people who had been given work permits and a law in 1968 restricted the entry of Kenyan Asians who held British passports. An act passed in 1971 restricted admission of blacks into Britain to those who were coming for particular employment for a limited time period.
Certainly, study after study shows that disadvantages experienced by West Indians and other blacks also cloud the lives of Indians, Pakistanis, Bangladeshis and other groups among Britain's 2.6 million ethnic minorities. But some people suggest that West Indians may have been among those least expecting to face discrimination when they arrived. According to that view, they came as almost ready-made British citizens. They spoke English, were educated in schools styled after those here, prayed in Christian churches and worshipped cricket as a second religion.
"Britishness was deeply instilled in them without their knowing what Britishness was really about," said Winsome Pinnock, a playwright whose parents immigrated to Britain from the West Indies in the late 1950's. "They believed they were coming to a great empire and that they could share in its riches because they were part of that empire."
 
 The History
The 'Color Bar' And Manual Labor
 Blacks have a long history in Britain. Africans were here in the Third Century as soldiers in the Roman Army. Centuries later, some of the Africans sold into slavery ended up here. Black seamen and their families started communities in British ports in the 1800's and West Indians came during World War II.
Nonetheless, Britain was virtually a homogeneous society until the 1950's. Its primary experience with nonwhites had been as their colonial master in the vast British empire. But in the 1950's, a postwar labor shortage, a damaged economy and a weakening grip on its territories forced Britain to encourage people from the Caribbean, the Indian subcontinent and Africa to come here to fill manual jobs. About 125,000 West Indians came between 1948 and 1958, many of them to man buses and subways and to work in hospitals, hotels and restaurants.
Under a 1948 law, people from the colonies were British citizens with the right to live here permanently. But historians and blacks who lived through the 1950's say that nonwhites were soon shunned as aliens. Amid an economic downturn, growing unemployment and strong winds of xenophobia, whites began to accuse nonwhites of stealing their jobs. The immigrants were also victims of Britain's class system, which labeled them second-class citizens and stifled their social progress. Underpinning all of this was a sentimental longing for Britain's imperial past, some Britons say.
 
No 'Legacy of Guilt'
 West Indians and other blacks found themselves faced with a so-called "color bar," a practice encouraged by white American soldiers who brought their policies of racial discrimination and segregation to England with them in World War II. Blacks were barred from jobs and housing and from hotels, pubs and other public services.
"White people in Britain don't have the legacy of guilt about the past as there is in America about the period of slavery, even though there is plenty to be guilty about," said Anthony Lester, a white lawyer who helped to draft Britain's equal-opportunity law. "Nor is the English dream like the American dream of equality. If you asked the average Briton what he was striving for, I don't think equality would be the first thing to come to mind."
 
 Anti-Bias Laws
Code of Protection 'Has No Gums'

In 1965, Britain introduced its first law aimed at fighting racial discrimination. Two other acts have followed but race relations experts, blacks and others say that discrimination against minority groups remains pervasive.
The current equal-opportunity law, the Race Relations Act of 1976, set up the Commission for Racial Equality to combat discrimination in employment, housing, education and other areas. But the act, like its predecessors, is widely viewed as having weak legal remedies. It has no provisions for class-action suits or contract compliance measures to motivate employers to hire minorities. The commission can help individuals pursue discrimination complaints through a so-called industrial tribunal, which can award compensation of up to about $17,000.
"The law not only has no teeth," said A. Sivanandan, director of the Institute of Race Relations, an independent research organization, "it has no gums. It makes racism respectable by default. Black people remain unprotected in every area of society."
Surveys support Mr. Sivanandan's assertion. One study by the Policy Studies Institute showed that one-third of all employers would hire whites over equally qualified blacks without even giving the black applicants an interview. Another study found that black workers were more than twice as likely as whites with similar qualifications to end up in manual jobs.
 
Lack of Political Will
 In the criminal justice area, a report by the National Association for the Care and Resettlement of Offenders said young blacks were likely to receive harsher treatment than whites in the criminal justice system. In housing, a study by the Commission for Racial Equality showed that one in five real-estate agencies discriminated against ethnic minorities.
Officials at the Commission for Racial Equality say they are not satisfied with progress made under the 1976 law but that the situation would be far worse without it. They say that they have been pressing the Government for years to introduce legislation on the British mainland that is similar to a tough law put into effect last year in Northern Ireland aimed at fighting religious discrimination against the Roman Catholic minority.
That law includes contract compliance and compulsory monitoring of a workforce's religious composition. Concerns that discriminate can be fined up to $60,000. A spokesman in Britain's Home Office said there were no plans to equip the Race Relations Act with similar provisions.
Many advocates of racial equality say the Government has never had the political will to fight discrimination. To assertions that it ignored race relations, the Government of former Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher responded that its economic policies had brought increased employment, higher incomes and a higher percentage of home ownerships to minorities. Prime Minister John Major has said he wants Britain to have "no artificial barrier of background, religion or race."
In a written policy statement, the Government says it is "wholly committed to a racially fair and just society." It says it vigorously enforces the Race Relations Act, monitors the civil service's employment practices and scrutinizes legislative and policy proposals to see that they contain the spirit of racial and sex equality. It also points to several Government programs aimed at benefiting minority groups, including one intended to regenerate inner cities, where many blacks and Asians live.
More radical measures to ease discrimination appear some way off, race relations experts say, because the cause of race relations remains widely unpopular in Britain.
 
 Blacks and the Police
Hostile Encounters, Accounts of Abuse
For a nation accustomed to viewing itself as tolerant and fair, the ongoing debate over the troubled relationship between the police and blacks is particularly unsettling.
An opinion poll in 1989 found that 68 percent of blacks agreed with the statement that "most police are prejudiced against colored people," compared with 22 percent of the total population.
Many blacks say that their belief is supported by many personal encounters in which the police are hostile or abusive. In one widely publicized incident, Linford Christie, a popular Olympic sprinter, was wrongfully arrested on a charge of stealing a car that he had in fact rented. Mr. Christie quoted the arresting police officer as saying, "What's a nigger like you doing in an England track suit?"
Carl Thompson-Ellis says that he, too, has been the target of police harassment. Mr. Thompson-Ellis is one of the Metropolitican Police's 475 nonwhite officers, who make up less than 1.7 percent of the 28,329-member force. He says that he has found "niggers out" and other racial epithets scrawled in his desk diary and on walls at work. White officers, he said, call blacks "nigger, coon and jungle bunny so often that they're not conscious of it because it's the norm."
Mr. Thompson-Ellis, a 48-year-old detective, said white officers target young black men for abuse. He said one practice involves beating up black suspects before taking them to the police station and, in order to explain the suspects' wounds, accusing them of assault or resisting arrest. A police department spokesman insisted that such incidents "do not happen." The spokesman said police officials recognized that what some white officers called "harmless banter" about nonwhites had elements of racism and that training programs had been started to try to stamp out such behavior.
The spokesman said the police force was working to improve relations with minorities and had an ongoing drive to recruit more nonwhite policemen. Under the force's grievance procedures, he said, policemen can lodge formal complaints against fellow officers who commit racially motivated offenses. Policemen found guilty of such offenses can be dismissed.
But Mr. Thompson-Ellis said many nonwhite policemen declined to complain about racist behavior within the force because "we don't know who we are complaining to."
"The moment you take a stand," Mr. Thompson-Ellis said, "you're put down as a troublemaker, someone who can't be trusted. We have to work with those guys. One of the things you don't want is to be in trouble on the street on some dark night, to radio for assistance and have no one answer you. What happens is they hear you and ignore you. So you keep quiet."
 
 The Future
Rage Among Fruit of the First Wave

Children and grandchildren of the first generation of West Indians in Britain speak of a simmering rage at what they view as this society's rejection of them. They say the anger could boil over as it did in riots -- some blacks call them uprisings -- in 1981 and 1985. In those years, blazing gasoline bombs illuminated the shortage of hope in desolate pockets of the nation's cities.
Some blacks hope that the rage will translate into greater political and economic independence in their community. The National Black Caucus, a national civil-rights body of sorts, was formed several years ago in an attempt to begin to make that happen.
The caucus and a network of black professionals -- including lawyers, probation officers and social workers -- have begun meeting informally to try to coordinate strategies to put the needs of blacks on the nation's agenda.
Blacks employed in London's subways -- where about one-third of the workforce is black -- have also come together to make demands and have won an agreement from management to try to insure equal opportunity in the workplace.
"We've given up on changing attitudes," said Peter Herbert, vice chairman of the Society of Black Lawyers. "Whites just don't realize that our disadvantage is their disadvantage. Unless there is a civil disturbance, they don't see how the underrepresentation of blacks in business, politics, education, housing and every other area impacts on them. What we can do is try to change behavior and outcome. If that doesn't happen, you only have to look at the ghettos in the United States to see what the future holds for Britain."

HEADLINE: Hunt for Marcos's Billions Yields More Dead Ends Than Hard Cash

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS

 BODY:
After a five-year hunt through the courts and banking systems of three continents, the Philippine Government has uncovered yachts and paintings and silver services and a few hundred million dollars, but not much more of the $5 billion or more Ferdinand E. Marcos is said to have stolen from his country.
As the six-year term of President Corazon C. Aquino nears its end next year, a touch of desperation has entered the search and her Government has begun talking of cutting its losses and making deals with Mr. Marcos's widow, Imelda. 
 Lawyers and Government officials involved in the search now say it will probably never be known just how much Mr. Marcos, who died in Hawaii in 1989, stole from the Philippines during his 20 years as President. Whatever the total, they say, it is now clear that only a small portion will be recovered.
 
Chocolate Tab Is $6,000
 Caesar Parlade, a former member of the agency seeking to recover the Marcos funds, the Presidential Commission on Good Government, put the possible total recovery at $1 billion to $1.5 billion.
"There is a large gap between what was stolen and what remains," he said in an interview. "If you look at the records you find that on one of her trips Imelda was able to spend $6,000 on the first day for chocolates. That is the kind of lifestyle that used up the money."
In the Philippines, peremptory confiscations of property, deals with some of Mr. Marcos's "cronies" and scores of civil suits have won the Government about $100 million in cash and another $100 million in assets that range from horses to hotels and from planes to sugar plantations.
In the United States, the Government has recovered banks, buildings and assorted baubles from the Marcos residences. It has succeeded, in a Los Angeles court, in freezing the Marcos assets worldwide. But it failed in New York in a case brought by American officials to convict Mrs. Marcos of racketeering in the purchase of properties with stolen Government funds.
The biggest single haul could come in Switzerland, where the Supreme Court ruled recently that the Philippine Government is entitled to recover $350 million in Marcos bank accounts in that country.
The pursuit of the Marcos wealth began with wads of cryptic documents that were left scattered through Malacanang Palace in Manila when Mr. Marcos and his entourage fled on the night of Feb. 26, 1986.
 
A Moral and Material Crusade
 With her first presidential decree after seizing office in a nearly bloodless "people power" uprising, Mrs. Aquino promised her people she would track down and recover the wealth and use it to help rebuild her country, one of the poorest in Asia.
The recovery was seen as a symbol of the moral crusade with which she came to power, and her first commissioner of the recovery effort, Jovito Salonga, now the Senate President, offered no quarter.
"A deal would amount to saying that it is all right for an official to steal, so long as he is ready to share his loot with the state later -- if prosecuted," Mr. Salonga said then.
It was he who first estimated the Marcos wealth at $5 billion or more, and Manila has stood by that estimate even as it scales back its goals and begins to talk of making deals.
 
Spending Goes Unchecked
 Within the Philippines, the Government moved to seize the assets of Mr. Marcos and his associates, but many of these became entangled in complicated court challenges as the wealthy men in the Marcos camp hired the nation's best lawyers.
For the assets the Government did recover, Mr. Parlade now urges quick disposal, worrying that they could pose "a great temptation" for Marcos-style corruption by the next President.
Abroad, the suit in a Federal court in Los Angeles froze the Marcos assets and resulted in an order under which Mrs. Marcos must submit an accounting of any expenditures over $10,000. Officials within the Philippine commission said Mrs. Marcos continues to spend freely, maintaining that her money comes from one of her daughters.
Most other efforts have proved unproductive, however, as the Philippine Government discovered that Mr. Marcos was a virtuoso in hiding his money in accounts everywhere from Lichtenstein to the Cayman Islands.
Some investigators who have been involved with the wealth-recovery effort say the inefficiency and political infighting that has characterized the Aquino Government may have cost the nation millions of dollars in potential recoveries.
 
Investigators Accused
 The presidential commission was itself accused of corruption almost from its first days, and has labored under controversy. Although few of the accusations have been proved, a number of the commission's fiscal agents have been charged with corruption and removed.
But Mr. Parlade, reflecting on the commission's difficulties, said, "From what I have seen, it is not corruption; it's ineptness."
Like much of the Government, he said, the commission has become bogged down in its own bureaucracy and hobbled by a constant turnover of its staff. Its approach and its politics have zigzagged as it has lurched through a succession of four commissioners.
And while the commission floundered, Mr. Parlade said, some Marcos assets have dropped dramatically in value. The Redwood Bank in San Francisco, for example, has depreciated because of bad investments from $40 million to about $6.5 million. The California Overseas Bank in Los Angeles, which was once worth $30 million, is now worth no more than $14 million, he said.
 
Assets Dwindle During Search
 Severina Rivera, who was legal director for the international recovery effort until December 1988, said property depreciation and Government mismanagement had cost millions of dollars in potential recoveries from four buildings in New York.
"With all the expenses of litigation, the Philippines got nothing out of the buildings when we could easily have made $100 million if we had sold in 1988," she said.
Political changes at the top tend to bring broad shifts in social and economic power in the Philippines. In this tradition, some of the recovered wealth seems to have changed hands from friends and relatives of Mr. Marcos to friends and relatives of Mrs. Aquino.
The President has said she does not plan to run for a second term, and whatever the outcome, the 1992 election is expected to mark an end to any vigorous pursuit of the Marcos wealth, as political power shifts.
 
'Aquino Corruption' on Agenda
 Looking ahead, some opponents are already preparing to supercede the search for Marcos wealth with an investigation of "Aquino corruption."
One of the new front-runners in the jockeying to succeed Mrs. Aquino is her estranged cousin, Eduardo Cojuangco, who was one of Mr. Marcos's closest associates and who returned to Manila under mysterious circumstances in 1989.
With some Filipinos suggesting that he only wants to become President to protect his own extensive assets, the possibility has arisen that Mr. Cojuangco could in the end be one of the big winners in the story of the Marcos wealth.
At the same time, after years of stubborn refusal, Mrs. Aquino said this month that Mrs. Marcos could return to the Philippines to face trial, a move that would meet a legal condition set by the Swiss Government for recovery of the funds held there.
Last fall the Government disclosed that it was negotiating with Mrs. Marcos for a settlement that some officials said might allow her to keep 60 percent of her assets, though their total extent remains unknown.
The deal under discussion apparently would have terminated attempts through the courts to recover all the Marcos wealth in return for funds held in Hong Kong, if these totaled $250 million or more.
Mrs. Marcos maintained that a formal agreement had been signed. But David Castro, the current commissioner of the presidential panel, insisted that the talks had only been exploratory.
 
Compromise Is Defended
 There was also an angry public reaction in Manila last November when the Government revealed that it had reached an agreement with one of Mr. Marcos's closest associates, Roberto Benedicto, to share with him in nearly equal portions $31 million in Swiss bank deposits in his name.
Defending the dealmaking, Mr. Castro said in an interview that in a legal process, "Compromise is required by the rules."
He added that overseas legal expenses in the search for the Marcos wealth had already reached $8 million, and that continued litigation could take many more years, with diminishing returns.
Without compromises, he said, the Philippines can only expect to recover the $350 million in Swiss banks and perhaps $250 million more from the Los Angeles suit, which seeks the return of all the Marcos assets.
One of Mr. Castro's precedessors as commissioner, Ramon Diaz, who is now Ambassador to Canada, disagreed strongly with this reasoning, saying: "This is not simply a legal case but an ethical case. You should never make deals with Marcos. You just open the door for others to follow."
He added: "Imelda is not supposed to retain anything. That is stolen money."
 
Still the Marcos Philippines
 The degeneration of the recovery effort from a moral crusade to a duel among sharp lawyers has brought a deep sense of disillusionment to many Filipinos. They say it is a signal that the country has remained in effect the Marcos Philippines, rather than some new Aquino Philippines, as it continues to labor under his legacy of corruption, economic decline and political violence.
Belinda Aquino, the author of "The Politics of Plunder," a book about Marcos corruption, said that along with most Filipinos, she had run out of hope that the Government would live up to its promise of fundamental change.
"We didn't really have a social revolution," said Ms. Aquino, who is no relation to the President. "We had a political upheaval in which we got rid of Marcos, but that's about it.
"Now with these deals, the message that is being sent to the Philippines is that it pays to steal, that crime pays. I think something should be done. But it's almost kind of too late, and what can you do?"

The former President is seen with his wife, Imelda, behind him in Manila after a disputed election in 1986. He fled the country shorty afterward and died in 1989 (Associated Press); The recovery effort was seen as the symbol of a moral crusade with which President Corazon C. Aquino came to power. (Reuters) (pg. 13)
