PUB-DATE: March 31, 1991

SECTION: Section 1; Part 1; Page 1; Column 1; Metropolitan Desk

LENGTH: 1651 words

HEADLINE: Recession and Guilt Pare Dining Trade and Menus

BYLINE: By MOLLY O'NEILL

 BODY:
In the last two years, the euphemistic Closed for Renovation sign has nearly eclipsed the Happy Hour signs in the windows of New York City restaurants. Crowds have thinned in the best dining rooms. Same-day reservations are rarely a problem.
New York City restaurateurs are worried about the paring of corporate expense accounts for restaurant meals in the last few years. They cite the erosive recession, which has cut into tourism and entertainment, and the growing number of young families eating at home.
 
Embarrasment of Riches
 But what worries restaurateurs more are customers like Eric Wyka. As he waited to check his camel's hair coat before dinner at the Rainbow Room recently, he glanced over his shoulder nervously. "I'd be real embarrassed if one of my friends who's been laid off saw me here," Mr. Wyka, a 40-year-old lawyer, murmured to his companion. "I mean, it's not like I go out so much any more, but I feel sort of guilty going out at all." 
 To restaurateurs, this mood change is scarier than dipping check averages and empty chairs. "We had the good times and now we're deep in guilty times," said David Liederman, who owns the Braodway Grill and closed his second restaurant, Chez Louis, last week to convert it into a less expensive dining room.
Peter Aschkenasy, owner of the venerable Gage & Tollner Restaurant in downtown Brooklyn, says a fundamental change is afoot. "Recessions come and go, that's the business: Good operators survive and adapt," he said. "The frightening thing isn't the drop in numbers or the change in spending, it's the attitude change."
The New York restaurant world has been especially vulnerable to even a slight shift in spending habits. In the fabulous free-spending '80's, new restaurants popped up faster than corn in a hot pan. In building restaurants, the '80's motto was "borrow and spend," and it left many places heavily in debt. Rents soared and operating costs climbed. By 1990, profit margins were so tight that to lose a dozen customers a day could mean not paying the vegetable bill.
"Restaurants throughout the country are taking it on the chin, but the situation is exacerbated in New York City by higher rental percentages and union difficulties," Charles Bernstein, editor of Nation's Restaurant News, a trade journal, wrote last month.
In other parts of the country, rent accounts for about 5 or 6 percent of monthly sales. In New York City, a restaurant's rent can eat up as much as 20 percent of sales when sales are good. In recent interviews, 25 New York restaurateurs cited declines in sales of 5 to 40 percent over the last year, pushing the rent share to as much as 50 percent of sales.
The number of sit-down restaurants, from McDonald's to Lutece, has dipped to 10,461 in 1991 from a high of 11,767 in 1988, said Steven Linden, acting director of the permits division of the city's Department of Health. Dining icons of the '80's like Batons, Jams and 150 Wooster were casualties. City labor statistics indicate that the New York restaurant world is still shrinking. In 1988, 135,600 people worked in eating and drinking establishments, said Michael Press, Deputy Commissioner of Business Development for New York City. Today, city restaurants employ 128,000 people.
Not all news calls for another roll of the doom-and-gloom drum. In tracking the use of American Express cards in New York City restaurants over the last year, Patricia Duffy, vice president of restaurant marketing for American Express, reported that while people spend the same money over all in restaurants, there is a change in how they spend it.
While her company will not divulge specific statistics, Ms. Duffy said, "In general, customers are shying away from lavish occasions and concentrating on value-oriented dining." In a nationwide study by Grey Advertising in New York City, a dramatic shift in spending was found but, according to Barbara Feigin, the study's author, "Restaurants are one quality of lifestyle thing that people would like to hold on to." Instead of excess, she said customers today are attracted to "a generous spirit and value in restaurants."
Still, many restaurateurs hear a death knell in every canceled reservation, as well as in every piece of proposed restrictive legislation and every health finding that might promote a fear of uninhibited dining. Economic factors can be dealt with pragmatically; the guilt factor, on the other hand, seems about as unstoppable as The Terminator to most restaurateurs. "Connoisseurship is being lumped together with 'nouveau riche' as some embarrassing '80's remnant," said Susan Liederman, co-owner of the Broadway Grill. "We are in real trouble."
Tom Margittai, co-owner of the Four Seasons, was more direct. "I've been in this business for 40 years and five recessions," he said. "This is the worst I've seen and it's not simply economics."
 
Held Up for Ridicule
 Along with symbols like designer bottled water and $2,000 business suits, New York City restaurants have been put in the stocks of the American conscience and held up for ridicule. When a pricey dining room closes, it is seen as just deserts for wanton indulgence; when a superstar chef shows up on the unemployment line, his bad fortune is often cast as a well-earned hair shirt. Just as restaurants mirror spending patterns, they are a blotter for the social conscience.
"A moral change is palpable," said Joseph Baum, whose restaurant consulting group, the Joseph Baum and Michael Whiteman Company, operates the Rainbow Room and recently closed Aurora, a midtown restaurant. "It isn't proper to show how much money you have anymore."
Restaurant patrons say their emotions can get the best of them on a night out. "I can't in good conscience order all the expensive food and wine," said Daniel Kaplan, president-elect of the 92d Street Y. "Knowing that colleagues are worried about their jobs takes all the joy out of the evening."
Mr. Wyka said, "I'm about as comfortable ordering foie gras or caviar as I would be driving a big flashy car through the Bronx."
 
Choosing the Homelier Fish
 Restaurateurs say the guilt factor is at work in what customers choose for dinner, and even modest restaurants notice a shift in appetite that is not always related to price. At Claire Restaurant, a Key-West-style seafood house, the owner, Marvin Paige, said that "customers want the homelier fish -- the grouper, the whitefish, hake and flounder. Just the sound of something extravagant like lobster makes them flinch these days."
Some restaurateurs have pursued the public with more modest concepts that mirror the mood shift. When Rakel opened in 1987, it was a pricey preserve of artful fare. Last year, prices were cut, the cooking took a homier bent and the restaurant was rechristened Cafe Rakel. Last month, after a coat of cozy paint and a few more grandmere touches to the menu, Raoul's Brasserie and Grill rose from the Rakel ashes.
No one's career parallels the recent history of New York restaurants more closely than Len Allison's. In 1975, just as the city was recuperating from the last restaurant recession, Mr. Allison spent "a few hundred bucks" on pots and pans and began serving dinner in his Brooklyn home. A year later, he borrowed $40,000 and opened a 50-seat restaurant in a former tavern in Boerum Hill. And in 1981, he invested $250,000 to build Hubert's, a 50-seat restaurant in Gramercy Park. In 1988, he mustered a group of investors and opened a $1.5 million Park Avenue dining room that seated 200 guests. In 1990 he closed it.
"I think we were a part of the late 80's, the fabulousness, the mania for more, more, more -- everybody wanted to be wowed," Mr. Allison said. "We lost our intimate connection with our customers. We got too pricey." This month, Mr. Allison opened Onda, a 50-seat restaurant in SoHo that he built for $75,000. Instead of chic fare, Mr. Allison is serving family-style platters of Chinese and Italian cooking; instead of $120 a person, dinner is about $30.
 
Better Food, Prices, Attention
 As New York City restaurateurs navigate the sea change by policing costs, slashing prices and expanding serving hours, customers are the obvious winners. "Dining has never been better in the City of New York," said Tim Zagat, publisher of the annual Zagat Restaurant Survey. "There's better-quality food, better prices, more personal attention and you don't have to wait months for reservations."
Mr. Baum agreed: "Restaurants are one of the first places to feel any change in the economic or social climate and certainly one of the first to react. We adjust, we realign, we get better." But he feels an ominous breeze. "When the profit margin tightens, every detail is magnified," he said. "Minding the details is the only way to keep the door open." Local restaurateurs are indeed minding details within their control. Careful buying is once again de rigueur.
Adam Tihany, a restaurant designer and the owner of Remi in Midtown, said that borrow-and-spend has been usurped by "rabid penny-pinching." He went on: "Everybody is looking for bargains. They want cut-rate groceries, wine, plates, glasses and paint jobs. Restaurateurs think bargain-hunting is the answer."
Cost-consciousness may solve a fraction of the New York restaurant equation, but frugal management cannot reverse the uneasiness. Mr. Baum talked of the 80's as the time when people became used to spending handsomely for a meal. Then, he said, the economy and national mood swing came along and changed the habit.
"You still can't eat better any place on earth," he said, surveying New York City from the Rainbow Room on the 65th floor of Rockefeller Center. Then he glanced Across his dining room, registered a handful of empty tables during a recent lunch hour and shook his head. "Do you have any idea how long it takes to change a changed habit?"

HEADLINE: Border Boom's Dirty Residue Imperils U.S.-Mexico Trade

BYLINE: By ROBERTO SURO, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NOGALES, Ariz.

 BODY:
Little more than a muddy creek when it starts flowing down a hillside in Mexico, the Nogales Wash passes by factories, shantytowns and noisy commercial streets before it reaches an invisible line and then suddenly becomes a swirling, stinking foreign policy problem for the Bush Administration.
When it crosses the border into the United States, the Nogales Wash is laced with toxic industrial pollutants and laden with untreated sewage. A public health emergency has been in force on the Arizona side for six months because the incidence of hepatitis there has climbed to 20 times the national average.
 
Pollution Threatens Trade Pact
 The runaway pollution and accompanying health threats can be found in virtually any city along the United States-Mexico border, which has undergone an economic explosion in recent years with the construction of hundreds of highly profitable American-owned factories operating under special trade rules. The American owners pay very low wages to the nearly half a million Mexicans they employ and are not obliged to use expensive antipollution equipment. 
 The pollution problems have become so grave that they have emerged as a major issue and potential obstacle to the free trade agreement that President Bush wants to negotiate with Mexico as a way to compete economically with Europe and Japan. The Mexican Government's environmental effort is in its infancy, and it is struggling with standards and enforcement.
Influential environmental groups, like the National Wildlife Federation, and others who oppose the free trade agreement, like the A.F.L.-C.I.O., have contended that the contamination of the Nogales Wash and other pollution problems along the border are just previews of what will happen across Mexico if tariffs and other trade barriers are removed, igniting a rush to develop new industries and spawn new wastes.
Moreover, Democrats in Congress say they will resist efforts for a speedy approval of a free trade agreement unless the White House agrees to address environmental issues in the negotiations with Mexico. The White House has not said whether it will.
But in American border cities, even the most committed advocates of free trade insist that existing environmental problems should be addressed before free trade become a reality.
"We who breathe the air and drink the water along the border are the first to say we support free trade and that we also want a long-term plan for the environment," said William F. Joffroy Jr., chairman of the Border Trade Alliance, an organization that promotes economic development on behalf of local governments and businesses on both sides of the border.
The contamination along the border runs the gamut from raw sewage to wood smoke to highly toxic industrial chemicals.
 
Wide Environmental Damage
 Across the Rio Grande from El Paso, for instance, firewood is the chief cooking and heating fuel for most of the 1.2 million residents of Ciudad Juarez, Mexico. Rubber tires are burned in kilns that make decorative bricks and tiles. Along with pollution from motor vehicles and industry, the smoke from these fires produces an acrid cloud over both cities under certain weather.
Furniture-making plants and metal-plating shops that fled environmental restrictions in California now flourish in Tijuana, Mexico, producing toxic wastes from their use of solvents.
Recreational use of the Rio Grande below Laredo, Tex., has long been considered unsafe because its sister city in Mexico, Nuevo Laredo, dumps about 25 million gallons of untreated sewage into the river every day.
Much of the development and growth along the border came as a result of the 1965 agreement between the two countries that allowed American companies to set up operations on the Mexican side. The American factories and assembly plants, called maquiladoras, import almost all raw materials and export almost all products virtually exempt from tariffs and other trade restrictions.
Now Mr. Bush wants to go further with the trade agreement, which would cut out all tariffs and trade barriers between the counties. A negative vote by either house of Congress before June 1 would deny Mr. Bush the authority to present the legislators with a completed agreement to be accepted or rejected.
"Along the border we already know what happens when free trade brings rapid economic growth to Mexico," said Mayor Suzanne Azar of El Paso. "We've seen the benefits but we have also seen some very tough environmental problems that are directly caused by that kind of growth."
 
Growth Boomed in 1980's
 The American plants grew quickly after the rapid devaluation of the Mexican peso starting in 1982 brought the wage of a Mexican worker down to about 50 cents an hour. Since then the number of maquiladoras has jumped to nearly 1,900 from about 300. They produce almost anything that involves a lot of labor, from carburetors to designer sunglasses.
On the outskirts of Nogales, a cluster of the plants sits on a hilltop. Big, box-like buildings, they do not have any smokestacks or cooling towers because they are used for assembling components rather than manufacturing parts or processing raw materials.
Down the hill, the neat industrial architecture gives way to ramshackle dwellings fashioned from cardboard and scraps of wood taken from the plants' trash bins. These are the homes of maquiladora workers, most of them peasants who have migrated from Mexico's agricultural regions in search of steady pay.
In about a dozen shacks the most common building material is a tan cardboard that once contained polyvinyl chloride. Written on the cardboard walls are warnings that the former contents could release hazardous fumes.
"This is some of the best stuff because it is real thick and keeps the rain out," said Juan Ramos, standing outside a two-room structure that served as home for him and five relatives. All had worked in the plants. Their water supply was stored in a 55-gallon drum that was also refuse from the plants. A bright yellow and orange label identified its former contents as a fluorocarbon solvent whose vapors are fatal if inhaled.
 
$27 for 49 Hours of Work
 The starting wage for a maquiladora worker is about 82,000 pesos, or about $27, for a 49-hour week. More experienced workers receive as much as 140,000 pesos, or about $47, in addition to fringe benefits like a subsidized lunch.
Catalina Denman, a public health specialist at the Colegio de Sonora, a research institute, who has conducted numerous studies in Nogales, had this to say about the situation: "In the course of just a few years the maquiladoras brought the industrial revolution and all that goes with it to a region that was completely unprepared for it.
"In the short term this is a positive development because a job is better than no job and because living conditions where these people came from were also terrible. In the long term, though, what happened in Nogales could happen all over Mexico if a free trade agreement produces the same kind of fast development with no real attention to human needs."
Behind Mr. Ramos's home was one of the communal outhouses used by residents of the shantytown. A little ditch from the outhouse led to a bigger ditch that flowed to a creek in the bottom of a ravine that flowed into the Nogales Wash, stream that grows in size with the seasons. Like virtually every other Mexican border town, Nogales does not have any sewage treatment plants.
After crossing the border the wash is contained within concrete embankments as it flows through the center of Nogales, Ariz. High concentrations of human wastes in the wash are blamed for the hepatitis epidemic that produced 58 cases and many suspected cases last year, said Patrick Zurick, deputy director of the Santa Cruz County Health Department.
Some of the cases involved people who had direct contact with the water where it flows through a popular recreational area outside of town or by children who skateboard on the embankments. Others apparently involved eating establishments and supermarkets along the wash where flies carried the disease from the water to food.
More ominous, Mr. Zurick said, is the contamination of an aquifer that runs on both sides of the border. Numerous homes and commercial establishments draw water from private wells tapped into the aquifer, he said, including several large warehouses where fruits and vegetables imported from Mexico are iced down and prepared for shipment throughout the world. In October the wells in those warehouses were declared public water supplies, by the county, requiring that they be chlorinated and tested regularly.
Across the border, Ciudad Juarez dumps all of its raw sewage into a canal that parallels the Rio Grande. In towns on the American side of the Rio Grande, the incidence of hepatitis from contaminated well water is so acqute that a recent study in one town, San Elizario, Tex., showed that everyone had been exposed to hepatitis at least once by the time they were 20 years-old.
Human wastes are not the only troublesome substances that have seeped from the Nogales Wash into the aquifer, Mr. Zurick said.
Last year, government and private health experts on both sides of the border repeatedly tested numerous water supplies in the Nogales area and found widespread contamination by the kind of chemicals that are normally found in industrial solvents and that are suspected or known carcinogens.
Although only a few samples showed unsafe levels of contamination, the results showed that the pollution was spreading through the aquifer, said Dick Kamp, director of the Border Ecology Project, a research and policy studies organization that participated in the water study. It seemed to originate in the industrial zone of Nogales, Mexico, where there are many Mexican-owned workshops as well as the American plants
"This is the most extensive study of water pollution done anywhere along the border," Mr. Kamp said, "and what it shows is that we have a problem but that so far we don't know the source of it or its full scope."
More than 1,000 American-owned plants generate hazardous waste but only 30 percent of these have complied with Mexican rules requiring them to file information on how they handle those wastes, according to a report issued last November by the Secretariat of Urban Development and Ecology, the Mexican Government's equivalent of the Environmental Protection Agency. Only 19 percent of the plants using toxic materials could show that they had disposed of wastes properly, the report concluded.
Mexico's rules governing the use of hazardous materials only went into effect in 1988. Most of the regulations are just as strict as they are in the United States, but standards for specific substances and the enforcement mechanisms are still being established.
"The U.S. isn't able to handle hazardous wastes efficiently and so you can't expect it of Mexico where this problem is just now being addressed," said Joe King, president of the Meribah Corporation, an El Paso-based concern that specializes in waste disposal on both sides of the border.
"Protecting the environment has become a political priority in Mexico," Mr. King said, "it has popular support. But the infrastructure is totally missing and building it will involve astronomical costs and a lot of time."

HEADLINE: Between Home and Heaven, a Ball Field

SERIES: Season of Dreams
A periodic visit with one New York City high school baseball team.

BYLINE: By SARA RIMER

 BODY:
The approach of spring in Washington Heights, the Santo Domingo of New York: People fling open their windows, and into their tenement apartments comes the call of merengue, a dozen different variations blasting from speakers in bodegas and record stores and cars cruising Broadway. And they get ready for baseball.
Every morning at about 5, on their way to factory jobs in New Jersey, the neighborhood's Dominican immigrants see 18-year-old Manny Ramirez -- the star of their own George Washington High School baseball team, their own major-league prospect -- doing his roadwork on the steep incline of Fort George Hill. 
 In the afternoons, Wilfredo Resto comes home from his job as a maintenance man at Boricua College to find his 16-year-old son, Alex, waiting to hone his fastball.
And Dolores Ventura, a seamstress in a factory, tells her 15-year-old son, Santos Herrarte, that it is time to quit his job at the discount store on Broadway. Mrs. Ventura supports nine children, and Santos's contribution helped. But he has played ball since he was a 6-year-old in the Dominican Republic.
"I want you to prepare for the baseball season," she says.
Baseball, with all its fleeting promise, connects all kinds of people in Washington Heights -- the commander of the local police precinct, the old man on the corner in the Yankees cap, the coach from Brooklyn, the teen-age girls who call out to Manny Ramirez in the hallways at school.
In Washington Heights, rents are high, jobs are scarce, and some of the streets where drug dealers operate are among the city's most violent. Last year, the 34th Precinct logged 109 of the city's more than 2,200 homicides, more than any other precinct except the 75th in the East New York section of Brooklyn.
In his office, the deputy inspector, Nicholas Estavillo, points to a wall of snapshots of some of the 2,500 guns confiscated in the last five years. Then, smiling, he shows off a mahogany clock inscribed from the Dominican-run F. Alfonso Little League. This is a neighborhood, he says, with four separate Little Leagues.
For fans and players here in Washington Heights, baseball is an escape from the harshness of New York. And it is something else. Dominicans may complain incessantly about the country they left -- the disastrous economy, the devalued peso, the lights that are always going out. But they also talk incessantly of going home. Baseball takes them there. In the Dominican Republic, there are long hours working in the sugar-cane fields and factories -- and then there is baseball.
Javier Del Castillo, 22, is a shortstop from San Pedro de Macoris -- a breeding ground for major league shortstops, including Mr. Del Castillo's cousin, Tony Fernandez of the San Diego Padres. Mr. Del Castillo, a former Washington team captain, is home from college, working at the post office. In free moments at work, he swings a cardboard mailing tube at wadded-up pieces of paper tossed into the air.
"What is there to do on a beautiful, tropical day in the Dominican Republic but play baseball?" he said the other evening. "Playing baseball is going to a new world -- to paradise."
In spring, paradise in Washington Heights can be found on the diamond high on a hill at 192d Street and Audubon Avenue, next to George Washington High, which has 3,000 students. The school is 66 years old, but the ballfield is new artificial turf.
 
Major League Legacy
 You can sit in the stands in the afternoons, surrounded by teen-agers, teachers and parents all cheering -- and cursing -- in two languages, and watch Manny Ramirez, Alex Resto and Santos Herrarte play ball. They and their 17 teammates are the George Washington Trojans, among the best of the city's 94 high school teams. Under their coach, Steve Mandl, 35 and once a left-handed pitching phenom in Bensonhurst, they have won the Manhattan division the last seven years.
Washington was once predominantly Jewish and Irish -- the class of 1941 included a straight-A student named Henry Kissinger -- but now most of its students are Dominican. As at many of New York's academically troubled high schools, staying in school can be hard. About 40 percent of the students are learning English, many while working to help their families.
The team is all Dominican. Most of the players learned the game with milk cartons for gloves and sticks for bats on the same rocky, dirt fields of the Dominican Republic that produced such major leaguers as Tony Fernandez, Alfredo Griffin and George Bell. To spend time with them is to learn about their childhoods, and to discover their Washington Heights. Their odds are long, but it is hard not to get caught up in their baseball dreams.
Alex Resto was accompanied to a practice by his best friend, David Morales. David, who does not play, recounted the moment in February when he first saw the roster posted in the hall.
"To tell you the truth, I jumped for joy," he said. "I told my mother, 'Ma, ma, Alex made it!' "
"I was afraid to try out last year," said Alex, a junior. "But this year my father said, 'Go for it.' Everything has changed for me now. I feel taller."
The 5-foot-8-inch rookie pitcher is preparing for an accounting career. But at night, in the bedroom he shares with his 10-year-old brother, Wilson, on 162d Street, he has this dream: "I'm drafted by the Cardinals, straight from school."
Alex and his teammates can stand in center field at night, after a game, and see the lights of Yankee Stadium off to the southeast. The Stadium is where they want to be in June, when the two best public school teams in the city compete for the championship. Washington made it in 1988, but lost to Cardozo, from Queens, 5-4.
 
Pressures Outside
 A high fence encloses Washington's ballfield. On the other side is the city. Two seasons ago, in May, 17-year-old Nelson Pena, a model student who played in the band at George Washington, was eating at a McDonald's not far from school when a stranger killed him with a .38.
The Washington first baseman, Ralph Gonzalez, was in Nelson Pena's music class. "I missed a day of school and when I came back, he had been shot, and they were collecting money for his funeral," he said. "There's nothing here. Even if you get a good job, you have to worry about being safe."
After practice one day, the star shortstop, Victor Diaz, a senior, explained why he had failed all his classes and missed most of last season. His brother, Frank, had been shot and killed on 177th Street last March at the age of 22. Frank, a pitcher, had been signed by the Montreal Expos, but had broken his arm and come home to drive a United Parcel Service truck.
"I just want to get out of New York," said Victor, who is called Speedy Gonzalez. He hurls himself around the bases so fast he sometimes misses them. "I don't have time to touch the base," he tells Coach Mandl.
Victor and Ralph, like most of the players, say they know drug dealers; some say they have been invited into the trade. "You see a 15-year-old kid driving a Mercedes-Benz and you get tempted," Ralph said. "But that's not the way to go. I play basketball, football and baseball. I go bowling. In New York, you have to play sports. Otherwise, the streets get you."
 
Things to Count On
 On the ballfield inside the fence, some things seem certain.
Victor Diaz will steal bases. He stole five in Thursday's exhibition.
Manny Ramirez, who plays center field and third base, batted .633 last season and is rated the best high school player in the city and one of the best in the country (he made USA Today's top 25), will hit balls out of the park. He hit 16 homers last season.
His teammates say they admire Manny, the son of a cab driver, for not acting cocky. But he would like to be identified in the newspaper as the Hitman. The big-league scouts are following the Hitman; so is Washington Heights. Even the neighborhood's greatest baseball success story -- a Panamanian immigrant named Rod Carew who graduated from Washington in 1964 and was recently elected to the Hall of Fame -- says he has heard of Manny Ramirez's bat.
Former Washington players still chasing big-league dreams will return. Roberto Ceballos, 20, who pitched in that championship game at Yankee Stadium, was there for the first exhibition this season.
Roberto walked out on the team his senior year. "The streets got to me," he said. He ended up spending the season in the hospital after being hit by a Lincoln. He says he wants to go to college and try for a comeback.
In the stands, his eyes were glued to the player in red shoes -- Manny Ramirez. "I just came to see my boy Manuel get a hot shot over the fence," he said. "I want him to get drafted. He's the one who has the best shot of all of us."
 
The Coach's Role
 Coach Mandl, calm and encouraging, will be flashing signs from the sidelines. On hot days, he will be sipping an orange juice and milk concoction called a morir sonando ("to die dreaming"), brought to him by Prisca Alvarez. Mrs. Alvarez is the mother of Javier Del Castillo. Her son -- who went to college on a baseball scholarship and hopes to be signed after graduation -- will be there to help the coach.
Washington will make mistakes. "Everyone thinks because they're Dominican they're born superstars," Coach Mandl said. "People don't know how hard they have to work."
Some public high school teams, like Cardozo and Canarsie in Brooklyn, left last week for spring training in Florida. Even with all the candy they sold, the Washington players can't think of Florida. With a $1,500 budget, they're already running out of balls. The coach is understandably ambivalent about all the balls Manny hits out in practice.
Washington's spring training was calisthenics on a wet and windy ballfield in New York City, hours of fielding grounders and batting practice.
Coach Mandl, who also teaches gym, gets paid about $3,000 for the season. He is not paid for his winter baseball club, open to the entire school and the neighborhood.
He is there for the baseball. Nineteen seasons ago, he was going to be Bensonhurst's next Sandy Koufax. But his future in baseball disappeared before he turned 17, when he ripped apart his knee playing basketball. Seven years ago, after working as a bank messenger and a substitute gym teacher, he became the Washington coach. "For a few years there, I didn't want to have anything to do with baseball," he said. "It hurt too much. When I came here, I got baseball back in my life."
The first exhibition was on the first day of spring. At his post inside the school entrance, Jose Estevez, a 26-year-old security guard, looked to seasons long gone. "I used to be a pitcher, back in Santo Domingo," he said.
At 3 P.M., in the clubhouse, Santos Herrarte put on his black-and-orange uniform for the first time. A year ago, he was in Santiago. Now, he anxiously approached the freshly painted blue wall where Coach Mandl had posted the lineup.
It said: "No. 7 -- Santos Herrarte -- second base." The rookie, who had been shy during practice, shouted joyfully -- "Herrarte!"
The rookie and his teammates grabbed their gloves and ran onto the field. Washington beat Cardozo, 5-1. In six exhibition games so far, their record is 4-1, with one tie.
Opening day is April 11.

SECTION: Section 1; Part 1; Page 7; Column 1; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 682 words

HEADLINE: More Cubans Try to Leave as Hard Times Deepen

BYLINE: By HOWARD W. FRENCH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MIAMI, March 26

 BODY:
Faced with ever-tighter austerity measures, Cubans are seeking a way off their island in numbers not seen since the Mariel boatlift of 1980.
Cuba has been hard hit by decreases in trade and aid from its former East-bloc partners. Since October, the State Department says, 70,000 Cubans have applied for visas at the United States Interests Section in Havana, twice as many as in the entire previous year. 
 In one reflection of the increased desperation of Cubans seeking to leave, the United States Coast Guard said that in the waters off Florida this year it has already assisted 162 Cubans who were seeking to flee the island on vessels ranging from rubber inner tubes and makeshift rafts to small boats.
Before last year, the Coast Guard said, only 40 to 50 Cubans sought to reach the United States by sea each year. Officials said empty vessels from Cuba, presumably those of asylum-seekers who died in the passage, are now often found as well.
In addition to the increased illicit flow of ordinary Cubans out of the country by means like these, the island has in recent weeks seen growing numbers of high-profile defectors. Last week, a major in the Cuban Air Force piloted his MIG-23 fighter to Key West, where he was granted asylum.
A few days later, the prominent program director of Radio Progreso in Cuba, Romel Iglesias, announced his defection in a radio interview during a visit to Miami. Mr. Iglesias, one of two humorists on Cuban radio, said he had found it impossible to perform in the climate of deepening hardship.
"Clearly, what we are seeing is a reaction to the deteriorating economic situation in Cuba," said William M. LeoGrande, a professor of political science at American University in Washington who studies Cuban affairs.
 "The last time we saw increases like this was in 1979, and that was the prelude to Mariel," Mr. LeoGrande said, referring to the six-month boatlift of 125,000 Cubans to the United States. He said that now the Cuban economy is worst it has been in the 32 years of the revolution.
In the last year, Cubans have faced growing power cuts, shortages of food and clothing and increased rationing. Last winter, city workers began rotating shifts in farm jobs to boost sagging food production. And in one of the starkest illustrations of the austerity, the country has recently resorted to mass substitution of bicycles for automobiles because of a scarcity of oil.
While President Fidel Castro has promised in many informal pep talks and speeches that those hardships would ultimately prove beneficial by "testing our ability to overcome," the sharp decline in living standards seems mostly to have spurred interest in flight from the island.
Analysts say the Government has pursued a two-track approach to the phenomenon: employing increasingly virulent language against high-profile defectors, while lowering age barriers and other restrictions on emigration by ordinary citizens to reduce social tensions resulting from shortages and other economic problems.
Denouncing Mr. Iglesias for his defection, a Cuban radio commentary said, "Because of fear of danger, rats abandon ship."
To emigrate or travel abroad legally, Cubans must obtain an exit visa from their Government, a process that human rights experts say has long been fraught with political considerations. While gradually changing its emigration policies, the Cuba Government has complained of over-selectiveness by the United States in according visas.
The State Department rejects these accusations. Of the approximately 38,000 Cubans who applied for non-immigrant visas to the United States in 1990, a spokesman said, about 34,000 received them. By act of Congress in 1966, Cubans who came here on tourist visas, unlike people from other countries, are automatically allowed to apply for residence after one year in the United States.
"Cubans are treated very leniently when it comes to immigration to the United States," a State Department official said. "Other countries cannot be expected to set aside their laws just to please the Cuban Government."

HEADLINE: Kennett Square Journal;
A Corner Of Mexico Takes Root Far Away

BYLINE: By FELICITY BARRINGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: KENNETT SQUARE, Pa.

 BODY:
Jesus Pizano was 14 years old and had the equivalent of $5 and a pack of gum in his pocket when he left the arid highland plains of central Mexico for southeastern Pennsylvania. Here, in long cinder block buildings, amid the gentle slopes and less gentle scents of Chester County farmland, he and the other men of Moroleon pick mushrooms.
"My uncle worked in mushrooms for 15 years," said Mr. Pizano, who is now 24. "I always thought I would come here and pick mushrooms." 
 His journey to this rural area brings to life a main finding of the 1990 census. Mexican immigrants who come to the United States to pick crops are more and more establishing permanent settlements: here in Kennett Square, in tobacco country in Virginia and North Carolina, and among the truck farms of Minnesota.
"There is a dispersion going on," said David Simcox, director of the Center for Immigration Studies, a Washington-based research organization. "You find Hispanics where you didn't expect to find them in the past, in rural areas where they didn't exist before."
In the process, the ethnic makeup of these communities is undergoing a steady, sometimes swift transformation. Schools scramble to find Spanish-speaking teachers for programs teaching English as a second language. Tortillas appear in grocery stores next to apple butter. Kennett Square's police force now has a Spanish speaker; neighboring New Garden Township (population 5,403) has two new Mexican restaurants and a grocery that caters to Mexican-Americans.
While most of the nation's 32 million Hispanic residents are in Texas and California, or urban areas like New York City and Chicago, Kennett Square offers a classic example of the spread of the country's expanding Hispanic minorities to new areas.
The century-old mushroom industry here, which provides virtually year-round employment, has helped created a unique racial and ethnic mosaic. In the early part of the century, Italians worked for Quakers until they began to buy their own farms. The Italians first employed blacks, then white Tennesseans escaping the poverty of Appalachia and then Puerto Ricans fleeing the unemployment on their own island.
But over the past 15 years most of the Puerto Ricans have been supplanted by the young men of central Mexico. The turnover has been such that more than 75 percent of the industry's 3,500 workers, and virtually all the pickers, are now Mexican, said Jamie Ciarocchi, chairman of the American Mushroom Institute and an owner of the Modern Farms mushroom operation.
Pickers earn $1.10 a basket, and a good picker can fill seven or eight baskets an hour. Many work up to 60 hours a week.
Census figures for New Garden Township show one resident in five is Hispanic, up from 15.2 percent in 1980. The vast majority of these are from Moroleon and other towns in the spare landscape of mesas and cactus 100 miles north of Mexico City. Like Mr. Pizano, most crossed the Rio Grande illegally and most left their wives and children behind.
After passage of the 1986 Immigration Reform and Control Act, which made it possible for some illegal aliens to obtain legal status, more than 2,000 agricultural workers were legalized in Chester County, almost all of them in the mushroom business, an immigration service report said.
"When I came, I never dreamed legalization was a reality," said Raymondo Ayllon, 28, a mushroom worker who left Guanajuato in 1980. "I didn't have long-range plans. I planned to stay till I got deported. Now I feel I can make plans for the future. As soon as I can bring my family, I will."
No one here claims to know the name of the first Mexican mushroom picker to find out about Chester County, or how the bridge between Moroleon and Kennett Square was built. Asked how they heard of Pennsylvania and mushrooming, worker after worker said "my uncle," or "my brother" or "a friend."
After workers become legal residents, they may petition the Government to have their families join them, and day after day Sister Jane Houtman, a Roman Catholic nun, sits in a small trailer on a back road as Mexicans knock on her door seeking help in filling out Government forms.
Many of the workers, like Mr. Pizano, speak little or no English. They hover around the edges of local life, eating in their new restaurants, listening to Spanish-language cable television, more a colony of Moroleon than a part of Kennett Square.
Sheila Druley, director of La Comunidad Hispana, a local service organization, hopes to change that by having her group help the Mexicans do everything from getting a driver's license to getting their children into schools.
Some of the Puerto Ricans who remain are also helping the Mexicans make the transition. Palmira Matos, a picker's daughter, works as a bilingual instructor in the migrant education program at Kennett High School, but she says she has been unable to break down the invisible wall between immigrants and natives.
"I don't have a lot of friends" among the non-Hispanic students, said one of her students, 15-year-old Maria Jacinto. "But I haven't heard anyone talk about conflict either."
Another student, Roberto Alvarez, 13, said, with neither regret nor pleasure, "I never had an American friend."

Jesus Pizano, an immigrant from Moroleon, Mexico, picked mushrooms at the Kalolin mushroom farm in Kennett Square. (Bill Cramer for The New York Times)

SECTION: Section 1; Part 1; Page 12; Column 1; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 702 words

BYLINE: By DAVID BINDER, Special to The New York Times; Albanians Vote Today, and Opposition Presses On

DATELINE: TIRANA, Albania, March 30

 BODY:
The people of Albania, blinking in the first political daylight they have been exposed to in almost five decades, will vote on Sunday in multiparty elections organized by the governing Communist Party.
The outcome is unclear. No polls have been taken. But the Communists, led by President Ramiz Alia, are under pressure from a popular democratic movement launched by Tirana University students in December. 
 While the Communists hold their final election rallies for relatively small numbers of the party faithful behind the doors of meeting halls, the newly formed Democratic Party headed by a charismatic cardiologist, Dr. Sali Berisha, has convened its supporters in the open, drawing about 90,000 to its last rally in the capital on Friday. Tirana has a population of 300,000.
These are the first contested elections here since 1923, but in that year Albania still had a semifeudal society and the candidates were chosen mainly by clans.
 
Push for Freedoms
 Albania is Europe's poorest country and it has been governed since 1944 by a Stalinist regime formed by Enver Hoxha. He died in 1985 after naming Mr. Alia to succeed him. Last May, plainly concerned by the fall of Communist governments across Eastern Europe in 1989, President Alia opened what he called a process of "democratization."
But a huge number of Albanians, disgusted with the Communists' disastrous economic policies and abuse of basic human rights, soon made it clear that they wanted their freedom far faster than the Government was were prepared to allow.
They stormed foreign embassies by the thousands to gain asylum. They commandeered boats to cross the Adriatic to Italy. They demanded and obtained the opening of churches and mosques closed in 1967 as part of an official campaign against all religious worship.
Dozens of demonstrators were killed in clashes with the police, but the protests compelled the release of 329 political prisoners, some of whom had been jailed for as long as 30 years. They also forced Mr. Alia to sack a dozen Cabinet ministers and form a new Government last month.
In the same period, Mr. Alia sought to improve his image here by opening diplomatic relations with the United States, inviting American and other foreign observers to monitor the elections and declaring Albania's willingness to adhere to human-rights conventions under the 1975 Helsinki agreement.
But those and other concessions by the Communist Government may have come too grudgingly and too slowly to give the 65-year-old President a strong edge in the balloting, especially among the young, who make up perhaps one million of the 1.8 million electorate.
Mr. Alia's stump speeches indicate a sense of doubt about his party's chances. Repeatedly he said, "We made mistakes." On Friday at his first news conference, he said, "I am not a dictator," adding that the Communists would accept defeat "if that is the will of the people."
In contrast to the polite applause accorded to Mr. Alia and other Communist candidates for the 250-seat Parliament, huge crowds of Albanians cheered wildly for the candidates of the Democratic Party in the cities and even in remote mountain villages.
Near the port of Durres, four young Albanian soldiers walking on the highway this afternoon were carrying Democratic Party placards, flashing "V" signs to passing motorists.
The Communists still appear to have some strength in the countryside, which the opposition parties have had difficulty penetrating. Sixty percent of the Albanians live in rural communities.
The Democratic Party was founded in December by Dr. Berisha with Gramoz Pashko, an economics professor, together with a few student leaders and a worker. Other parties have also sprung up but have not gained widespread support.
The Alia Government has provided the opposition parties with space for campaign headquarters, some telephones and some automobiles in this country where private car ownership was banned until this month.
The Democrats have pledged to bring Albania "into Europe" and to create genuine "freedom and liberty." They have also hinted broadly that American economic assistance for Albania would be generous if they win.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Albanians vote today in their first contested elections since 1923. Outside a polling station set up in Tirana, citizens gathered yesterday to read information about voting procedure. (Associated Press)

THE ECONOMY
 
Albania does not release economic figures, but by Western estimates it is the poorest country in Europe. In 1981, the World Bank said Albania's per capita gross national product was $820. But the country is self-sufficient in grain and possesses considerable mineral resources, including chromium ore, the country's leading mineral export. Albania's hydroelectric potential is being expanded for increased export of electricity to neighboring countries.
 
THE PEOPLE
 
Ethnic Albanians make up about 97 percent of the country's 3.5 million people (based on the 1981 census). Before the Communist takeover in 1944, Albania was 70 percent Orthodox and 10 percent Catholic. In 1967, the Government outlawed religion, closed all houses of worship and declared Albania by law "the first atheist state in the world." The average monthly income is $60.
 
GEOGRAPHY
 
The country is 11,000 square miles, slightly larger than Maryland. Aside from coastal lowland along the Adriatic, the country is mountainous and rugged. It convers 210 miles from north to south and 90 miles at its widest point.
 
HISTORY
 
Albania, then called Illyria, was part of the Roman Empire from 176 B.C. until A.D. 395. For the next thousand years it was ruled by the Byzantines, Huns, Bulgarians and Slavs before coming under the control of the Ottoman Turks, Albania gained independence in 1912 but was occupied by Italy from 1914 until 1920. It became a republic in 1925, but was taken over by Italy again in 1939 and occupied in 1943 by the German Army. A group led by Communist was the strongest of the wartime resistance groups, taking power in 1944. The Communists began relaxing their control in 1989.

LENGTH: 1316 words

HEADLINE: The World;
'Clean Win' in the War With Iraq Drifts Into a Bloody Aftermath

BYLINE: By PATRICK E. TYLER

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
AS the nightmare continued for the 18 million people of Iraq last week, President Bush sought to put a period at the end of the Persian Gulf war, but it would not end. Indeed, the United States' "clean win," as Gen. Colin L. Powell, Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, had termed it, was being soiled by the bloodbath it had unleashed inside Iraq.
A victorious George Bush, who had exhorted the Iraqi masses to overthrow Saddam Hussein in the wake of his defeat on the battlefield, decided after a long meeting with his senior advisers on Tuesday that there had to be a limit to United States intervention in Iraq's internal affairs. 
 After having pointedly warned Baghdad not to use poison gas or combat aircraft anywhere in Iraq, warnings that had encouraged the rebellion, Mr. Bush pulled up short when Iraq continued to fly combat helicopters against the insurgents. The rebels said they could not stand up to that kind of attack, and asked to use captured Iraqi jets to fight back. Mr. Bush again said no.
The end of Mr. Bush's ambiguity toward the fate of the rebels spawned criticism that he was consigning the Iraqi people to murderous retribution, something the West was not willing to tolerate in Eastern Europe.
The situation was only complicated when the allied commander, Gen. H. Norman Schwarzkopf, wondered aloud in a television interview whether history would second-guess Mr. Bush's decision to stop the war just as he, General Schwarzkopf, was hours away from annihilating the Iraqi units that are now engaged in bringing dissident towns and cities to heel.
Thousands upon thousands of Iraqi refugees streamed into the American-held territory in southern Iraq and they reported that more refugee convoys were on the way. They included Iraqi Army deserters who said they had refused orders to gun down women and children, and civilians who told of unspeakable atrocities against members of their families.
The Pentagon spokesman, Pete Williams, said Iraqi soldiers were still surrendering faster than the allies could send home the soldiers taken prisoner during the fighting. In addition to more than 60,000 Iraqis in prisoner of war camps, there were 25,000 civilian and military refugees in the American occupation zone, with the prospect that more violence and social breakdown inside the country could flush many times that number toward the borders.
In the north, a ragtag army of Kurdish rebels that effectively controlled Iraqi Kurdistan prepared for a battle for survival against the Republican Guard, which reclaimed Kirkuk after it fell to the rebels earlier in the week, albeit tenuously.
Some analysts wondered whether a harsh Iraqi crackdown in Kurdistan would trigger the same kind of exodus that occurred in September 1988, when Baghdad sought to crush Kurdish forces that had collaborated with Iran during the Iran-Iraq war. During that campaign of repression 100,000 Kurds, fearing death and chemical weapons, fled into neighboring Turkey and Iran.
Now, a month after the shooting stopped, as the United Nations prepares to vote on a formal cease-fire resolution, a number of Iraq watchers wonder if this is the beginning of the end for the rebellion and for the return of stability to Iraq.
More likely, it is just the end of the beginning of a long and vexing Persian Gulf crisis.
"What we have now is worse than what we had when Iraq was in Kuwait," said Christine Moss Helms, an Iraq scholar who has advised both the White House and the Pentagon during the crisis. "We have a situation where we entered the region to put down the Iraqi threat, and we spent $80 to $100 billion to do it, we ejected the Iraqis from Kuwait, but what did we get for it? We still don't have Saddam Hussein out of power and now we have this deteriorating situation on the frontier between the Persians, the Arabs and the Turks."
The core issue for Ms. Helms and for a number of other Mideast analysts was whether the United States and its allies went too far in devastating Iraq's armed forces and key elements of the country's economy, encouraging a premature uprising that may be doomed.
Referring to the Bush Administration, Ms. Helms said, "I think they'll lose either way: the longer they stay, anti-Americanism will increase throughout the Arab and Muslim world and if they leave, all these forces, rolling on their own momentum," will encourage an extended low-intensity war within Iraq where none of the contenders for power will be strong enough to prevail and where interested outsiders in Iran, Turkey and Syria will play behind the scenes to protect their own interests.
"If Iraq continues to disintegrate," Ms. Helms said, "it may be unstoppable, it may have a momentum of its own and the implications of the fragmentation of Iraq will go far beyond its borders" and affect the stability of Iran, Turkey and Syria.
Whatever the implications, it seemed certain that Mr. Bush, despite last week's White House pronouncement on neutrality, could not escape involvement in Iraq's internal affairs. To the contrary, Mr. Bush is set upon a course both to pressure Iraq's internal political structure and hobble its oil-exporting capabilities and to control the flow of basic technology into the country.
 Military power by its nature creates demands to use it, and as long as Mr. Hussein is in Baghdad, under pressure economically as instability reigns in much of his nation, the prospects for future crises in the region will remain.
 
A Continued Presence
 The White House and Pentagon have made clear that the moment a cease fire is signed, about 100,000 United States Army troops from the VII Corps will pull out as soon as possible and relinquish their occupation responsibilities to a United Nations observer force and Red Cross officials.
But even if American forces leave Iraq in coming weeks, the United States will continue to be the dominant military power in the Persian Gulf through the military alliance that Mr. Bush is erecting with his Arab allies. The United States Central Command is rushing to complete its blueprint for a new Arab-American security force, heavily dependent on American air and naval power. And the State Department and Pentagon are working on a long-term strategy to squeeze Iraq economically -- at a cost of more suffering for its people -- until Mr. Hussein is forced from power.
This strategy is not without its contradictions.
Mr. Bush says he has nothing against the people of Iraq, yet even after a United Nations team reported that allied bombers had returned the country to a "pre-industrial age," Mr. Bush is in effect asking the Iraqi people, who had little to say about Mr. Hussein's rise to power to begin with, to either challenge his rule at great personal risk or suffer the consequences of pre-industrial deprivation extended indefinitely.
The prevailing view among the United States intelligence community and some Arab allies in the Mideast is that Mr. Hussein probably cannot survive for more than 12 months. This assessment may have been one factor encouraging Mr. Bush to stay at arms' length from what may be a transitory uprising and instead make a play to influence the succession in Baghdad, if there is one. He will try to persuade the leadership class in the army and in the ruling Arab Baath Socialist Party to make the necessary change.
But it is always dangerous to bet that you can change a regime in the Arab world, where tribe, family and the modern technologies of control conspire to perpetuate rule. The Reagan Administration became obsessed with driving Col. Muammar el-Qaddafi from power in Libya, only to be repeatedly frustrated.
As Mr. Bush prepares to cement his anti-Hussein policy in place, he will have to differentiate between the pursuit of that objective and its impact on the people of Iraq, who will have to live with the consequences.

HEADLINE: PRO HOCKEY;
Penguins' First Title Is Cause to Celebrate

BYLINE: By JOE LAPOINTE

 BODY:
Most of the time, winning a division title in the National Hockey League's regular season is a little like qualifying to buy life insurance. It's good to have it, but there isn't much cause for celebration. Even local sportscasters sometimes announce such a hometown achievement after the Final Four basketball preview and the baseball scores from spring training.
For the Pittsburgh Penguins, their winning of the Patrick Division title last week was treated somewhat differently. When the team clinched by defeating Detroit on Wednesday night, players donned colorful T-shirts that announced their success. 
 A thousand fans greeted the team plane the next day at the airport. Flowers, balloons and telegrams arrived in the Civic Arena locker room. A long line formed at the box office window to buy the remaining playoff tickets.
The Penguins haven't yet attained the stature of the Steel Curtain Pittsburgh Steelers team, which once dominated the National Football League, or even of the Family of Pirates, which was one of baseball's best. And the division title means only that the Penguins get a playoff spot -- along with 15 other teams -- and the home ice in the first two rounds.
But after 24 seasons of mismanagement, financial crisis and early spring vacations, the hockey team's first pennant has given the citizens who follow hockey something to shout about.
"Not one banner or pennant in the building," said Bob Johnson, the coach. "This is a start."
On the blackboard inside the locker room, somebody had written: "Even the mighty oak tree begins with a tiny acorn. (We have the seed)."
After they play the Rangers tonight at Madison Square Garden in the regular-season finale, the Penguins will open the Stanley Cup playoffs Wednesday night at home against the New Jersey Devils. It will be only the second time in the last nine seasons that they have been part of the tournament.
A major reason for the turnaround is Johnson, who is part of an intriguing collection of Penguin people who were not with the team a year ago. A 60-year-old American, Johnson is finishing his first season as Pittsburgh coach after spending the previous three as executive director of USA Hockey, which supervised the sport at the amateur level up to the Olympic team. He is the oldest coach in the N.H.L.
"He has such enthusiasm for the game," said Tom Barrasso, the first-string goalie. "He comes to the rink and enjoys himself. There is never a stale day for him. That makes it entertaining for all of us."
 One of his players, Jaromir Jagr, is a talented and emotional 19-year-old rookie from Czechoslovakia and is the youngest player in the league. His point total of 56 is among the best for first-year players.
"He's a challenge," Johnson said, "because he is a young player. He's getting better all the time."
In between these extremes are Ulf Samuelsson, Grant Jennings and Ron Francis, obtained earlier this month from Hartford, and Bryan Trottier, a 16-year veteran who stopped here this season on his way from the Islanders to the Hall of Fame.
Among the holdovers from last season are Mark Recchi, a right wing who has emerged as the team's top scorer with 113 points; Paul Coffey, a defenseman with 93 points who remains one of the league's best skaters and attackers at his position, and Kevin Stevens, a left wing who is having his best season with 85 points.
And then, of course, there is Mario Lemieux, the best player on the team, one of the greatest players of this era, and one who has often been overlooked while laboring through a star-crossed career.
 At midseason, the Penguins were holding on to fourth place. Their surge to the top coincided with both the demise of the Rangers and the return of Lemieux, who missed almost an entire calendar year with back pain, back surgery and a post-operative back infection.
Back in the lineup for only 25 games this season, Lemieux had 19 goals and 26 assists after yesterday's 4-4 tie with Philadelphia. He scored three goals in a 3-1 victory over the Flyers on Tuesday night but missed Wednesday's title clinching because his left eye was stitched and swollen shut where a puck hit it the night before. Had it hit an inch lower, it might have resulted in another career-threatening injury.
Despite scoring titles, a most-valuable-player award and All-Star status, Lemieux has played for seven years in the long shadow of Wayne Gretzky and, recently, alongside the glare of Brett Hull. With the Penguins so often missing the playoffs, Lemieux has not received the recognition that comes from the post-season spotlight.
Physically, Lemieux still feels occasional pain in the back and says he is still on medication. As recently as last autumn, he wondered if he would ever play again.
"It was certainly a tough period in my life," he said. "I certainly had some doubts about playing hockey again.
"I couldn't walk for a couple months. It was pretty tough. I didn't expect the infection. It was worse than the surgery. It was very painful. I had a lot of support from a lot of different people: my family, my friends, my doctor, my teammates. That's what you need when you are down."
 During the post-game celebration Wednesday, Jagr approached Lemieux with a photographer in tow and asked him to pose for a picture with arms around each other's shoulders.
Jagr is of similar size and plays with many of the same skills. His hair is long and curly like Lemieux's and he wears the number 68 while Lemieux wears 66. "He is my favorite," Jagr said.
Jagr was the first draft choice of Craig Patrick, the general manager who replaced Tony Esposito last season. Patrick, the former general manager of the Rangers, was the assistant coach and assistant general manager of the 1980 United States Olympic team, which won the gold medal. One of his first hires at the executive level was Scotty Bowman as director of player development and recruitment.
Like Johnson, who has had success at the high school, college and professional levels, Bowman came with an impressive track record. He coached the Montreal Canadiens to four consecutive Stanley Cup titles in the 1970's.
The division title, Patrick said, "is one, small step." But sustaining their regular-season success through the playoffs may be difficult for the Penguins. Their division is the weakest in the league and their record would rank no better than third in the Norris or Smythe, second in the Adams. Although their goal total of 335 is best in the league, they have given up 295, more than all but Quebec, Vancouver and Toronto.
"People are going to realize we've got a shot," Stevens said, speaking of the Stanley Cup. "It's not going to be one of those teams that is a fluke. We worked our way to the playoffs and we're going to work our way right through them. Wednesday night, we're going to be raring to go."

HEADLINE: The Region;
Albany, Squeezed, Puts Pressure on Local Governments

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH KOLBERT

DATELINE: ALBANY

 BODY:
WITH its vast office complexes and almost 200,000 employees, New York State government often seems like an empire unto itself. But despite its imposing size, the government is, for the most part, just a way station for tax money. The state collects it from all corners of the state, then redistributes it to counties and villages and school districts according to elaborate -- and often manipulated -- formulas.
This year, as Gov. Mario M. Cuomo and legislative leaders wrangle over how to close a $6 billion budget gap, it is the state's function as middleman for its municipalities that is most sorely being challenged. By the time New York finally resolves its budget problems, a process that is expected to extend for several years, many experts predict that the state's role as broker will have changed substantially, as local governments are forced to rely on their own resources. 
 But already, as this exercise haltingly gets under way -- the state budget, due tomorrow, will be late again for the seventh year in a row -- many groups are questioning whether the cure will prove worse than the disease.
"State aid is a leveling device," said Cynthia Green, an analyst for the Citizens Budget Commission, a watchdog group financed by businesses. "For those communities that can't adequately finance needed services, the state has the capacity to help. It has a broader tax base, and a wider range of residents with different incomes to tax. So to the degree that the state cuts aid to local governments, equity will probably be lost."
 
Angry Local Officials

 
 To close the state's budget gap, Governor Cuomo has proposed spending cuts totaling $4.5 billion. About three quarters of these cuts, or $3.3 billion, come out of money that would have gone to local governments, which last year received in aid roughly two thirds of the $29 billion the state collected in taxes. The proposed cuts include a $428 million reduction in unrestricted aid to localities and an $875 million cut in aid to school districts.
To close the remainder of the gap, the Governor has proposed a 10-cent-a-gallon increase in the state's gasoline tax and a diverse assortment of other "revenue enhancements." But he has staunchly resisted calls from many quarters to raise the income tax to offset some of the cuts, saying that this would hurt the state's competitive position.
Confronted by hordes of angry local officials, Cuomo administration officials have adamantly maintained that municipal governments can cut their budgets just as the officials plan to cut the state's.
"There is going to be a shock of realization at the local level," the Governor's budget director, Dall W. Forsythe, said. "But I don't think the answer will be higher taxes at the local level."
Local officials, however, say there is a limit to how much they can cut spending and still pick up trash, provide police protection and educate children. By refusing to raise state taxes, they say, the Governor is simply forcing them to raise local taxes to make up the difference.
"The men and women in the State Capitol have as big a role in raising local taxes as the men and women who run local governments," said Edwin L. Crawford, executive director of the New York State Association of Counties.
At this point, it is too early to tell how much of the proposed cuts in state aid will translate into the reduced spending the Governor has suggested, and how much into the higher taxes local officials say they fear. But at least some increase in local taxes seems unavoidable. New York City, for example, has already announced plans to increase income and property taxes to make up for some of the loss in state aid. And with New York City's taxes so high already, many experts are questioning whether a city tax increase won't prove more damaging than a state tax increase.
"It makes more sense to raise the state income tax than the New York City income tax," said Steven D. Gold, director of the Center for the Study of the States at the State University of New York in Albany. While Governor Cuomo has resisted an increase in the state's income tax on the grounds that it would harm the state's economy, Mr. Gold and other experts say that an increase in the city's income tax is likely to drive out even more businesses than an increase at the state level.
"New York State's income tax is the fourth or fifth highest in the country, depending on what income level you're at," Mr. Gold said. "But New York City's income tax is just in the stratosphere." To the state's top tax rate of 7.87 percent, New York City adds another 3.9 percent, and it plans to increase this to 4.3 percent.
The question of what will happen to local taxes as a result of the state budget is particularly significant in New York because the state already relies so heavily on local taxes. While most states raise roughly half of their revenues -- excluding Federal funds -- from state taxes, in 1989, the last year for which data are available, New York raised only 39 percent of its revenues through state taxes. Over the years, this proportion has been falling; in 1988, New York raised 40 percent of its revenues through state taxes.
When one looks closely at the figures, one sees that it is local taxes, not state taxes that are responsible for New York's reputation as a high tax state.
For each $100 a person earned in New York last year, roughly $7.55 was paid in state taxes, less than would have been paid in 20 other states. But the New York State resident also paid $8.01 in local taxes for every $100 earned. That was more than a resident of any other state would have paid. Taken together, New York's state and local taxes are second only to Alaska, which raises most of its revenues from oil companies.
Cuomo administration officials say that New York's high local tax rates raise questions about the efficiency of local governments. But local officials say that much of the responsibility for the tax rates lies with the state, which sets requirements for local-government spending in such areas as education, criminal justice and medical care. New York, they point out, is one of only a handful of states that requires its municipalities to pick up a share of Medicaid and welfare costs.
"Sixty percent of our budgets are mandated," Mr. Crawford of the Association of Counties said.
In the clamor of name-calling and fault-finding that passes for state budget negotiations in Albany, policy questions about the appropriate relationship between state and local government are often lost in the tumult. But these questions are emerging as the central issue facing the Governor and the Legislature over the coming decade, and resolving them may take almost that long.
"There may well be some real changes that are made in the structure of state and local taxes," Mr. Forsythe, the Governor's budget director, said. "But I think this is a debate that's going to go on for some years."
