LENGTH: 1627 words

HEADLINE: CONNECTICUT Q&A: WILLIAM J. CIBES JR.;
The Budget's Got (Gasp!) an Income Tax

BYLINE: By ROBERT A. HAMILTON

 BODY:
THE first budget from Gov. Lowell P. Weicker Jr. is a $7.48 billion spending plan that would reduce the sales tax from 8 to 4.25 percent and eliminate the corporate surcharge and taxes on capital gains, interest and dividends. That plan however, would impose an income tax on wages, a tax long resisted by many state legislators, at a flat rate of 6 percent.
Mr. Weicker, a lifelong Republican who ran as an independent last year, caused a stir in the political community when he picked William J. Cibes Jr., a Democrat, as his budget chief. 
 Mr. Cibes, whose formal title is Secretary of Policy and Management, had mounted his own campaign for governor last year, with tax reform, including an income tax on wages, as the main plank in his platform. A professor of political science on leave from Connecticut College, he is a 10-year veteran of the legislature who was co-chairman of the Finance, Revenue and Bonding Committee.
In an interview at his office in Hartford, Mr. Cibes answered questions about tax revision. Here are some excerpts from the conversation.
Q. Has your job of building support for the budget been made any more difficult by the press hounding Governor Weicker for his statement that adding an income tax in a recession was like throwing gasoline on a fire?
A. I think of the three candidates, Weicker was the one who most clearly said everything was on the table. He most clearly said he didn't know the full dimensions of the problem. I think what he was saying was, I am not for an income tax. And I can tell you he was not for, and probably still is not for, an income tax.
It's just that his conclusion, after looking at the numbers, was that the other alternatives would not work. If you just looked at an income tax, it would certainly not be good for the economy. It's got to be part of an overall, balanced program, and the Governor's program certainly is that.
 Q. Is an income tax the only answer this year?
 A. It's never been a panacea, and it isn't this year. It's part of a whole package the Governor has developed. The reduction of the sales tax, elimination of the capital gains, dividends and interest and the deficit financing are all important parts of it. It all fits together. An income tax is what makes possible all of the reductions in taxes which provide for economic development.
 Q. In your own gubernatorial campaign, you advocated a progressive income tax rate. How do you feel about a flat rate?
 A. A lot of people overlook that with the large exemption, $25,000 for a couple, even a flat rate has a fairly progressive effective rate. A family making $30,000, for example, pays taxes at 6 percent on $5,000, or $300, for an effective tax rate of 1 percent. A family making $50,000, making the same computation, the effective tax rate is 3 percent. At $100,000, the effective tax rate is 4.5 percent.
There is a fairly steep progression built into the tax rate because of the large exemption. I should also point out that you have to produce a certain amount of money, and if you move to the progressive rate I was proposing last summer, from 4 to 8 percent, the numbers don't work out.
Q. Things have changed that quickly?
 A. Oh, yes. When I was campaigning last summer, I talked about a maximum $600 million deficit. Now we're talking about $2.4 billion. Things have changed substantially. And a flat tax also makes the state competitive in terms of attracting those people likely to make the decision to move a company to Connecticut. A top rate of 6 percent is where you can go and remain competitive with New York and Massachusetts and Rhode Island.
 Q. Will there have to be more tinkering with the tax structure over the next few years?
 A. One of the nice things about a democracy is those who have been elected can make adjustments in response to the demands of their constituents. I expect any tax structure is going to be adjusted at the margins, just as the first one has been for 40 or 50 years or however long we've had a sales tax. I see continued tinkering to adjust it to what people feel is important, but if you adjust it in a major way, you're going to be losing revenue. You don't wave a magic wand and come up with revenue.
I've been using the "Calvin and Hobbes" cartoon in which Calvin said math was like a religion, it takes a leap of faith to believe you can take two numbers and add them and get one new number. Well, I think math atheism has afflicted a lot of people in Connecticut. You have to look at the numbers, and the numbers don't add up. But the Governor's package makes the numbers add up.
 Q. Was it grating to be the only candidate last year saying tax reform was necessary, and now it appears that will happen?
 A. No. There have been a lot of people who have looked at the revenue structure in this state over the years, and they have argued, and argued aggressively, that things need to be changed. They feel, probably, that they have been prophets as well. From my perspective, I have always been able to look at myself in the mirror each morning, in the campaign and now.
 Q. How likely is an income tax to pass the legislature this year?
 A. I think the chances are improving daily, because it is becoming more and more obvious to those looking at the numbers that no other alternative fits, which was the Governor's conclusion.
 Q. How much does it help, in terms of selling that plan, to have alternative tax packages out there that call for things like taxing utilities?
 A. I think it's helpful in letting people know there aren't very many pleasant alternatives this year, and that other options, other than the Governor's, are worse. I was talking yesterday with another legislator who was telling me about a couple of other packages that might come out. One package would tax financial services, commercial leases, increase the tax on capital gains to 10 percent and increase the tax on dividends and interest to 16 percent. That hardly seems to me a way to stimulate the economy.
Another legislator is working on a package that would expand the base of the corporate tax, by eliminating the dividends exclusion, end the double weighting of the sales tax. All of those things were designed to provide some benefit to business here. That hardly strikes me as a way to make Connecticut attractive and competitive.
 Q. Businesses have been complaining for years that if any more is put on the corporate tax structure, they will begin moving out of state. Is that going to happen?
 A. The short answer is yes. The long answer is, We seem to have already demonstrated that by what we did in 1989, when we expanded the sales tax base, which turned out not to be very productive in terms of revenue. Obviously, there are some other factors -- we're in a recession, or at least a downturn in the economy, and that caused a diminution in tax receipts, so it's hard to sort out all the factors.
 Q. How are you going to sell the income tax to a state where a majority of people still don't believe it's necessary?
 A. The Governor and the Lieutenant Governor are going around the state speaking to people about the dimensions of the problem. Because people are so concerned about their taxes is one reason the Governor did so much cutting on the expenditure side first. We essentially went through the expenditure side for eight weeks before we ever looked at revenues. We went over the prioritization of programs within each agency, looking at 7, 13 and 20 percent cuts and going over each option with the commissioners. There was a really exhaustive consideration of those options.
And the result has been a lot of very upset people because of the cutbacks in expenditures. The Governor has said those are not nice cuts, but the Governor sensed, we all sensed, we had to look at expenditures first. Also, this program provides for a number of tax reductions.
For low- and moderate-income people, the sales-tax rate reduction is a significant benefit; it reverses the pattern of state taxation, which in this state has hit the lower income group hardest, and the highest income groups least.
 Q. Is the three-year plan to finance the deficit too short a period, as some people have alleged?
 A. The Governor is strongly against long-term bonding to deal with this problem, and the rating agencies have also made that clear, that if you don't give any indication of dealing with the problem now, it is going to affect the rating on Connecticut bonds, which has a cost to the taxpayer, too, in terms of interest rates. So you lose money that can otherwise be diverted to programs.
And a lot of people wanted us to deal with it in a shorter period of time. But if you try to deal with it this year, it takes a $750 million bite out of the economy this year when we're struggling to recover from a downturn, which also doesn't make a lot of economic sense. So doing it over three years is a moderate response.
 Q. There has been more of an attempt to rein in the capital budget this year than in a long time, but are there things being put off that perhaps should not be put off?
 A. I think we have to be more careful about what we do bond for. Last year, the legislature -- me -- produced a $1.7 billion bond package. The Thomas Commission pointed out that if we continued to do that, in about eight years we would be beyond any prudent level of debt service, and the things we would have to bond for, we couldn't.
The Governor is recommending about $500 million, and obviously you can't do that without postponing things some people feel are important.
But you have to make some choice, and the Governor did.

GRAPHIC: Photo: William J. Cibes Jr., the state's budget chief, is a 10-year Democratic veteran of the legislature who was co-chairman of the Finance, Revenue and Bonding Committee. (Steve Miller for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Two Counties Gird to Trim Services

BYLINE: By LINDA SASLOW

 BODY:
BOTH Nassau and Suffolk Counties, reeling from a sluggish economy that has provided less-than-expected tax revenue, are expecting severe cuts in social-service programs under the new state budget.
Even if the state does not meet its April 1 deadline tomorrow, the budget, whenever it is passed, is likely to force the counties to make up differences from the shortfall. Social services are one of the largest areas in the budget, and they are expected to be among the hardest-hit categories. 
 The mood in Albany is one of deep concern, said Mitchell H. Pally, director of legislative and economic affairs for the Long Island Association. Mr. Pally, the business group's chief lobbyist and liaison aide in Albany, noted last week that no decisions had been made on reducing the state budget and expenses.
 "Most people realize that difficult decisions have to be made," he said, "and they don't yet know how to make them. For everyone up there, there is a different opinion, and the interpretation of figures depends on the particular interest group looking at them. The state will have to cut back on expenses, and how this will be spread out is hard to say."
Estimates on the timing for passing the budget are as uncertain as how the cuts will be made, Mr. Pally said. The passage will be between this week and July, but probably between May 1 and May 15, he said.
Facing a gloomy forecast, county officials expect severe financial shortages. Whereas those in Nassau express more of a wait-and-see attitude, the outlook in Suffolk seems more pessimistic.
The cuts will be the same in both counties, but the difference is in how they will be perceived, Suffolk Social Services Commissioner Ruth Brandwein said.
According to Ms. Brandwein, the three main areas of concern are abolishing state financing for Title 20, which provides most of the money for local social services; a cap on long-term medical care and a "lag payment" for reimbursement for the long-term disabled.
Title 20 programs that would be affected by the loss in financing include day care for low-income families, shelters for battered women, foster care, homemaker services, services to prevent domestic violence, child and elderly abuse and counseling.
Services for women and children would be most severely affected, Ms. Brandwein said.
 
Expenses to Rise by $3 Million
 The cap on long-term health care would not affect services, but would greatly affect Suffolk, she said, adding that the county's expenses would increase about $3 million over one year.
The combined factors of hospitals' releasing patients prematurely and a growing elderly population have contributed to an increase in home health care.
Home health-care costs increased 20 percent last year, but now the state wants to control the costs, she said, adding:
"If increases for home health care this year exceed 4.5 percent, the county will have to pay the difference. But Suffolk County doesn't have the money to make up that difference.
" This will mean that there will be pressure to reduce the number of hours of home care, and it will have to be administered more stringently."
In terms of "lag money" for the long-term disabled, there will be a delay in the state reimbursement to the counties, Ms. Brandwein said. The last payment for 1991 will not be made until next year. For Suffolk, that means it will lose $2.3 million until 1992, with each payment subsequently delayed. And that, Ms. Brandwein said, is money that the county will never be able to make up.
"Many people ask why it is happening," she said. "For social services, Federal money for programs has been reduced by more than 70 percent over the past decade.
"At this point, nobody knows what's going to happen, but I'm not optimistic because there's no money at any level of government. It will mean the county's having to cut other services or raise taxes."
The Nassau Social Services Commissioner, Joseph A. D'Elia, said that Nassau had more of a wait-and-see attitude and that the Gulotta administration was collecting contingency plans.
"There are an awful lot of trial balloons going up in Albany, and we don't know yet which groups will be most affected," Mr. D'Elia said. "The impact on the county falls into the unknown."
Deputy Budget Director Pam Grella said it appeared that Nassaumight be looking at a loss of $6 million to $10 million that was not figured in its 1991 budget.
"We could be talking about an additional cost of $4.4 million for Aid to Dependent Children, and up to $5 million in Medicaid," she said. "We don't know where we're going to end up, because things keep changing. The mood in the county is not pessimistic, but realistic. We've been looking at countywide ways to cut back."
 
Thousands of State Mandates
 A major concern is the state mandates, which number in the thousands and whose reimbursements are dictated by the state, Ms. Grella said. The mandates started as partnership agreements, she said, but the state is cutting its reimbursements and leaving the counties to pick up the differences.
"In the Handicapped Children's Program, for example, the state was paying 50 percent and had agreed to increase their share by 5 percent a year," Ms. Grella said. "For 1991, the budgeted percentage from the state was 65 percent, and now they've dropped back to 50. I don't know where they think we're going to get the money."
The only hope for counties is the Legislature, Ms. Grella said. A committee made up of members of the State Association of Counties and representatives from the Governor's Budget Office was recently formed to look at the mandates and try to eliminate some restrictions.
"But a state mandate is a state mandate," Ms. Grella said. "And realistically, we don't expect to see any alleviation in the near future."
Another factor hurting the counties is the slow reimbursement, she said. It often takes nine months to be reimbursed after claims are made, she declared.
"It's a big problem," Ms. Grella said. "And the counties have nobody else to pass their shortfalls on to. The current economy does not help, with more people unemployed and candidates for public assistance. Unfortunately, we're in a very difficult time."
 
Proposed Cuts Called Devastating
 For all local governments, the proposed social-service reductions will be devastating, said the Deputy Suffolk County Executive for Finance, Robert Kurtter.
The cap on long-term medical care mostly affects home care and nursing-home care, he said.
In an effort to help local governments in the late 70's, the state began a phase-in program to pay increasing Medicaid costs. To obtain 50 percent of the financing from the Federal Government, the state and county have to combine to match the other half. The state's plan was to increase its percentage from 25 to 40, leaving the counties to assume 10 percent.
"But the state has now said that long-term care is growing too fast, and it can't pay," Mr. Kurtter said. "They have repealed this phase-in takeover, saying they will only pay up to 4.5 percent growth, and that whatever falls above that is the county's share.
"We don't have the money, and we have no control. These are state-mandated programs, and they are strangling the counties."
In December, the state also rescinded a decision made in 1989 to raise from 50 to 75 percent its share of the costs of the mandated Preschool Handicapped Children's Program.
"In one fell swoop, Suffolk County lost $11 million in that repeal action," Mr. Kurtter said. "We're supposed to be partners with the state, but they are setting mandates, controlling programs and eligibility and then handing us the tab."
The worst-hit sectors are in social services, mental health and probation, Mr. Kurtter said. Some program cuts, he said, are counterproductive, adding that the state, in its attempt to balance a budget, had devastated local governments.
